i 
Approval 
This thesis was prepared over a five-month period at the Department of Civil and 
Mechanical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). It was 
submitted as the final assignment for the degree of Master of Science in Architectural 
Engineering (M.Sc.).

==PAGE:4==
 
 
ii 
Abstract 
Despite the emphasis of the Danish building regulations to incorporate people with 
various disabilities into the design of a building, their minimum requirements for access 
and fire safety in residential buildings remain insufficient. The current architectural 
design methodology lacks comprehensive universal design considerations, which 
impede both egress from the building and the ability to recognize the alert in fire 
emergencies. In addition, the current building regulations, standards, and certification 
systems lack a holistic approach to the integration of universal design considerations 
regarding fire safety. The necessity of universal design evolved over the last decades, 
introducing principles, goals, and guidance for the building industry to implement. 
Furthermore, the building industry only implements the minimum requirements which 
leads to buildings that exclude people with disabilities from using and living in them. 
 
As part of this thesis, a gap analysis was conducted to analyze the current building 
regulations and certification systems DGNB, LEED, and BREEAM with respect to 
access and fire safety in residential buildings. Further, empirical research and expert 
interviews were conducted to identify current implementations, challenges, and 
limitations in the industry as well as to understand the needs of people affected by 
disabilities. The research yielded the development of a taxonomy, solution spaces, and 
a pattern language that indicate how to make a residential building more universal in 
terms of fire safety. Finally, a taxonomy tool and a pattern language tool were 
developed. The objective of these tools is to facilitate both comprehension and 
utilization. While the taxonomy tool focuses on the general global approach, the pattern 
language tool assesses the performance of specific projects based on evaluation 
criteria.  
 
The pattern language and solution spaces can facilitate the implementation of the 
design methodology. Furthermore, incorporating the taxonomy into the design process 
can serve as a baseline for challenging and adjusting building regulations and their 
requirements.

==PAGE:12==
Introduction 
 
1 
1. Introduction 
The Danish building regulation defines that a residential building “must be sufficiently 
safe for persons in case of fire” (§82) and that alerting people in the building must be 
done “in a way which enables quick commencement of evacuation” (§88). Further, the 
design, planning, and execution must consider the “persons' knowledge of the escape 
routes in the building”, that escape routes are “easy to identify, reach, and use”, and 
“the possibility and ability of persons to use the escape routes in the building” (§94). 
(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023) 
 
Although these paragraphs seem to include a variety of people with different 
disabilities, requirements in the Danish building regulations do not meet the needs of 
all of them. Further, the industry is mainly not implementing more than required. 
Architects are planning with stereotypes of people (Bjerke, 2023), resulting in 
inaccessible buildings that exclude people with disabilities from using and living in 
them. The lack of understanding of the specific use “can result in a denial of the need 
for accessible buildings, as people with disabilities do not use the buildings”. 
(Grangaard, 2018) 
 
As a result, many people in our society encounter barriers within their buildings. It is 
therefore important that those, regardless of having a disability or not, are afforded the 
same opportunities to live in and utilize an apartment. Disabilities may occur in various 
ways, affecting diverse areas of the human body, emerging at a young age, or 
developing over time. Moreover, people may be affected by disabilities for an extended 
period or a brief duration. There is a possibility that a person with hearing impairment, 
visual impairment, a wheelchair user, or a person with autism resides in the same 
building as people without any disability. All of them should be provided by installations 
facilitating their use. Regarding fire safety, features must be implemented that allow 
egress without external help and the recognition of alerting systems.  
 
To further investigate the previously introduced aspects, two hypotheses were 
formulated and examined: 
 
==PAGE:13==
Introduction 
 
2 
1) The current architectural design methodology lacks comprehensive universal 
design considerations, which impede both egress from the building and the 
ability to recognize the alert in fire emergencies. 
 
2) The current building regulations, standards, and certification systems lack a 
holistic approach to the integration of universal design considerations regarding 
fire safety. 
 
To examine and validate the two hypotheses various methodologies were used in this 
research which yielded the development of a taxonomy, solution spaces, and a pattern 
language on how to make a residential building more universal concerning fire safety. 
The three outcomes are created to provide a comprehensive design classification 
system, design options, and design rules, respectively.  
 
The focus is on multifamily houses in Denmark. The goal is to create an overall 
approach that includes the majority of potential residents and visitors. Every person 
should have equal possibilities to be alerted and to escape in case of fire.  
Universal design supports the inclusion of all kinds of people and ensures that design 
solutions are implemented that apply to most of them. The approach of universal 
design is “grounded in the belief that the broad range of human ability is ordinary, not 
special” (Steinfeld & Maisel, 2012). 
 
To ensure the reasonability and feasibility of the outcome, industry stakeholders were 
involved in the process of identifying the obstacles and limitations of the status quo 
and consulted for the validation of the created patterns and solutions. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:14==
Background 
 
3 
2. Background 
In the last decades, the population in the world changed. On the social level, people 
become more aware of gaps in society, people being excluded from living a self-
organized and independent life, and that inclusion needs to be supported strongly 
(Maisel et al., 2017).  
 
On the physical level, western societies are constantly aging. Current statistics show 
that the life expectancy for women living in Denmark is 83,4 years and for men 79,6 
years (Statistics Denmark, 2024). But not just constant aging needs to be considered 
in the physical change of the society. In 2022, 36,1% of the people living in Denmark 
reported to have a disability (eurostat, 2023). Disabilities can evolve from a young age 
or develop over time (Jamoom et al., 2008). People living with disabilities face many 
challenges in their daily lives. No matter if using public transport, getting groceries, 
going to work, or living in their apartment. The built environment does not offer many 
solutions to facilitate these challenges (Fatima et al., 2024).  
 
Still, the building industry lacks awareness of these changes. The minimum 
requirements in the Danish building regulations and certification systems, such as 
DGNB, LEED, and BREEAM do not incorporate the needs of people with different 
diabilities. In the certification criteria, environmental aspects of sustainability 
predominate (Ferreira et al., 2023). The social aspects are included in some criteria in 
a minor extent, for instance accessibility to and in the buildings in DGNB and BREEAM. 
However, the industry stakeholders primarily implement minimum requirements given 
in the building regulations and certification systems without further improvements 
outside the frameworks (see 5.3). 
 
Implementing a universal building design can resolve these inconveniences and ease 
not only people’s lives with disabilities but also those without them.  
 
2.1 Universal Design 
In the second half of the 20th century, a change in the design of buildings and the built 
environment started to evolve. In the 1960s the “concept of designing for disability” 
evolved in the United States (USA) and Europe. In 1970 the barrier-free design was 
==PAGE:15==
Background 
 
4 
introduced in Japan. Several obstacles were meant to be eliminated for wheelchair 
users. The reason for wheelchair users being at the center of this movement was 
caused by the strong visibility of the disability. The barrier-free design was followed by 
Denmark and Sweden introducing the concept of normalization, which included 
mentally impaired people and later people with physical disabilities. The process was 
further enforced by protests from groups of people with disabilities. The aim was to 
“live normal lives” and buildings being designed to be accessible from the beginning. 
(Kose, 1998) In the 1980s the concept of barrier-free and accessible evolved into 
universal design influenced by Ron Mace. He claimed that “design that works for those 
who are disabled also works better for the entire population” (Maisel et al., 2017).  In 
1997, a working group of architects, product designers, engineers, and environmental 
design researchers which was led by Ron Mace, developed the principles of universal 
design to provide guidance for a broad range of design disciplines including 
environments (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997). Part of the group was Edward Steinfeld, 
who in 1984 founded the IDEA Center at the University at Buffalo. He was convinced 
that inclusive design represents a crucial element in the creation of socially sustainable 
communities and dedicated his professional life to establishing a leading center for 
research and practice in this field. (UB - University at Buffalo, n.d.) Together with 
Jordana Maisel, they developed the goals of universal design and established the 
following definition: 
 
“Universal Design is a design process that enables and empowers a diverse 
population by improving human performance, health and wellness, and social 
participation” (Steinfeld & Maisel, 2012) 
 
The building design process typically overlooks people with disabilities. As a result, 
different barriers for people will occur which hinder the use or access of specific areas 
and buildings. According to Steinfeld & Maisel, universal design addresses these 
barriers, reduces them, and creates benefits for all users.  
 
The objective is to design and compose environments that can be accessed, 
understood, and utilized by the majority of people, independent of their age, size, 
ability, or disability. The design is intended to be accessible to all people who wish to 
==PAGE:16==
Background 
 
5 
utilize it (CEUD, n.d.). If the design is universal and therefore inclusive, it can also be 
economically and socially beneficial (Maisel et al., 2017).  
 
Still, there is one main obstacle hindering the establishment of universal design: 
knowledge. Knowledge is required about user needs and good examples, about their 
limitations, and about the implementation of an ideal process where users’ needs are 
incorporated from the beginning (Department of the Built Environment (AAU), n.d.). 
Kirkeby (2015) concluded among others in her paper Accessible Knowledge – 
Knowledge on Accessibility that architects and practitioners look for knowledge in the 
work that is done and the people they talk to. A few gain knowledge through research-
based articles and reports. This means academic findings can only slowly reach the 
industry and be applied in practice.  
A lack of knowledge can result in overlooking essential universal elements or in 
implementing them at a later stage of the project, which will have financial implications. 
The early implementation of universal design is of importance and can only be done 
efficiently if it is conducted with sufficient knowledge.   
 
2.2 United Nations’ Agenda 2030 
In 2015, the United Nations introduced the Agenda 2030 and the 17 Sustainable 
Development Goals (SDGs) “to secure the rights and well-being of everyone on a 
healthy, thriving planet”(United Nations, n.d.-c). The agenda is a “roadmap for ending 
poverty, protecting the planet, and tackling inequalities”. The 17 SDGs offer ways to 
approach these problems and ensure no one is left behind. (United Nations, n.d.-c) 
Universal Design approaches for residential buildings can be recognized in the SDGs  
10 - Reduce inequality within and among countries and 11 - Make cities and human 
settlements inclusive, safe, resilient, and sustainable. They underline the importance 
of social inclusion, sufficient and safe housing, and all-encompassing urbanization 
(United Nations, n.d.-b).  
 
Leave no one behind  
‘Leave no one behind’ is the central theme of the agenda and goals. All member states 
are committed to “eradicate poverty in all its forms, end discrimination and exclusion, 
and reduce the inequalities and vulnerabilities that leave people behind and undermine 
==PAGE:17==
Background 
 
6 
the potential of individuals and of humanity as a whole” (United Nations, n.d.-a). 
Discriminatory laws, policies, and social practices impede people’s access to services, 
resources, and equal opportunities. It results in groups being marginalized. (United 
Nations, n.d.-a) 
 
17 Sustainable Development Goals 
The 17 SDGs, as shown in Figure 1, are the core of the Agenda 2030 and introduce 
the challenges, targets, and indicators of how to solve today’s problems. They are an 
“urgent call for action by all countries – developed or developing – in a global 
partnership” (United Nations, n.d.-b). Since their implementation in 2015, the progress 
towards the goals has been published yearly. 
 
 
Figure 1 - United Nation's Sustainable Development Goals (United Nations, n.d.-b) 
 
Universal design can address two of the sustainability goals and assist in reaching 
specific targets. The SDGs and their targets relevant to the scope of this thesis are 
presented and defined by the United Nations (n.d.-b) as follows: 
 
SDG 10 – Reduce inequality within and among countries 
Target 10.2: By 2030, empower and promote the social, economic, and political 
inclusion of all, irrespective of age, sex, disability, race, ethnicity, origin, religion, or 
economic or other status.  
 
 
==PAGE:18==
Background 
 
7 
SDG 11 – Make cities and human settlements inclusive, safe, resilient, and sustainable 
Target 11.1: By 2030, ensure access for all to adequate, safe, and affordable housing 
and basic services [and upgrade slums]. 
Target 11.3: By 2030, enhance inclusive and sustainable urbanization and capacity for 
participatory, integrated, and sustainable human settlement planning and management 
in all countries.  
 
2.3 European Union Taxonomy for Sustainable Activities 
The European Union (EU) established 2020 the taxonomy regulations and herewith 
set out the four overarching conditions that economic activities have to meet to qualify 
as environmentally sustainable (European Commission, n.d.-b, n.d.-c) 
 
The four conditions are as follows:  
1) Making a substantial contribution to at least one environmental objective, 
2) Doing no significant harm to any of the other five environmental objectives, 
3) Complying with minimum safeguards, and 
4) Complying with the technical screening criteria set out in the Taxonomy 
delegated acts (European Commission, n.d.-c). 
 
The EU Taxonomy presents a classification system that is intended to help “companies 
and investors identify environmentally sustainable economic activities to make 
sustainable investment decisions” (European Commission, n.d.-c).  
 
The European Commission (n.d.-c) defined six climate and environmental objectives, 
as presented in Figure 2. 
 
==PAGE:19==
Background 
 
8 
 
Figure 2 - The six objectives of the EU Taxonomy for Sustainable Activities (European Commission, n.d.-c) 
 
To ensure the contribution of economic activities to the shown objectives, performance 
criteria were set by the EU in delegated acts (European Commission, n.d.-c).  To 
facilitate the navigation of the EU Taxonomy, the European Commission introduced 
several tools, including the EU Taxonomy Compass. This tool provides a visual 
representation of the sectors, activities, and criteria included in the EU Taxonomy 
delegated acts (European Commission, n.d.-b).  
 
The thesis employs the compass as a template to ensure that the taxonomy for fire 
safety is developed in the same manner that facilitates access for a variety of users. 
 
2.4 Danish fire statistics  
In May 2023, the Danish Emergency Management Agency (Beredskabsstyrelsen) 
published the most recent numbers regarding fatal fires in Denmark in 2022. They 
define a fatal fire as a fire that leads to the death of one or more people. In 2022, 52 
people died in fires, which were not caused by suicide. The majority of fires were 
caused by smoking (76%), followed by uncareful meal preparation (9%), and 
==PAGE:20==
Background 
 
9 
electricity-related issues (7%) (see Figure 3). From 2013-2022, 51% of the fires 
occurred in ordinary housing without care, 31% in ordinary housing with care, and 10% 
in nursing homes. The missing 5% were counted in daycare centers and others (see 
Figure 4). (Beredskabsstyrelsen, 2023)  
 
 
Figure 3 – Reasons for fire in Denmark 2022, Fire statistics in Denmark 2022, Figure adapted from 
Beredskabsstyrelsen (2023) 
 
Figure 4 - Building types where Fire occurred in 2022, Fire statistics in Denmark 2022, Figure adapted from 
Beredskabsstyrelsen (2023)

==PAGE:21==
Background 
 
10 
Investigations revealed that the main risk factors for dying in a fire were mobility 
impairment and substance abuse (Beredskabsstyrelsen, 2023). Another study has 
shown that most fatal residential fires occur during the evening and nighttime. It was 
assumed that this was because of the long detection time of the residents being asleep. 
(Gummesen & Dederichs, 2017) Gummesen & Dederichs also identified that 50% of 
fatal fires broke out in bed- or living rooms and that 50% of the reason was related to 
smoking. Further, like the findings of the Danish Emergency Management Agency, 
more people living in non-care apartment buildings died in the fire than people living in 
single-family houses or care homes.  
 
It becomes evident that residential fires can result in the death of one or more people 
and the potential reasons are a long detection time due to people being asleep or under 
the influences of substances, not able to realize the danger of the situation. 
Additionally, mobility-impaired people face challenges leaving the apartment and are 
therefore more likely to die in the fire. (Beredskabsstyrelsen, 2023; Gummesen & 
Dederichs, 2017)  
 
2.5 Fire Regulations 
The Danish building regulation Bygningsrelementet (BR18) sets the requirements for 
architects and engineers to design a building. It defines and mandates compliance with 
the fire regulations. However, the fire requirements for residential buildings are not as 
high as those for buildings with a more public function, such as offices, hotels, and 
hospitals.  
 
The following paragraphs will introduce the fire regulations relevant to this thesis and 
currently defined in Chapter 5 of the BR18. The information is taken from Social- og 
Boligstyrelsen (2023).  
 
In general, the design of a building must ensure the safety of people in case of fire. 
Moreover, it is essential to guarantee that a safe evacuation in and around the building 
is possible and that the rescue personnel have secure options to search and rescue 
people within the building, as well as to carry out extinguishing works (§82).  
 
==PAGE:22==
Background 
 
11 
Buildings are divided into different categories indicating the existence of sleeping 
facilities, whether people in the building know and are familiar with the escape routes, 
the ability of people to save themselves without the need for outside help, and the 
maximum number of people for whom the fire-related unit is designed (§85). Due to 
the thesis’s focus on residential buildings, regulations applying to building category 4 
are used and analyzed. This means people sleep in the building, and people are 
familiar with the escape routes. It is assumed that people can bring themselves to 
safety without additional help and that there is no limit of people for whom the unit is 
designed (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023).  
 
A further classification for buildings regarding fire is the determination of risk classes. 
In total, there are four risk classes which are each divided into the building categories 
and the building’s design, sections above ground, and the total number of people with 
common escape routes and fire loads (§86). Table 1 displays the risk classification of 
building category 4, relevant to the scope of this thesis (multi-family houses).  
 
 
Risk Class 
Design, sections above ground, and the total number of 
people in a building section with common escape route 
1 
Buildings with maximum one floor above ground and maximum 
one floor below ground, where the floor is no more than 5,0 meters 
below ground level. 
2 
Buildings or building sections where the floor of the top story is no 
higher than 9,6 meters above the ground level. 
3 
Buildings or building sections where the floor of the top story is 
between 9,6 meters and 45,0 meters above the ground. 
4 
Buildings or building sections that are not covered by the risk 
classes 1-3.  
Table 1 - Risk classes for building category 4 (BR18 §86)(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023)  
  
In and around the building, fire safety installations and fire extinguishing equipment 
must be installed. The choice of those must consider the detection of fire in an early 
stage; the quick alarm to the emergency service; the quick alerting of people in the 
building; the safe evacuation of people staying in the building via escape routes; the 
controllability of the fire; the routing of smoke and heat away from the building; the 
==PAGE:23==
Background 
 
12 
assessment of escape routes and wayfinding for people in and around the building; 
and the safe conditions for the rescue personnel (§88).  
 
Buildings must be designed in a manner that people can become aware that a fire 
broke out, and that escape routes and rescue openings are designed to evacuate and 
rescue people, respectively (§91). This further means, that fire needs to be detected 
early, and the alarm needs to notify people in the building and the emergency services 
(§92).  
 
In building category 4, more precisely in residential buildings, a smoke alert system 
must be installed (§93) whereby the alarm only must notify people in the housing unit 
where the fire evolves (Traffik- Bygge- og Boligstyrelsen, 2019).   
 
The escape route in a building is a coherent system of exits, walking areas, and stairs 
that guarantee the safe leaving of residents to the outside or a safe space. To do so, 
the people in the building should be knowledgeable of the escape routes and should 
be able to identify, reach, and use them. Whereby the ability of a person to use the 
escape routes must be considered. The routes and doors should be dimensioned 
according to the required widths and the people using them (§94).  
 
In buildings with a top-story floor height greater than 22 meters, anti-panic lighting in 
escape route stairways (§96) and at least one firefighter elevator must be installed 
(§133). 
 
To provide evacuation for people not able to use the given escape routes, rescue areas 
must be provided which can be accessed by the rescue personnel using ladders 
(§128). 
 
Consultation of a Fire Engineer 
When designing and constructing a building in risk class 2-4, a fire engineer must be 
assigned according to §20 in BR18. The fire engineer is responsible for ensuring 
compliance with Chapter 5 Brand (Fire), and that the documentation is provided, 
adequate, and checked. (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023).  
 
==PAGE:24==
Background 
 
13 
Often, fire engineers do not attend the very early phases of a building. The architects 
provide the first draft with their common knowledge and then consult fire engineers. 
This can result in bigger changes due to potentially not complying with building 
regulations. In those cases, involving the engineers can become a disruptive element 
in the design and function of the building. (KHR Architecture, n.d.)  
 
The early implementation of fire engineers is therefore crucial to ensure a universal 
design regarding fire safety, as this will avoid the need for extra costs and work at a 
later stage in the project.  
 
 
==PAGE:25==
Theory 
 
14 
3. Theory 
This chapter presents the theoretical framework that is relevant to the scope of this 
thesis. The following paragraphs provide an overview of key terminology, different 
disabilities, and certification systems. It also offers a theoretical explanation of the later-
developed taxonomy and pattern language. 
 
3.1 Universal Design 
The design process often overlooks people with disabilities, older adults, or children. 
Universal Design aims to address the barriers to provide benefits for all users and to 
ensure usability for people with different abilities (Steinfeld & Maisel, 2012). To achieve 
this Universal Design is based on seven principles and eight goals. 
 
Seven Principles 
The seven principles were developed by Mace, Connel, et al. in 1997. They are 
intended to be applied for the evaluation “of existing designs, guide the design process, 
and educate both designers and consumers about the characteristics of more usable 
products and environments” (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997). The principles are defined 
as follows (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997): 
 
1 
Equitable Use 
The design is useful and marketable to people 
with diverse abilities.  
2 
Flexibility in Use 
The design accommodates a wide range of 
individual preferences and abilities. 
3 
Simple and Intuitive Use 
The use of the design is easy to understand, 
regardless of the user’s experience, knowledge, 
language skills, or current concentration level. 
4 
Perceptible Information 
The design communicates necessary information 
effectively to the user, regardless of ambient 
conditions or the user’s sensory abilities. 
5 
Tolerance for Error 
The design minimizes hazards and the adverse 
consequences of accidental or unintended 
actions. 
6 
Low Physical Effort 
The design can be used efficiently, comfortably, 
and with a minimum of fatigue.  
==PAGE:26==
Theory 
 
15 
7 
Appropriate Size and 
Space for Approach and 
Use 
Appropriate size and space are allotted for 
approach, reach, and manipulation regardless of 
physical characteristics such as size or mobility.  
 
Eight Goals 
After critics were developed regarding the seven principles, the IDEA Center 
proceeded to extend the framework beyond usability, incorporating social participation 
and health. Context started to play a significant role in the development of realistic 
applications. Consequently, the eight goals of Universal Design were developed to 
assess and implement universal design to all design domains inside the constraints of 
existing resources. (Steinfeld & Maisel, 2012) The goals are defined as follows 
(Steinfeld & Maisel, 2012): 
 
1 
Body fit 
Accommodating a wide range of body sizes and 
abilities. 
2 
Comfort 
Keeping demands within desirable limits of body 
function and perception. 
3 
Awareness 
Ensuring that critical information for use is easily 
perceived. 
4 
Understanding 
Making methods of operation and use intuitive, 
clear, and unambiguous. 
5 
Wellness 
Contributing to health promotion, avoidance of 
disease, and protection from hazards. 
6 
Social integration 
Treating all groups with dignity and respect.  
7 
Personalization 
Incorporating opportunities for choice and the 
expression of individual preferences. 
8 
Cultural appropriateness  
Respecting and reinforcing cultural values, and the 
social and environmental contexts of any design 
project.  
 
3.2 Disabilities 
The built environment is mainly designed for people without any disability or 
impairment. It is designed for those who do not have “long-term physical, mental, 
intellectual, or sensory impairments which in interaction with various barriers may 
hinder their full and effective participation in society on an equal basis with others” 
==PAGE:27==
Theory 
 
16 
(United Nations, 2006). Although accessibility in and to buildings is becoming more 
established, there remains a gap in awareness among clients, architects, and 
developers regarding the full spectrum of disabilities and impairments, beyond 
wheelchair use (Grangaard, 2018). Thus, the next paragraphs introduce further 
disabilities and impairments, which are essential in the context of universally designed 
buildings with regard to fire safety. 
 
 
Blindness & Visual Impairment 
Visual impairment describes a wide range of loss in vision function. The visual function 
can be divided into different abilities. For instance, the ability to resolve detail (visual 
acuity), the ability to focus (accommodation), the field of vision (the area which can be 
seen), color vision, and the adaptability to light (Douglas & McLinden, 2005). 
Therefore, many different types of visual impairment can occur. Commonly, tests 
identifying the visual acuity are taken to identify the degree of visual impairment to 
blindness. People being able to see a letter on the vision chart from 6 meters, rather 
than people seeing the same letter from 12 meters, already have a mild vision 
impairment (visual acuity < 6/12). People having an acuity of worse than 3/60 are 
categorized as blind. (WHO, 2019) However, those considered blind may still have the 
ability to perceive light or color. According to Downey (2023), only 6-8% of blind people 
worldwide have no light perception. Around the world, at least 2.2 billion people have 
a near or distant vision impairment, which is defined as a decreased ability to see. 
Further, it can affect people of all ages, whereby most people with visual impairment 
or blindness are over 50 years old. (Oxford English Dictionary, n.d.; WHO, 2023)  
 
People having a visual impairment already at a young age can experience delays in 
motor, language, emotional, social, and cognitive development. Adults with vision 
impairment can have difficulties in finding employment and are at a higher risk for 
depression and anxiety. The latter can result in social isolation, difficulties in walking, 
a higher risk of accidents, and an increased chance of entering nursing or care homes 
early. (WHO, 2023)   
 
Deafness & Hearing Impairment  
Deafness is “the fact or quality of being deaf, loss or absence of hearing, because of 
a congenital condition or because of aging, injury, or a medical condition later in life” 
==PAGE:28==
Theory 
 
17 
(Oxford English Dictionary, 2023). In an extended use, deafness is the “inability to 
perceive or be affected by certain kinds of sound, musical rhythm, or harmony” (Oxford 
English Dictionary, 2023). If the hearing loss in the better-hearing ear is greater than 
35 decibels (dB), it is referred to as disabling hearing loss. The World Health 
Organization (WHO) observed, that 25% of people older than 60 years are affected by 
disabling hearing loss. Hearing loss can be mild, moderate, severe, or profound and 
can affect both ears and just one. (WHO, 2024) The WHO estimates that by 2050, 
around 2,5 billion people have some degree of hearing loss, and a minimum of 700 
million people need hearing rehabilitation. Currently, over 1 billion young adults are at 
risk of permanent, avoidable hearing loss because of unsafe listening practices. (World 
Health Organization [], 2024) Hearing impairment is any degree of hearing loss, from 
mild to profound. The term includes deafness or hard-of-hearing abilities. (DO-IT, 2022)  
 
Being affected by a hearing impairment or complete loss can result in having limitations 
in communication and speech, cognition, social isolation, education, and employment 
(WHO, 2024). 
 
Mobility Impairment 
Patla and Shumway-Cook defined a mobility impairment as occurring “when 
impairments in mobility restrict the ability of individuals to move about in their natural 
environment in order to carry out activities essential to daily life” (Patla & Shumway-
Cook, 1999; Rosenberg et al., 2011). In short, people have severe difficulty in walking 
(Clarke et al., 2007). In addition to the inability to walk or move any other part of the 
body, poor motor control may also be present. Proprioception, or the sense of body 
awareness, enables us to understand the relationship between body parts. 
Furthermore, it regulates the force required for several activities. (Rymanowicz, 2014) 
 
Mobility impairments can impact people differently. For instance, they may take longer 
to walk a certain route, to enter a building, or to maneuver in small spaces. 
Furthermore, the presence of physical barriers can impede access to a building or 
room. (DO-IT, 2022) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:29==
Theory 
 
18 
Autism 
Autism is a neurological disorder that is characterized by severe and profound deficits 
in the areas of social skills and communication. Further, affected people often show 
restricted patterns of behavior, attention deficits, and mental retardation. Autism can 
be identified by self-injurious and compulsive ritualistic behavior, aggressiveness, and 
hyperactivity. (Good, 2011; Schreibman & Koegel, 2005) 
 
There are three types of autism defined as follows (Good, 2011): 
1 
Autistic disorder 
This type can be identified by the presence of 
language delays, social and communication 
challenges, unusual behaviors and interests, as 
well as intellectual disabilities. 
2 
Asperger syndrome 
People affected by this condition may show milder 
symptoms than those previously described, 
including social challenges, unusual behaviors, 
and interests. In general, people with Asperger 
syndrome do not have language or intellectual 
disabilities. 
3 
Pervasive Development 
Disorder-Not Otherwise 
Specified (PDD-NOS) 
(Atypical Autism) 
In contrast to the first two types, people with this 
condition show fewer and less severe symptoms, 
yet still meet some of the criteria for the previously 
mentioned conditions. It may be that they only face 
social and communication challenges.  
 
Worldwide, 1 in 100 children (1%) is diagnosed with autism (Zeidan et al., 2022), in 
Denmark it is 21,8 in 10.000 children (0,218%) (Parner et al., 2011). 
 
ADHD 
Attention-Deficit/Hyperactivity Disorder (ADHD) is a neurodevelopmental disorder 
characterized by impulsivity, hyperactivity, and attention. Impairments often associated 
with ADHD are the inability to be focused or increased distractibility. It usually affects 
all aspects of the people’s life. People with ADHD may have greater difficulty with 
emotional dysregulation, frustration tolerance, and motivation. (Neprily et al., 2023)  
==PAGE:30==
Theory 
 
19 
Approximately 2,58 - 6,76% of adults worldwide are diagnosed with ADHD (Song et 
al., 2021). 
 
A common characteristic of people with ADHD is hypersensitivity. This can include 
being very sensitive to physical stimuli, such as sound, sight, touch, or smell, or 
emotional stimuli. People diagnosed with ADHD tend to be easily overwhelmed by too 
much information. (Neurohealth Associates, n.d.) 
 
3.3 Standards, Certification Systems, and Guidelines 
Building projects always follow country-specific standards and guidelines. These 
ensure a common understanding and application of what elements need to be 
considered. The objective of standards is to define the fundamental minimum 
requirements to be met. To identify further opportunities, the industry implements 
certification systems indicating extended solutions that can be followed. However, 
standards of the building regulations are mandatory while certification systems are 
optional and require a certain fee. The following paragraphs describe the building 
regulation standard of Denmark BR18 as well as the three most common building 
certification systems DGNB, LEED, and BREEAM. Lastly, the specifically for universal 
design developed certification system isUD is introduced. 
 
BR18 
The Bygningsreglementet 18 (BR18) is the Danish building regulation that defines the 
requirements that must be met in all building projects in Denmark. They are designed 
to guarantee that the construction and design of a building ensure fire protection, safety 
and security, and health of the occupants. BR18 sets the minimum requirements that 
can be improved by the industry without worsening other mandatory criteria. The 
current version of BR18 (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023) from 2023 was taken as a 
baseline for this thesis.  
 
The building regulations are divided into 22 chapters, whereby chapters 2 - Access 
Conditions, §48 to §62, (Danish: Adgnagsforhold) and 5 - Fire, §82 to §158, (Danish: 
Brand) were analyzed specifically in section 5.1. 
 
==PAGE:31==
Theory 
 
20 
DGNB 
The Deutsche Gesellschaft für Nachhaltiges Bauen (DGNB) was established in 2007 
as a nonprofit organization in Germany and is currently the largest network for 
sustainable buildings in Europe and the second-largest network for sustainable 
buildings in the world. It advocates for verifiable good buildings, lifeworthy districts, and 
an overall sustainable environment. DGNB aims “to actively shape the transformation 
of the construction and real estate industry, to promote understanding of the need for 
sustainable buildings, and to anchor it in the general public's consciousness”. (DGNB, 
n.d.-b) The criteria and requirements of the Danish version for new buildings and 
comprehensive renovation (Nye bygninger og omfattende renoveringer) of 2023 are 
analyzed in section 5.1 (Green Building Council Denmark, 2023). For new buildings 
and comprehensive renovation, the DGNB system is divided into six qualities with 
different weights in the evaluation, and 37 criteria. Environmental, economic, and 
social qualities are evaluated equally. The DGNB system further includes the 
consideration of the SDGs and the EU taxonomy. The qualities, their weights, and the 
number of criteria are distributed according to Table 2. 
 
Quality

weight, Table adapted from Green Building Council Denmark (2023) 
 
The DGNB system can be applied worldwide. It is adaptable to the climatic, structural, 
legal, and cultural characteristics of other countries. Denmark, Spain, Austria, 
Switzerland, and Croatia have developed country-specific criteria catalogs and certify 
buildings by local DGNB System Partners. (DGNB, n.d.-a) 
 
 
 
==PAGE:32==
Theory

LEED 
Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design (LEED), introduced by the U.S. 
Green Building Council in 1998 is the world’s most widely used green building rating 
system. LEED provides criteria to create “healthy, highly efficient, and cost-saving 
green buildings” (U.S. Green Building Council, n.d.). The criteria and requirements of 
version v4.1 for building design and construction (BD+C) for new buildings of July 2023 
are analyzed in section 5.1 (U.S. Green Building Council, 2023). The LEED BD+C for 
new buildings is divided into nine categories with different points to score, as presented 
in Table 3.

LEED can be applied to projects worldwide and there are no country-specific changes 
in the system. According to the U.S. Green Building Council (n.d.), LEED is used in 
186 countries and territories.  
 
BREEAM 
BREEAM is a third-party certification system for the build environment. It was 
introduced by the BRE group in 1990. The system is designed to ensure the fulfillment 
of environmental, social, governance, health, and net zero goals. (BRE Group, n.d.) 
BREEAM is the leading sustainability assessment method in the world and its 
framework covers the whole life performance of an asset, from new construction to 
==PAGE:33==
Theory 
 
22 
refurbishment (BREEAM, n.d.). The criteria and requirements of the BREEAM 
international new construction version 6.0 from 2021 as well as the appendant lifetime 
home criteria are analyzed in section 5.1 (BREEAM, 2021; Department of Housing & 
Development, n.d.). The BREEAM catalog is divided into ten environmental sections 
with different weighting. Additionally, 10% for innovation can be reached. The focus of 
the analysis is on the project type Multiple residential dwellings which is divided into 
the two categories partially fitted and fully fitted. While partially fitted apartments still 
require new residents to fit out their homes, fully fitted apartments are handed over 
already including, for instance, floors and kitchens. BREEAM (2021) Apartments in 
Denmark are considered fully fitted following BREEAM’s definition. Table 4 illustrates 
their sections and weights.

BREEAM can be applied to projects worldwide and there are no country-specific 
changes in the system. According to Newman (2024), over two million assets are 
registered worldwide.  
 
isUD 
Innovative Solutions for Universal Design (isUD) is a certification program developed 
and maintained by the Center of Inclusive Design and Environmental Access (IDEA 
==PAGE:34==
Theory 
 
23 
Center) at the University at Buffalo’s School of Architecture and Planning. With the 
provided solutions, they want to ensure inclusivity and for the public to recognize the 
value of Universal Design to make the world healthier, friendlier, and safer. (IDEA 
Center, n.d.-a) The proposed solutions can be accessed via the website also outside 
of the certification process (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). The currently published solutions 
were analyzed as part of this thesis in section 5.1. Currently, the certification is 
exclusively conducted in the USA.  
 
3.4 Taxonomy 
A taxonomy is a uniform and comprehensive classification system (Silva et al., 2022) 
that can characterize objects or actions according to specific criteria. These objects 
and actions can range from organisms in the biological context to economic activities 
at a corporate level.  
 
The taxonomy developed in this thesis classifies buildings according to criteria that 
determine how universally designed the building is (further referred to as taxonomy). 
It further shares a common definition of activities that can be considered sustainable 
(European Commission, n.d.-b) for all potential users. Characteristics for the building 
classification are defined as attributes. These express overall universal design criteria 
that must be considered in the building process regarding fire safety in residential 
buildings. Attributes must be sufficiently comprehensive, yet flexible and able to 
account for the particularities of the built environment at any location in the world (Silva 
et al., 2022). The attributes are described with varying degrees of detail. The initial 
level indicates the elements to be implemented, while the second level specifies the 
location and methodology for the implementation.  
 
In the context of this thesis, the EU Taxonomy Compass was used as a template to 
create a taxonomy for universal design concerning fire safety. The taxonomy follows 
the “Do no significant harm”-principle that certain activities should be implemented 
without causing harm to others (European Commission, n.d.-c). In comparison to the 
EU Taxonomy Compass, the attributes can be equalized with the sector and the first 
level with the activity to fulfill (European Commission, n.d.-a).  
 
==PAGE:35==
Theory 
 
24 
3.5 Pattern Language 
In 1977, Christopher Alexander developed the pattern language for building and 
planning. The book describes problems and solutions for different elements in the built 
environment. These elements are called patterns and give detailed information on how 
to shape towns, neighborhoods, houses, gardens, and rooms. The book is designed 
to present one potential language and should be evaluated critically. Numerous 
additional solutions could be explored. The patterns are always written in a uniform 
format (Alexander et al., 1977): 
 
§ Picture 
§ Introductory paragraph 
§ Headline with the essence of the problem 
§ Body of the problem 
§ Solution 
§ Diagram (illustrating the solution) 
§ Paragraph tying pattern to all smaller patterns which are needed to complete  
 
Figure 5 illustrates an example by Alexander et al. (1977) from the original pattern 
language. The selected page presents the later paragraphs of the body of the problem 
and the solution. While the abstract to the problem is written comprehensively, the 
solution is presented in a concise format. The objective is to illustrate the significance 
of the problem and the necessity for a solution, while also indicating that the solution 
can be achieved through a different approach as proposed. This can be accomplished 
according to individual preferences or local conditions (Alexander et al., 1977).  
 
In the context of this thesis, the pattern language by Christopher Alexander was used 
as a template to create a language for universal design concerning fire safety. The 
pattern language provides designers with techniques and strategies to develop more 
accessible and usable buildings (Valtolina & Sisto, 2022).  
==PAGE:36==
Theory 
 
25 
 
Figure 5 - Example of a pattern in the original pattern language by Alexander et al. (1977). The cut-out indicates 
final part of the body of the problem, the solution, and the diagram illustrating the solution for pattern “132 Short 
Passages” 
 
 
==PAGE:37==
Methodology 
 
26 
4. Methodology 
Throughout this thesis, a variety of methodologies were utilized to firstly identify current 
limitations and obstacles in implementing universal design, secondly to develop 
approaches and tools to make buildings more universal in terms of fire safety, and 
thirdly, to validate the findings and outcomes of this thesis. The following paragraphs 
introduce the different methodologies.  
 
4.1 Literature Review 
Literature was reviewed comprehensively to understand the initial meaning and the 
problem of implementing universal design. The goal was to identify current gaps, 
limitations, and theoretical frameworks. Moreover, it provided insights into existing 
solutions or theoretically proposed solutions (further referred to as solutions).  
 
The literature review findings were utilized in all the following methodologies and 
analyses. It offers a comprehensive overview of all relevant topics related to universal 
design, thereby facilitating the identification of potential solutions and the critical 
evaluation of those. 
 
4.2 Gap Analysis 
A large number of standards, regulations, and certification systems exist within the built 
environment. However, social considerations are often overlooked when these are 
created and applied. A gap analysis was chosen to detect and evaluate the lack of 
social considerations in today’s building process. While accessibility is already 
considered in some respects, further solutions benefiting people with other and more 
diverse disabilities are regularly ignored. 
 
To identify the gaps regarding universal design, the Danish building regulation BR18 
concerning access (chapter 2 of BR18) and fire safety (chapter 5 of BR18), as well as 
the certification systems DGNB, LEED, and BREEAM were analyzed.  
It was focused on the determination of gaps in common access areas as well as safety 
and supporting installations in the apartment and building. 
==PAGE:38==
Methodology 
 
27 
For DGNB, the criterion Universal Design which belongs to the social qualities was 
evaluated (see section 3.3). This criterion included access conditions to, at, and in the 
building, stairs and their fit-outs, elevators, and the furnishing of homes.  
 
The LEED certification focuses on energy, materials, waste, indoor air, and thermal 
comfort. For the gap analysis, the pilot criterion Inclusive Design was evaluated. This 
pilot is currently in a test phase and will be included in the next version v5 (USGBC, 
2024). The criterion includes physical access in and outside of the building, wayfinding, 
assistive technology, emotional health, and inclusive spaces.  
 
For BREEAM, the criterion Accessibility which belongs to the section on health- and 
well-being was evaluated (see section 3.3). For residential buildings, the criterion 
follows the guidelines of the Lifetime Home criteria. These include the approach to the 
building, entrances, stairs and lifts, internal doorways and hallways, circulation spaces, 
entrance level bed space and bathroom, bedroom and bathroom fit-out, handle 
heights, and location of service control.  
 
The objective of the gap analysis is to compare the previously mentioned criteria, which 
are considered to be actual performance, with the IDEA Center's isUD solutions, which 
define the target values and desired performance, respectively.  In the case where no 
isUD solution was identified, the findings of the literature review and observations were 
employed.  
 
4.3 Empirical Research  
In addition to the literature review, data was gathered through empirical studies. This 
approach provides insights through direct and indirect observations and experiences 
(Center on Knowledge Translation for Technology Transfer, n.d.). The different 
research angles are described in the following. 
  
House of Disabled People’s Organizations 
The Disabled People’s Organizations Denmark (DOPD), located in Taastrup Denmark, 
developed their office building together with several stakeholders and foundations. The 
house is claimed as the “most accessible house in the world” (Danske 
==PAGE:39==
Methodology 
 
28 
Handicaporganisationer, n.d.). Although the focus of this study is on residential 
buildings, a visit to the office building was conducted. The goal was to verify already 
made ideas by seeing implemented solutions and get further inspiration on what could 
be adapted for a residential building.  
 
The visit was conducted in combination with an interview with Christian Bundgaard, a 
political consultant at Dansk Blindesamfund. This enabled, in addition to the atrium and 
basement, access to office spaces that are not publicly accessible. Furthermore, 
building details could be shared.  
 
User Questionnaire 
It is crucial to involve the people who are affected by the design decisions. Therefore, 
a short questionnaire was developed to identify the current living situations of people 
with visual, hearing, or mobility impairments and to get insights into their obstacles and 
wishes. The questionnaire outline, shown in Figure 6, was used to interview an 
employee of the Dansk Blindesamfund and to get some insights from the employees 
of the Danske Døves Landsforbund and Dansk Handicap Forbund, as well as one 
person with ADHD. All seven interviewees are affected by disabilities. The perspectives 
of a blind person, two people with hearing impairment, three people sitting in a 
wheelchair, and one person with ADHD were collected.

4.4 Expert Interviews 
Universal design is often an intangible term for most stakeholders within the real estate 
and construction industry. Interviews with different industry experts (experts) were 
conducted to investigate the knowledge and implementation of universal design 
solutions and considerations. Further, the process and responsibilities of each 
stakeholder were aimed to identify.  
 
Two interview series were conducted with each participating party. The first series 
explored the status quo about the experts’ knowledge and implementation of universal 
design methods within the building’s design process. The second interview series was 
conducted to discuss the results, specifically the pattern language and solution spaces, 
to verify their applicability among industry stakeholders. 
 
Interview Series 1 – Status Quo 
To ensure a comprehensive and similar outcome of the interviews, an interview guide 
was developed. The guide, shown in Figure 7, covered the universal design topics in 
general, the design process, costs, fire, and the shape of the floor plan. The goal was 
to identify gaps, current limitations, and already implemented or planned solutions.  
 
To avoid irrelevant questions because of previously given answers, but simultaneously 
ensure that they are available for the other interview partners, the guide was structured 
with directional arrows giving indications to the next question to ask. This structure 
facilitated the flow of the interview and a fast orientation for the interviewer.  
 
All three interviews were conducted in person in the office spaces of the expert’s 
companies. The interviews’ duration varied from 21 to 52 minutes. The interviews were 
recorded to prevent the loss of any relevant information.

4.5 Case Study 
A project, which is currently under construction in Denmark, was provided by an 
industry stakeholder. The plans were used to apply the findings of the pattern language 
to a real-world example. The goal was to identify already implemented universal design 
principles and to create awareness of missing considerations.  
 
The individual patterns and the pattern language tool were taken as a baseline for the 
evaluation. A corresponding floor plan or section was identified for each pattern and 
analyzed according to evaluation criteria that were developed beforehand. The 
evaluation criteria included the optimum requirement which indicates the universality 
of each pattern and its criteria. These are based on the Danish building regulations 
BR18, and the presented certification systems DGNB, LEED, BREEAM, and isUD.  
 
The objective of the application was to evaluate the current status of the project. This 
was done by indicating its performance on scales highlighting criteria that is outside of 
building regulations and criteria that achieved universal design. Simultaneously, these 
scales highlight the room for improvement if a criterion is not yet in the range for 
universal design.  
 
The information concerning the architect, the owner, and the project details are to be 
kept anonymous. 
 
4.6 Validation Survey 
An online survey was sent out via Microsoft Forms to 11 industry stakeholders in the 
building industry: six architects, one project developer, zero engineers, and four other 
relevant stakeholders (i.e. consultants). The survey introduced all solution spaces (see 
section 5.6) developed during this thesis in a short and precise way. The objective was 
to identify whether those involved in the design process would consider implementing 
==PAGE:46==
Methodology 
 
35 
the proposed solution spaces. It was clarified at the beginning of the survey, that cost 
considerations as well as cost-benefit calculations were not incorporated in the 
development of the solution spaces. Before the solution spaces were introduced, 
people were asked to indicate their professions, years of work experience, and the 
country they work in. The survey was then divided into the three chapters: egress route 
design, alerting systems, and people’s behavior. For each solution, the respondent 
could choose between three responses: Yes, With further improvements, and No. 
 
Additionally, the participant had the option to leave a comment directly under each 
introduced solution.  
 
The last part of the survey briefly introduced the idea of the theoretically developed 
pattern language tool (see section 5.9). The participants were requested to allocate 
the stakeholder who would be in charge of this tool, assuming it is fully developed and 
implemented.  
 
The survey outline can be found in Appendix G.

5. Results 
The subsequent pages showcase the findings derived and identified in this thesis. This 
chapter offers the conclusions drawn from the methods introduced in the previous 
chapter, along with the resulting taxonomy, solution spaces, and patterns. Additionally, 
a real-world example is presented, demonstrating the application of the pattern 
language to a case study, along with the validation of this approach and its acceptance 
within the industry. 
 
5.1 Gap Analysis  
The building industry is guided by regulations, standards, and certification systems to 
create safe and sustainable buildings. These are utilized for orientation by all relevant 
stakeholders. However, the principles of universal design are frequently neglected in 
these systems. To identify gaps in current practices, the Danish building regulation 
BR18 and the three certification systems DGNB, LEED, and BREEAM were analyzed 
in more depth to identify gaps regarding universal design. Only the relevant chapters 
to the scope of this thesis were chosen (see section 4.2). The fully documented gap 
analysis can be found in Appendix A.  
 
BR18 
The BR18 does not have criteria specifically considering universal design in residential 
buildings. They have some criteria aligning with the solutions provided by isUD, 
however, they are kept general or to lose. For this gap analysis, Chapters 2 – Access 
and 5 – Fire of BR18 were evaluated.  
 
The following main gaps were identified in BR18:  
§ Differences in colors (contrast) or material (tactility) can be circumvented by light 
installations, however, contrast and tactility are crucial for visually impaired and 
blind people to be able to guide their way through the built environment. Further, 
people with cognitive impairments, for instance, autism, need structure to cope 
with the environment (Autism Speaks, n.d.). In isUD, wayfinding systems that 
include tactile guide stripes that have a different color and texture are suggested 
(IDEA Center, n.d.-b).  
==PAGE:48==
Results 
 
37 
§ The door width of 0,77 meters is too narrow. According to the standard ISO/TR 
13570-2:2014(E), the maximum occupied width of a wheelchair is 0,80 meters 
(Dansk Standard, 2014) which results in wheelchair users not being able to 
enter the building, apartment, or further rooms in the apartment. In isUD, a door 
width of a minimum of 0,865 meters is suggested (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ Information about the layout of the building does not need to be displayed in any 
form in a residential building, however, permanently displayed information in 
regular writing and Braille supports residents and visitors to be aware of egress 
procedures and alerting systems. In isUD, a display of wayfinding systems at all 
primary entrances, access points to each floor, and corridor intersections should 
be provided (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ Only one smoke detector with a sound signal is required in an apartment, 
however, hearing-impaired and deaf people have difficulties in recognizing 
those systems. Further, sounds can be overstimulating to people with cognitive 
impairments, for instance, autism (Good, 2011).  In isUD, no solution regarding 
alerting systems is given, yet different systems with additional visual signals and 
vibration should be provided. 
§ No further lighting installations (e.g., anti-panic lighting or egress route lighting) 
in buildings with a top-story floor height under 22 meters are required, however, 
in case of light failure, hallways and stairwells would be dark which increases 
the risks of accidents. In isUD, no solution regarding those lighting installations 
is given, yet it is suggested to implement as no difference is seen for impaired 
or disabled people between buildings lower or above 22 meters. 
§ Buildings with two stories above ground must not be equipped with elevators, 
however, this excludes people with mobility impairment or sitting in a wheelchair 
from moving in or visiting people within the apartment. In isUD, all stairs along 
primary circulation routes have an adjacent elevator (IDEA Center, n.d.-b).  
§ Installation of a firefighting lift is only required in buildings with a top-story floor 
height of 22 meters. In cases where the aerial ladder of the firefighter truck is 
not able to reach a safe area provided on a higher floor, the rescue of people 
with mobility impairment or wheelchair users is limited. In isUD, no solution 
regarding a firefighting lift is given.  
 
 
==PAGE:49==
Results 
 
38 
DGNB 
The newest version of the Danish DGNB criteria catalog for new construction and 
comprehensive renovation (version 2023) introduced one criterion called Universal 
Design to ensure access for all and design that supports a long life and many life 
situations. It is seen as important for inclusive health and comfort. (Green Building 
Council Denmark, 2023) In general, the DGNB minimum requirements align with the 
BR18 requirements. The Green Building Council Denmark adjusted the original DGNB 
system to Danish standards to provide a country-specific system. For this gap analysis, 
the criterion of Universal Design was evaluated. It includes access conditions to, at, 
and in the building, stairs and their fit-outs, elevators, and the furnishing of homes. 
It is important to note that some DGNB criteria meet the universal design requirement 
set by isUD in the medium or maximum value. As the building industry often applies 
minimum criteria (see section 5.3), those values are considered. The minimum 
requirements of DGNB are in alignment with those outlined in BR18, and thus included 
in the listing. 
 
The following main gaps were identified in DGNB:  
§ Although the universal design criteria must be fulfilled to receive the certification, 
it is possible to do so with the minimum requirements (equal BR18) 
§ Tactile elements and color contrast must only be implemented outside the 
building. However, contrast and tactility are crucial for visually impaired and 
blind people to be able to guide their way through the built environment. Further, 
people with cognitive impairments, for instance, autism, need structure to cope 
with the environment. In isUD, primary routes are equipped with tactile guide 
stripes that have a different color and textures. Primary routes are including 
routes inside the building. (IDEA Center, n.d.-b) 
§ The minimum door width of 0,77 meters is too narrow. According to the standard 
ISO/TR 13570-2:2014(E), the maximum occupied width of a wheelchair is 0,80 
meters (Dansk Standard, 2014) which results in wheelchair users not being able 
to enter the building, apartment, or further rooms in the apartment. In isUD, a 
door width of a minimum of 0,865 meters is suggested (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ Differences in colors (contrast) or material (tactility) can be circumvented by light 
installations in the minimum criteria, however, contrast and tactility are crucial 
for visually impaired and blind people to be able to guide their way through the 
==PAGE:50==
Results 
 
39 
built environment. Further, people with cognitive impairments, for instance, 
autism, need structure to cope with the environment (Autism Speaks, n.d.). In 
isUD, wayfinding systems that include tactile guide stripes that have a different 
color and texture are suggested (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ The maximum score for the criteria considers handrails at two heights on both 
sides. However, this may necessitate that people of varying heights utilize more 
physical effort to obtain guidance and support. In isUD, two handrails in the 
heights of 0,61 meters and 0,84-0,915 meters on both sides of the stairs are 
suggested (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
 
LEED 
LEED published a pilot criterion named Inclusive Design in 2019. However, as it is 
currently a pilot credit, it remains in a test phase and has not yet been integrated into 
the certification system. The criterion includes physical access in and outside of the 
building, wayfinding, assistive technology, emotional health, and inclusive spaces. 
 
The following main gaps were identified in LEED: 
§ Tactile guidance and attention patterns must only be installed in exterior areas. 
However, contrast and tactility are crucial for visually impaired and blind people 
to be able to guide their way through the built environment. Further, people with 
cognitive impairments, for instance, autism, need structure to cope with the 
environment (Autism Speaks, n.d.). In isUD, primary routes are equipped with 
tactile guide stripes that have a different color and textures. Primary routes are 
including routes inside the building (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ The area of the clear floor space, in front of appliances and fixtures, with 0,76 x 
1,32 meters is too small. According to the standard ISO/TR 13570-2:2014(E), 
the maximum occupied area of a wheelchair is 0,80 x 1,33 meters (Dansk 
Standard, 2014) which results in wheelchair users not being able to access 
these appliances and fixtures. In isUD, a clear floor space of 0,865 x 1,42 meters 
is suggested (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ Only the provision of ambient lighting is required, however, in case of light 
failure, hallways and stairwells would be dark which increases the risks of 
accidents. In isUD, no solution regarding those lighting installations is given, yet 
==PAGE:51==
Results 
 
40 
the installation of further lighting, for instance, anti-panic and egress route 
lighting, as described for BR18 is suggested.  
 
BREEAM 
Within the criterion of accessibility in the BREEAM criteria catalog for international new 
construction (version 6.0, 2021) Inclusive and accessible design (residential only) is 
introduced. This refers to a checklist of the Lifetime Homes design criteria. However, 
the solutions proposed within the criteria are limited to people with walking 
impairments, hence, wheelchair users.  
 
The following main gaps were identified in BREEAM: 
§ The minimum door width of 0,75 meters is too narrow. According to the standard 
ISO/TR 13570-2:2014(E), the maximum occupied width of a wheelchair is 0,80 
meters (Dansk Standard, 2014) which results in wheelchair users not being able 
to enter the building, apartment, or further rooms in the apartment. In isUD, a 
door width of a minimum of 0,865 meters is suggested (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ The minimum width of 0,90 meters and maximum width of 1,20 meters for the 
hallway is insufficient for wheelchair users with an occupied width of 0,80 meters 
to pass simultaneously with walking people having an average width of 0,531 
meters (Dansk Standard, 2014, 2017). In isUD, a hallway width of 1,22 meters 
is suggested  (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ A handrail on one side of the stairs and at a height of 1,0 meters is required. 
However, this may necessitate that people of varying heights utilize more 
physical effort to obtain guidance and support. In isUD, two handrails in the 
heights of 0,61 meters and 0,84-0,915 meters on both sides of the stairs are 
suggested (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
§ Lifetime home criteria mainly focus on wheelchair users, however, inclusive 
design - as the criterion is named - has to incorporate diverse abilities 
(Interaction Design Foundation, 2016).

Results

5.2 Empirical Research 
The findings of the observations made by the researcher are presented in the following 
paragraphs. 
 
House of Disabled People’s Organizations 
The visit on April 18th, 2024, included a visual inspection of the building and an 
interview with Christian Bundegaard, who is an employee and representative of the 
Dansk Blindesamfund. During the visit, the atrium, the basement, the main stairs, as 
well as one office wing were inspected. The pictures in Figures 9-14 show relevant 
solutions that the Disabled People's Organizations Denmark (DOPD) has implemented 
in the building and is likely to be adapted to residential buildings. All pictures were 
taken by the author of this thesis.  
 
The findings are divided into five categories: Escape Route for Mobility-impaired 
People and Wheelchair Users, Handrails and Fencing, Tactile Guidance on the Floor, 
Color Contrast, and Technical Installations.  
 
Escape Route for Mobility-impaired People and Wheelchair Users 
Figure 9 shows the solution to provide a safe escape route to the ground floor and out 
of the building for people with walking disabilities. From the atrium, doors are installed 
on both sides of the elevator area to an access balcony (picture 1). All doors from the 
atrium to the access balcony and back to the elevator area are equipped with push-
button mechanisms that facilitate their opening. These are situated at a height that is 
convenient for wheelchair users as well as walking people. Picture 2 in Figure 9 shows 
the elevator area. The gate in front of the atrium is closed when the fire alarm is 
activated. This creates a fire-safe area behind the gate where people can wait up to

hour 
or 
take 
the 
elevators 
down 
to 
the 
ground 
floor 
(Dansk 
Handicaporganisationer, 2019). As is seen in the back of the picture, people can enter 
the area via the access balcony.

Handrails and Fencing 
Figure 11 shows the main staircase in the center of the building, leading to all upper 
floors and the basement, and its characteristics can be seen. DOPD introduced various 
features to make it easier for people of all abilities to navigate and use the stairs. 
Picture 6 shows handrails at two different heights, picture 7 metal studs indicating the 
floor number (here the third floor), and picture 8 the distinguished edges of the stairs. 
They used metal plates in contrast to the wooden stairs.  
 
Besides the tactile characteristics, the perforated plates used as fencing for the stairs 
and walkways, improve the acoustic throughout the whole atrium. This ensures calm 
surrounding on the upper floors even if events on the lower floors are held or not.  
 
 
Figure 11 - Visit of Office Building of Disabled People's Organizations. Pictures 6 to 8 show the main staircase 
situated in the middle of the atrium. DOPD implemented two handrails, tactile guiding on the handrails, as well as 
contrast on the stair edges. The perforated plates used as fencing optimize the acoustics throughout the whole 
atrium.

patterns, supported by a high luminance contrast were implemented. Figure 12, with

levels, respectively.

User Questionnaire 
A questionnaire was developed and distributed to involve people affected by 
disabilities. The questionnaire included questions about the housing situation and 
current challenges in general and with regards to fire safety. A total of seven people 
responded to the questionnaire. One representative from the Dansk Blindesamfund 
was interviewed directly. Two representatives from the Danske Døves Landsforbund 
and three from the Dansk Handicap Forbund returned the completed questionnaire. 
Additionally, one person with ADHD provided feedback. The transcript of the interview 
and the answers to the survey are to be found in Appendix B. 
 
From the results shown in Table 5, three people living in single-family homes - of which 
all are wheelchair users - could adjust their homes according to their needs. All three 
homes are one-story buildings that permit leveled access to all rooms. One of the 
wheelchair users, additionally, has 24/7 personal assistance. The incorporation of 
15 
13 
14 
==PAGE:57==
Results 
 
46 
several installations and the circumstance of living in a one-story building, allow them 
unhindered use of and access to their homes. Hence, two of the wheelchair users are 
defining their houses as universally designed.  
 
Although none of the people indicated difficulties in wayfinding, the blind person 
claimed to have some in the beginning, and occasionally in situations where unknown 
obstacles are placed in the hallway by other residents.  
 
Six out of seven people answered that the current fire alerting system is sufficient. 
However, one of them is referring to her apartment. It is assumed that she installed 
further devices on her own because of the previously mentioned non-existing deaf-
friendly fire alarm. The only person who has denied it being a good solution is also a 
deaf person. She indicated that she had to accommodate some form of aid, which 
included flashing lights and vibrating devices. Two additional people for whom the 
solution is sufficient have stated the issue of people being deaf will not be able to be 
addressed. Regarding further installations, they requested enhanced signage and fire 
plans, the integration of flashing lights in all rooms, and a vibration device in the bed. 
Additionally, a less steep exit was requested by a wheelchair user.
