5.3 First Expert Interview Series 
Interviews were conducted with three industry experts (experts) of different 
backgrounds: project development, consulting, and architecture. This allowed for 
insights into why the industry may be slow in implementing universal design and which 
challenges the individual stakeholders are facing. The interviews provided a real-world 
perspective compared to the theoretical approaches identified in the literature review. 
The interviews aimed to broaden the understanding of the limitations, gaps, and 
obstacles faced by the industry. Further, already-implemented solutions were 
identified.  
 
The following experts were selected for the interviews: 
Christian Gjessing Bruun 
Head of ESG and Sustainability  AKF Holding A/S 
Emma Nielsen 
Project leader  
Carlberg ApS 
Victoria Chenais 
Architect  
Henning Larsen 
Architects A/S 
 
The interviews took place on, March 15th,2024 with C.G. Bruun, on March 25th,2024 
with E. Nielsen, and on April 22nd,2024, with V. Chenais. 
 
The following paragraphs represent the findings of the first interview series with these 
experts. As previously stated in section 4.4 a total of two interviews were conducted 
with each expert. The findings of the second series will be presented in section 5.11. 
The transcripts of the first interview series can be found in Appendix C.  
 
The results from the first interview series are divided into five categories: 
Understanding Universal Design, Incorporating Disabilities in the Building Design, 
Applying Universal Design Solutions, Fire Safety, and Financial Aspects. 
 
Understanding Universal Design 
In general, the interviewees shared a common understanding of what is meant by the 
term "universal design". However, their explanations of this concept did result in a 
greater variety of interpretations. This is partly because their occupations differ, but 
also due to the lack of a common definition of universal design. Universal design was 
==PAGE:59==
Results 
 
48 
defined as the accessibility for all users, the framework to improve people’s well-being 
and mental welfare, and as an “integration of a wider array of needs and making sure 
that everyone regardless of their physical or mental capabilities, feels welcomed, seen, 
safe, and can have as equal access to space, as people without physical impairments” 
(V. Chenais).  
 
Incorporating Disabilities in the Building Design 
During the interviews, all three interviewees referred to the Danish building regulations, 
noting that they include the consideration of people with disabilities. Such 
considerations are required by law or included in certification systems, such as DGNB.  
The two companies AKF and Henning Larsen are mainly focusing on physical 
disabilities. AKF implements solutions to ensure “that elderly people or handicapped 
people, physically handicapped people can enter buildings and all the functions” (C.G. 
Bruun). According to V. Chenais, Henning Larsen considers in their work “physical 
disabilities, people in wheelchairs, for instance, people with visual impairments”, 
however, it is mostly focused on physical disability. In contrast, Carlberg’s primary work 
focuses on people having neurodivergent disabilities, such as ADHD, autism, and 
further cognitive impairments. Both, AKF and Henning Larsen think “designing for the 
lesser crowd can be tricky” (V. Chenais) and that standardized solutions and products 
are always easier to implement. “Especially, when you do not even know if there is 
going to be any blind people in the development” (C.G. Bruun). 
 
Applying Universal Design Solutions 
The companies of the interviewees are implementing solutions considering a variety 
of users. These result primarily from the building regulations and certification system. 
For example, contrast and tactile elements (e.g., through different stones) are placed 
on the outside of the entrance areas of a building. It was suggested by V. Chenais that 
solutions should be implemented in a flexible and transformable manner, 
accommodating different needs. Further, she emphasized that solutions should also 
incorporate more than the turning space of a wheelchair, also light design, sound 
design, as well as tactility can be improved.  
 
Carlberg is a small research consultancy company, “[they] do work with a lot of 
universal design. [They] see universal design as one thing among the many things 
==PAGE:60==
Results 
 
49 
[they] work with, but [they] work with social sustainability as a kind of broad umbrella 
term” (E. Nielsen). Therefore, they incorporate universal design considerations in each 
of their projects. For example, higher standards than demanded by the building 
regulations will be set in the creation of building programs for architecture competitions 
to tender. These standards are especially highlighting the importance of acoustic, 
sound, and space. Moreover, contrast elements on steps and means of color 
coordination are used to not only support visually impaired people but also people 
having neurodivergent issues. The implementation of their solutions is always 
validated by follow-ups, post-occupancy evaluations, observations, measurements, or 
surveys. Nevertheless, Carlberg is still relying on the architects and engineers to 
incorporate the proposed standards into their designs. 
 
Nonetheless, all experts also mentioned limitations, and why they or the industry have 
not implemented broader universal design solutions yet. The main issues mentioned 
were the lack of regulations and the low minimum standards. “If you have these 
minimum requirements, often you will get the minimum solution. You would not get the 
in-between solution” (C.G. Bruun) in case a range for dimensioning a solution existed. 
The “rules themselves are valid and are very well-intentioned but then the translation 
in the built environment can not necessarily bring qualities” (V. Chenais). The industry 
is perceived as being slow to adopt new ideas and resistant to change. Further, a 
common understanding and knowledge are deficient. It is often the case that the 
budget is not allocated for pre-project clarifications on universal design or post-project 
evaluations on how the building worked and functioned, and if implemented solutions 
are sufficient for the user. Consequently, it is challenging to learn, share knowledge, 
and implement changes. “It [Universal Design] is not seen as something useful and 
important” (E. Nielsen).  
 
In conclusion, the application of universal design is neglected due to missing 
regulations and a common understanding. The three experts support the 
implementation of more universal design solutions if it is done correctly.  
 
Fire Safety 
Regarding the implementation of additional fire safety installations, the experts did not 
mention any exceedances of the requirements specified in the building regulations. 
==PAGE:61==
Results 
 
50 
The reasons for this include the absence of laws, a lack of knowledge, and concerns 
about the misuse of technical installations by building occupants.  
 
In terms of the design of the egress route, the interviewees indicated that there were 
some minor adjustments made facilitating the rescuing of mobility-impaired residents. 
For example, balconies are incorporated into each apartment to serve as a potential 
rescue area, odd-shaped corners, and hallways are tried to be avoided, accessible 
bedrooms are closer to the exit of the apartment, and waiting areas in the hallway are 
implemented. However, “the architectural qualities of a building or fitting the program 
will always prevail on the quality of an egress route. It is more like [the architects] have 
to tick the box and make sure it complies” (V. Chenais).  
 
Financial Aspects  
All experts were in joint agreement that change is costly, yet, they saw the cost 
increase in different areas of the project. On the one hand, universal design is seen as 
“another product than the standard product that is on the shelf” (C.G. Bruun) which 
makes it more expensive, and the time allocated to rethink solutions to make them 
universal is “more work, hence more budget” (V. Chenais). On the other hand, 
“universal design does not necessarily cost extra, but it costs in the early process” (E. 
Nielsen) to understand, and to take time and to ensure knowledge goes through the 
whole process. Further, E. Nielsen emphasized that choices of product implementation 
should be done more pragmatically in favor of universal design solutions than to 
aesthetics of the façade. 
 
In essence, the higher costs are not inherently tied to the adoption of a universal design 
solution. Instead, it primarily reflects the investment of time in the initial stages of the 
process. This phase, marked by thorough collaboration and knowledge sharing, serves 
as the foundation for uncovering and implementing effective solutions. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:62==
Results 
 
51 
5.4 Taxonomy 
Based on the findings from the literature review, gap analysis, empirical research, and 
expert interviews, a taxonomy was developed. The developed taxonomy … 
1) … aims to create a comprehensive classification system for fire safety in 
residential buildings.  
2) … shares a common definition of activities that need to be considered to design 
a building according to universal design principles.  
3) … sets general requirements which should be fulfilled by the building industry. 
 
The requirements are described in the form of attributes and levels. The layout and 
structure of the EU taxonomy served as a blueprint for the creation of this taxonomy.  
 
The development of the taxonomy resulted in the identification and definition of seven 
attributes. These attributes describe specific elements, characteristics, and qualities 
that should be implemented in a building. They are defined as follows:  
 
Haptic. Haptic refers to everything concerning the sense of touch (Hatzfeld & Kern 
Editors, 2014). It helps people to be aware of things without seeing them and guide 
themselves through the built environment. Haptic elements, which can be touched with 
hands, feet, or assistive technology will facilitate wayfinding and orientation in a 
residential building. Haptic devices can support an alerting system to indicate that an 
action is required. 
 
Levelness. Levelness indicates the uniformity of the floor surface (TEJJY, n.d.). A 
leveled floor is crucial to allow every person to access and walk through residential 
buildings, no matter if walking with or without assistive technology, or driving in a 
wheelchair. 
 
Optic. Optic refers to everything concerning light and vision (Young, 2023). Visual 
elements in residential buildings support the orientation and guidance for residents, as 
well as visitors. Lighting elements support those or other features by ensuring visibility 
also in dark surroundings. Optical devices can support an alerting system to indicate 
that an action is required. 
 
==PAGE:63==
Results 
 
52 
Preparation. In case of an emergency, residents must be ready to cope with potentially 
stressful situations. Previous preparation is essential to enable residents to handle 
such situations in an appropriate and timely manner, while also facilitating an orderly 
and secure evacuation for all other residents and their visitors. Preparation in this 
context, is the practice and awareness of situations requiring fast and correct actions. 
 
Size. Size indicates how small or large something is. Size can help people see or read 
a specific text or it can define the area of a space allocated to a specific use. 
 
Sound. Sound refers to everything that can be heard by the human ear. It is dependent 
on the frequency and pitch of the auditory scene (Oxenham, 2018). Sound helps 
people to become aware of things by playing a specific alert or recorded voice. Sound 
alerting systems are commonly used alerting systems. 
 
Space. Space is “a boundless, three-dimensional extent in which objects and events 
occur and have relative positions and directions” (Vastaranta et al., 2020). The built 
environment is a human-made space in which people live, work, and recreate 
(Kaklauskas & Gudauskas, 2016). Here, space is meant to be an extent where people 
can freely move or wait without interfering with other residents or visitors of the building. 
 
The attributes of the taxonomy can be further described in different levels of detail, as 
illustrated in Figure 15. Level 1 (light blue) introduces criteria and requirements for how 
the attributes can be displayed in the build environment. Level 2 (light grey), if defined, 
provides further details on where or how the implementation should be done. 
 
==PAGE:64==
Results 
 
53 
 
Figure 15 - Attributes and levels of detail of the taxonomy for fire safety. The attributes (dark blue) describe 
specific elements, characteristics, and qualities. Level 1 (light blue) introduces criteria and requirements for how 
the attributes can be displayed in the build environment. Level 2 (light grey) provides further details on where or 
how the implementation should be done.

==PAGE:65==

54 
The definitions of the first levels and, if defined, second levels are as follows:  
 
Tactility. The installation of tactile elements in the common circulation areas that 
extend from the outside entrance to the top floor of the building. 
1. Tactile elements must be integrated on the floor guiding the way through areas 
with wider circulation spaces.  
2. Tactile elements must be installed on the walls in narrow circulation spaces, 
indicating the location and possible corridor junctions. 
 
Braille. Integration of signs offering readability in ordinary letters and with tactile code 
braille. 
 
Grip. Implementation of grip-friendly and anti-slippery materials. 
 
Vibration. Installation of alerting systems in the bedroom, which provide haptic signals 
in case of emergency. The devices should be connected to the other alerting systems 
and should not require battery change. 
 
Gradient. Ensure that exits and corridors throughout the whole building have no 
threshold. Residents must be enabled to self-evacuate from each floor without having 
to use stairs, steep ramps, or steps. 
 
Contrast. Implementation of contrasting elements in common and private spaces. 
They should be designed in an appropriate size.  
1. Contrast can be ensured by using light-dark elements that are distinguishable from 
each other.  
2. Contrast can be ensured by using strong colors indicating a specific area of the 
building or apartment. 
 
Lighting. Implementation of lighting elements throughout the entire residential building 
to support wayfinding and orientation, even in low-visibility conditions. It must be 
ensured that lighting elements are operational in the event of a fire emergency, with an 
emergency circuit that activates the lights upon the activation of the fire alerting 
system. Installation of alerting systems that provide a visual signal in case of 
==PAGE:66==
Results 
 
55 
emergency. The devices should be connected to the other alerting systems and are 
operated on the electrical circuit. They should be installed in bedrooms and the 
hallways leading to the exit of the apartment in places that are visible from all sides. 
 
Signage. The residential building must be equipped with signage indicating 
emergency exits, egress route directions, and instructions in case of emergency. All 
signs should be color-coded and illuminated according to DS/ISO 7010:2019. 
 
Training. In case of an emergency, residents must be ready to cope with potentially 
stressful situations. Previous preparation is essential to enable residents to handle 
such situations in an appropriate and timely manner, while also facilitating an orderly 
and secure evacuation for all other residents and their visitors. Preparation in this 
context, is the practice and awareness of situations requiring fast and correct actions. 
 
Font Size. All written elements within the building must be large enough to be read by 
most residents and visitors.  
1. Written Instructions for the use of elevators to ensure awareness of the specific 
usage during fire emergencies.  
2. Written Instructions on emergency signs, to ensure understanding of actions to 
be taken with a quick grasp.  
3. Floor numbers to ensure fast readability in stressful situations. 
 
Width. Ensure wide enough circulation spaces throughout the whole building under 
the consideration, that in emergencies multiple people are passing simultaneously.  
1. Doors must be designed in an adequate width, so people with assistive technology 
can easily enter the subsequent area. 
2. Circulation spaces like hallways must be designed to ensure that people, no 
matter if with or without assistive technology, pass each other without collision.  
 
Instructions. Installation of alerting systems, which give instructions in case of an 
emergency. The devices should be connected to the other alerting systems and are 
operated on the electrical circuit. They should be installed in bedrooms and the 
hallways leading to the exit of the apartment. 
 
==PAGE:67==
Results 
 
56 
Tone. Installation of alerting systems playing a tone in case of emergency. The devices 
should be connected to the other alerting systems and are operated on the electrical 
circuit. They should be installed in bedrooms and the hallways leading to the exit of the 
apartment. 
1. The tone should have a sufficiently loud volume.  
2. The frequency band of the tone should be such that it can be heard by everyone.  
 
Area. In residential buildings, a dedicated area must be implemented as a safe waiting 
space for people not being able to use the stairs. This area must be provided on each 
floor in front or close to the elevator. Especially, in case of emergency, it must be 
ensured, that the space is not interfering with passing residents or visitors of the 
building and that it is close to the elevator or rescue area. 
 
Spatiality. The floor plans have been designed in such a way that each apartment has 
direct access to a safe corridor, which leads to the staircases and elevators. This 
corridor is located either on the outside of the building or inside the building along the 
outside wall, with exit options. 
 
The different levels are not seen as separate criteria but rather as interconnected 
qualities and characteristics. Therefore, the taxonomy introduces links to connected 
levels. These describe in which manner one level can influence and support another 
level. In addition, the taxonomy includes a section on benefits, where the group of 
people who will particularly benefit especially the requirement are defined. Although 
universal design is meant to facilitate access and use (Interaction Design Foundation, 
2016) for everyone, there may be certain groups who benefit more from a solution. 
Moreover, the taxonomy provides information about a connected pattern. The pattern 
language is introduced in section 5.7. The comprehensive taxonomy can be found in 
Appendix D.  
 
5.5 Taxonomy Tool 
A taxonomy tool has been developed to facilitate the use of the taxonomy. As 
mentioned in section 3.4, the structure of the taxonomy is based on the structure of the 
EU Taxonomy Compass. The idea of the tool is to provide a comprehensive but easy-
==PAGE:68==
Results 
 
57 
to-follow overview. The tool is divided into three groups: egress route design, alerting 
system, and people’s behavior. This grouping is also applied in the solution spaces 
(section 5.6). Figure 16 shows the tool layout.

enhanced usability compared to a table, such as Excel.

5.6 Solution Spaces 
The taxonomy represents a design classification system whose requirements must be 
considered to design a building universally regarding fire safety. To provide the industry 
with a framework for reference, solution spaces were developed. These can be 
regarded as design options that could be employed to comply with the taxonomy.   
 
As introduced in section 3.1, seven principles for universal design were developed to 
maximize access by everyone (University at Buffalo, n.d.), “to evaluate existing design, 
to guide the design process, and to educate both designers and consumers” (Mace, 
Connel, et al., 1997). The solution spaces were analyzed to ascertain their compliance 
with the principles to ensure that the broad spectrum of characterizations connected 
to universal design is included (see Compliance with principles in Table 6). 
 
Table 6 shows the solution spaces organized into the three groups, as introduced in 
section 5.5: egress route design, alerting system, and people’s behavior. An indication 
regarding compliance with one or more of the seven principles is given. The objective 
is to illustrate that the implementation of the solution spaces comprehensively meets 
a broad spectrum of characterizations connected to universal design. A total of 29 
solution spaces were developed.  
A more detailed description of each solution space can be found in Appendix E.

Table 6 - Solution Spaces. The table is divided into the three groups: egress route design, alerting system, and 
people’s behavior. It indicates the solution spaces connected to the different attributes and level. Further 
compliance with one or more principles of universal design is shown. 
 
Some of the proposed solution spaces are to a certain extent already implemented in 
residential buildings. For completeness, they are also incorporated. Particularly, these 
include handrails which are designed allowing for a secure grip (E5), the 
implementation of anti-panic lighting (E12), and the illuminated signs indicating the 
location of exits (E16). Currently, the implementation of anti-panic lighting is only 
mandatory for residential buildings with a top-story floor height of 22 meters, as 
specified under §96 of the BR18 (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023).  
 
The other solution spaces introduced are identified by analyzing existing standards, 
building regulations from a different country, certification systems, or existing 
implementations. For instance, in the house of the Disabled People’s Organizations, 
several best-practices were identified (Figure 9 to Figure 14). Further, the 
questionnaire confirmed the need for a different alerting system, and the interview with 
V. Chenais (22.04.2024) showed that waiting spaces are incorporated in hallways.  
 
In conclusion, for those solution spaces that are not yet integrated into residential 
buildings, known solutions derived from diverse building types, existing projects, and 
==PAGE:75==
Results 
 
64 
other domains were utilized to ensure the development of cost-effective solution 
spaces that are easy and fast to implement.     
 
As indicated in the introduction to this section, compliance with the seven principles of 
universal design was checked by following the definitions and guidelines given by 
Mace, Connell, et al. (1997) as introduced in Chapter 3.  
 
Solution spaces complying with Principle 1 – Equitable Use 
The solution spaces E4, E6, E12, E13, E19, E20, E22, A1, A2, A3, and A4 are useful 
and marketable to people with diverse abilities (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997). They 
provide all users with the same usage options. The solution must provide equal service 
to each person (E19, E20, and E22), for instance, sufficient space to pass doors and 
hallways. Alternatively, they provide equivalent services (E4, E6, E12, E13, A1, A2, A3, 
and A4) such as distinct alerting signals and information displays, different escape 
routes, or lighting installations. 
 
Solution spaces complying with Principle 2 – Flexibility in Use 
The solution spaces E2, A1, A2, A3, A4, and P2 accommodate a diverse range of 
individual preferences and abilities (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997). The solution E2 is 
usable for people being right- or left-handed by placing handrails on both sides of the 
stairs. The different alerting systems (A1, A2, A3, and A4) provide an indirect choice in 
methods of use by offering different warning signals and the option to integrate further 
installations into the apartment. P2, which is the annual training is supposed to be done 
for each tenant at their own pace.  
 
Solution spaces complying with Principle 3 – Simple and intuitive Use 
The solution space E15 leads to an easy-to-understand layout of the egress route, 
“regardless of the user’s experience, knowledge, language skills, or current 
concentration level” (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997).  Rectangular lamps (see Picture 15 
in Figure 14) are designed to eliminate unnecessary complexity. Even in cases of 
limited visibility, either through smoke or impairment of the person, the direction of the 
egress route can be identified.  
 
 
==PAGE:76==
Results 
 
65 
Solution spaces complying with Principle 4 – Perceptible Information 
The solution spaces E1, E3, E4, E8, E9, E10, E11, E14, E16, E17, and E18 
communicate necessary information about the surrounding situation effectively to the 
user, “regardless of ambient conditions or the user’s sensory abilities” (Mace, Connel, 
et al., 1997). Some of them use tactile or pictorial elements to present essential 
information, either through markings on the floor (E1) or through installations on walls 
(E3, E16, and E17). Others are designed with adequate contrast, such as illuminance 
(E8, E9, and E14) or color (E10 and E11) contrast, between the installation and its 
surroundings. Further, solution spaces complying with this principle maximize the 
readability of essential information (E4 and E18).  
 
Solution spaces complying with Principle 5 – Tolerance for Error 
The solution spaces E7, E11, E16, A5, P1, and P2 are designed to mitigate the risks 
and adverse consequences associated with unintentional or accidental actions (Mace, 
Connel, et al., 1997). Ensuring the non-existence of a threshold on the way out of the 
apartment or building (E7) eliminates hazardous elements in the form of stumbling. 
The solution spaces E11 and A5 introduce the installation of warning elements for 
hazards that are installed in the building. E16 provides fail-safe features by designing 
the signs in photoluminescent material to ensure their visibility even in case of light 
failure. The solution spaces P1 and P2, which refer to the preparation of residents via 
one-time and recurring training, are introduced to discourage unconscious action and 
tasks that require vigilance (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997). 
 
Solution spaces complying with Principle 6 – Low Physical Effort 
The solution spaces E2, E5, E6, E7, E19, and E20 “can be used efficiently, comfortably, 
and with a minimum of fatigue” (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997). Some of the solution 
spaces were established to allow people in the building to always move in a neutral 
body position (E2, E19, and E20). The implementation of two handrails, as well as 
hallways and doors that are sufficiently wide, ensures that people are not required to 
perform actions such as bending or turning. Other solutions minimize physical effort 
used over a longer time (E5, E6, and E7). Anti-slip materials provide a safe grip for 
people relying on the support of a handrail. Further, mobility-impaired people are not 
required to encounter steps or stairs. 
 
==PAGE:77==
Results 
 
66 
Solution spaces complying with Principle 7 – Size and Space for Approach and Use 
The solution spaces E19, E20, and E21 provide appropriate space for approach and 
use regardless of the user’s body size, posture, or mobility (Mace, Connel, et al., 1997). 
They lead to the provision of adequate space, in the form of wider doors, hallways, and 
sufficiently sized waiting spaces, for people relying on assistive devices or personal 
assistance.  
 
5.7 Pattern Language 
The taxonomy and solution spaces, introduced in sections 5.4 and 5.6, were used to 
develop a pattern language.  This was accomplished by combining different solution 
spaces into groups that collectively describe an arrangement within the building. 
Subsequently, the patterns were developed in a more general manner and connected 
to attributes and their levels. The pattern language is created to serve as a set of rules 
to improve fire safety in residential buildings based on universal design.  
 
The patterns can be executed by applying previously introduced solution spaces, 
whereby they indicate potential solutions. Further developed ideas and solutions can 
be incorporated to implement the pattern in a building, as long as they always consider 
the universal design approach.  
 
The pattern language is inspired by the original publication by Christopher Alexander 
from 1977 as introduced in section 3.5. The layout and structure were strictly followed 
to create a pattern language for residential buildings concerning fire safety.  
 
By combining the taxonomy and solution spaces, six patterns were identified:  
1) Integrated alerting system,  
2) Learned behavior,  
3) Enough space,  
4) Visible route,  
5) Tangible route, and  
6) Egress without stairs.  
The individual connection of the patterns to the attributes and their levels in the 
taxonomy are shown in Figure 19.  
==PAGE:78==
Results 
 
67 
 
Figure 19 - Connection of each pattern to the taxonomy. The attributes (dark blue) and levels (light blue) were 
combined to identify the six patterns (light grey).  
 
As shown, the pattern integrated alerting system stemmed from the levels vibration, 
lighting, instruction, and tone. All four levels introduce solution spaces for alarm

devices with different signaling. The combination of the various signals allows for the 
implementation of a universal alerting system, which ensures that the fire alarm is 
recognized by each resident and visitor. The pattern learned behavior includes the 
level training. The solution spaces introduce the knowledge-sharing of egress routes 
and alerting systems for residents, which ensures preparedness and the right behavior 
in case of an emergency. The pattern enough space was created by combining the 
levels width and area. The solution spaces of both levels introduce spaces such as 
hallways, entrances to apartments, or waiting areas that must be wide enough to 
permit unobstructed passage, entry, or waiting, respectively. This approach avoids 
collisions and accidents in situations where residents and visitors are in a rushed state 
of mind and move through the building at a fast pace. Two patterns according to the 
design of the route were established. Visible route and tangible route resulted from the 
levels contrast, lighting, signage, and font size, and tactility, braille, and grip, 
respectively. All solution spaces introduce the implementation of specific elements in 
common areas to ensure wayfinding independent of the residents’ and visitors’ 
abilities. The sixth pattern, egress without stairs was developed including the levels 
gradient, width, and spatiality. The solution spaces introduced in these levels facilitate 
egress for residents and visitors with mobility impairments, particularly in the event of 
a fire emergency. 
 
In the following, the pattern language will be introduced in detail. As previously 
mentioned in 3.5, the structure followed the original pattern language by Christopher 
Alexander. However, two components remain unconsidered in this pattern language. 
First, the picture in the beginning of each pattern will not be included, and second, 
there is no paragraph at the end about the connection of single patterns. The six 
patterns are structured and formatted according to the following: 
 
§ NUMBER AND TITLE 
§ Introduction 
§ Core of the problem 
§ Body of the problem 
§ Solution 
§ Diagram illustrating the solution 
==PAGE:80==
Results 
 
69 
This pattern language concerning fire safety in residential buildings is meant to be seen 
as one comprehensive design rule. Therefore, all patterns should be implemented to 
ensure a universally designed building.  
 
1 INTEGRATED ALERTING SYSTEM 
… smoke detectors are nothing new. They are in every apartment. The standard 
version sends a loud alarm to notify residents and visitors. But what about people who 
are not able to hear this sound or people who would feel overwhelmed? This pattern 
introduces alternatives to the standard version.  
 
In residential buildings, only smoke alarms that emit an audible warning signal 
are mandatory and therefore integrated. 
 
According to the Danish building regulations and its requirements regarding fire, smoke 
alerting systems must be installed in the apartments and connected to the electricity 
supply (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023). The function of a smoke alerting system in 
residential buildings is to alert people in the housing where the fire is registered (Social- 
og Boligstyrelsen, 2019). Further, one smoke detector is required to be installed in the 
apartment.  
 
The currently used smoke alerting systems are not manufactured following a universal 
design, as they fail to provide warning for people with hearing impairment. Moreover, 
smoke detectors can cause overwhelmed senses for the blind and visually impaired 
(Fire Administration, 1999), or autistic people (Good, 2011). Existing solutions 
regarding smoke detectors with different alerting signals are already known on the 
market. For example, the company Hörgeschädigten Technik B&K GmbH in Germany 
introduced several devices for people with hearing-impairment. These include a 
wireless vibrating pillow (Drahtloses Vibrationskissen, Typ 83.0) and a radio flashlight 
(Funkblitzlampe Control, Typ 82.2). However, these devices must always be operated 
in combination with the radio-capable smoke detector (Rauchwarnmelder, Typ 89.0) 
from the same company. According to the company, the vibrating pillow is laid under 
the pillow without any fixation (Hörgeschädigten Technik B&K GmbH, n.d.), which 
induces the risk of falling out of the bed. An additional example would be a voice 
sounder connected to the alerting system giving instructions to the residents and 
==PAGE:81==
Results 
 
70 
visitors. This procedure has already been introduced in the House of the DOPD 
(Interview with C. Bundegaard, 18.04.2024). 
 
In conclusion, universal design options for smoke detectors exist but are limited and 
not used in common practice. Often tenants with hearing impairment must purchase 
the solution themselves and exchange existing smoke detectors with for example the 
previously introduced radio smoke detectors connected to further devices.  
Furthermore, it is a possibility that residents of apartments may develop a hearing 
impairment over time, particularly as they age. It is probable that those people fail to 
exchange devices in order to retain the ability to recognize the fire alarm.  
 
Therefore:  
Smoke alerting systems with different alerting signals should be integrated as 
standard in residential buildings. This will ensure the recognition of fire alarms 
no matter if people are affected by a disability or develop an impairment over 
time. Smoke detectors indicating a sound should be further connected to 
devices providing vibration, flashlight, and voice introduction. 
 
 
Figure 20 - Diagram for pattern "Integrated alerting system" 
 
2 LEARNED BEHAVIOR 
… known from office buildings, it is not uncommon to practice evacuation procedures 
to be prepared for the case of an emergency. Alerting systems will be started and every 
person, no matter if an occupant or visitor must leave the building. This pattern 
introduces the idea of implementing such training in residential buildings.  
==PAGE:82==
Results 
 
71 
Residents are not prepared for the case of an emergency. If residents are 
moreover affected by disabilities those situations can result in sensory 
overwhelming, hiding, or panicking.  
 
In situations where people are confronted with high danger, two distinct reactions may 
be observed. In such instances, people may either maintain calmness and comply with 
instructions, thereby facilitating the orderly evacuation process, or people may 
experience feelings of overwhelming distress, a narrow focus on their own needs, or 
even a fear response that leads to hiding (Good, 2011).  
 
Good (2011) explored the reactions of people with autism in case of fire. It became 
evident, that smoke alerting systems can cause sensory overload issues, and that they 
would rather not respond to the smoke alarm or even hide instead of evacuating. This 
situation would make it very difficult for the rescue personnel to find and save the 
person. It will cost time and put not only the residents in a dangerous situation but also 
the firefighters. To reduce the risk, not only Good (2011) suggests practices and home 
fire escape plans, but also the National Autistic Society. According to them, autistic 
people often like to prepare in advance before visiting shops, stores, or museums. This 
reduces their level of anxiety. (The National Autistic Society, n.d.) An adaption to 
prepare the evacuation in case of fire in a residential building could be possible.  
 
But not only autistic people struggle with high-tense situations. The senses of people 
who are blind or visually impaired can also be overwhelmed when the alarm of the 
smoke detector is activated. The high-decibel sound makes it difficult to process 
audible clues and instructions effectively. (Fire Administration, 1999)  
 
Besides people with impairment, non-impaired people can react selfishly and disregard 
helping people with further needs or prioritizing themselves. The concept of Proulx 
(2004), using elevators for evacuation, also emphasizes necessary training to ensure 
that everybody can leave the building as soon as possible. This specific solution can 
only work if non-mobility-impaired people take the stairs and leave the elevator for 
mobility-impaired residents and visitors. According to Proulx (2004), building 
occupants are capable of following evacuation procedures. To do so, instructions must 
be readily available, and training must take place.   
==PAGE:83==
Results 
 
72 
Therefore:  
At key handover, evacuation practices should be introduced to the tenant. 
Conducted by the landlord, the tenant will gain knowledge about the alerting 
systems and egress route design. Further, it should be possible to refresh the 
knowledge annually. 
 
 
Figure 21 - Diagram for pattern "Learned behavior" 
 
3 ENOUGH SPACE 
… leaving the building, residents must walk through corridors and stairwells. People 
using a wheelchair or rollator need more space, either to exit their apartment, move 
through the area, or wait for the elevators. This pattern defines the needed space to 
ensure obstacle-free egress.  
 
The defined mandatory hallway door width is insufficient to support the 
simultaneous passage of a person walking and a person in a wheelchair. Further, 
the required door width does not even allow a wheelchair user to pass. 
Residents waiting for an elevator to carry them down must wait in the hallway, 
which creates an obstacle for other individuals. 
 
In the event of a fire occurring in, above, or below an apartment, it is evident that this 
and adjacent residents will attempt to evacuate the apartment as quickly as possible 
and in the least obstructed manner possible. According to the egress route design, 
their way will lead out of their apartment through the protected corridors and stairwells 
==PAGE:84==
Results 
 
73 
to the outside - places that are defined as places of safety (Papaioannou, 2006a). 
However, the door width required in the regulations is too small for a wheelchair user 
to get out with external help. In addition, the design of the egress routes is often 
developed for people who are not mobility-impaired and can easily follow the stairs. 
People who are mobility-impaired, short, or long-term, are not considered and have 
difficulties in leaving the building to the ground floor. It can be argued that apartments 
specific for mobility-impaired people can be located on the ground floor. However, 
these are often limited to a small number and therefore might not be available in the 
preferred location. Further, it was observed that ground floors, also in new 
developments, are raised and only accessible via a few stairs. This design excludes 
wheelchair users from moving into the building at all. Moreover, it is not always the 
case that everyone who requires such accommodation wishes to live on the ground 
floor. It commonly occurs that people reside for a long time in their apartments and 
may get mobility-impaired with age. Those people could be trapped in the building if 
no external help or universal exit is provided. The U.S. Fire Administration/National 
Fire Data Center (2006) revealed, that elderly are at a higher risk of sustaining injuries 
during residential fires when attempting to evacuate themselves. This implies that at 
some location within the building, they encounter obstacles.  
 
Current designs of buildings show that the corridor is mostly designed as a connection 
from the elevator and stairs to the apartments. The width of a corridor required in the 
building regulations is not sufficient enough to generate a space where it is possible to 
pass as a walking person and a person using a wheelchair. Further space is needed 
to allow people with a mobility-impairment to wait for the elevator or a rescue team.  
 
Therefore:  
Create safe waiting spaces on each floor. Spaces that are sufficiently spacious 
to accommodate individuals with mobility impairments until the use of the 
elevator is possible or a rescue team can carry them down. Corridors and doors 
should be designed to permit wheelchair users to pass without risk of injury or 
collision. 
==PAGE:85==
Results 
 
74 
 
Figure 22 - Diagram for pattern "Enough space" 
 
4 VISIBLE ROUTE 
… leaving the building in a fire emergency can be demanding for people. People rely 
on visual support through light or contrast elements. It facilitates orientation and calms 
down. This pattern introduces possibilities for how to create such visual support in a 
residential building.  
 
Visual support in residential buildings is barely required in current regulations. 
No matter if egress route and anti-panic lighting, or contrast elements varying in 
luminance or color.  
 
Sufficient illuminated corridors and stairwells facilitate the wayfinding for every person. 
It decreases panic and helps guide residents on the way out of the building. Currently, 
residential buildings that are below 22 meters in height do not have to be equipped 
with anti-panic lighting according to the Danish building regulations (Social- og 
Boligstyrelsen, 2023). Because of this regulation, it is ordinary that only lighting for 
general use to illuminate the building is installed. Moreover, in some older buildings, 
the light is not activated automatically as soon as a person walks through a corridor 
but will only be activated if a light switch is pressed manually. In case of emergency, 
people may face difficulties in finding the light switch under stress or think to be able 
to cope with the situation without light. Systems such as egress route and anti-panic 
lighting, illuminated signage, or photoluminescent stripes on the floor can support the 
wayfinding. Nevertheless, the installation of visual elements is necessary not only 
when the lighting is extinguished, but also when it is in operation. The integration of 
==PAGE:86==
Results 
 
75 
appropriate equipment is often insufficiently comprehensive. The installation of ceiling-
mounted lamps in a manner that indicates the direction of the hallway and the display 
of information in an appropriate size can assist in orientation within the building. The 
displayed information can be general information about the egress route design or 
systems of the building, emergency and warning signs, or floor numbers.  
 
Further visibility can be achieved through contrast variations. Especially luminance 
contrast (light-dark differences) is needed for vision-impaired people. The differences 
in color or tone supplement these luminance contrasts and can increase the 
detectability for further residents. Safety yellow is supposed to have the best color 
conspicuity, as defined in ISO 3864-1:2011. (Dansk Standard, 2019d) An example can 
be seen in the streets of Japan, where walkways are designed with tactile patterns, 
colored in yellow (UCLA Disabilities and Computing Program, 2014). 
 
Therefore:  
Installing several lighting or contrast elements must be crucial in designing 
spaces within the residential building. Visibility can be supported through 
specific light installations, such as photoluminescent guiding stripes on the 
floor, ceiling-mounted guiding lamps, egress route-, and anti-panic lighting. Size 
and contrast variations must be considered in facilitating the recognition of 
installations, information, and exits.  
 
 
Figure 23 - Diagram for pattern "Visible route" 
 
 
 
==PAGE:87==
Results 
 
76 
5 TANGIBLE ROUTE 
… guidance on the floor and the wall can facilitate people to leave the building. It 
provides directions, offers orientation, and warns of obstacles. Mainly used in public 
places, it supports wayfinding. In this pattern, the use of tactile elements in residential 
buildings is introduced. 
 
The only touch-related element in a residential building is the handrail following 
along the stairs.  
 
In 1967, the Japanese engineer and inventor Seiichi Miyake created Tenji blocks and 
brightly colored patterns in the form of textured lines or circles installed on the ground. 
Both were supposed to help blind and visually impaired people. (UCLA Disabilities and 
Computing Program, 2014) Tenji blocks, also known as tactile paving, are used 
internationally to provide the location and directional information at crosswalks to 
blind pedestrians (Kapsalis et al., 2024). There are two kinds of Tenji blocks, indicating 
caution or giving directional clues. The first type, also known as tactile attention 
patterns, are dotted blocks, cones, or truncated domes. They are placed close to stairs, 
ramps, and elevators or indicate a change of direction. The second type, known as the 
tactile guidance pattern, is designed in the form of flat-topped, elongated, and parallel 
bars. These facilitate following a direction and indicating a safe way of travel. (Dansk 
Standard, 2019d; UCLA Disabilities and Computing Program, 2014) These elements 
have so far been introduced in public outdoor spaces. However, a translation of them 
to 
the 
interior 
might 
be 
considerable. 
The 
building 
of 
the 
Dansk 
Handicaporganisationer implemented tactile patterns already on the ground floor and 
basement level floor. It supports the wayfinding around the atrium and indicates the 
locations of stairs and elevators. Nowadays, residential buildings are designed to have 
wider entrance areas. In such cases, tactile patterns could also help the occupants to 
identify the route. 
 
In addition to the tactile patterns on the floor, guiding elements can also be installed on 
the wall. A very common example of this would be handrails, placed on both sides of 
the stairwells and preferably following on the wall to the apartments. According to 
Danish building regulations, it is required to have one handrail at a height of 
approximately 0,80 meters on both sides of the stairs (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023), 
==PAGE:88==
Results 
 
77 
averaged for people of different sizes. However, for kids, very small or tall adults, this 
height of the handrails may present a challenge. Implementing handrails at more than 
one height could decrease the physical effort used to follow them. This solution can be 
seen in different office buildings, hospitals, or hotels. Yet, it could be further 
implemented in residential buildings given that people in different heights and with 
different abilities live there.  
 
Therefore:  
In the entrance area inside the building, tactile patterns such as guidance and 
attention patterns should be included and placed at the necessary route to the 
stairwells and elevators. Further, two handrails at two different heights on both 
sides of the stairs should be installed to facilitate the moving of all residents no 
matter how tall they are. The handrails should be located at a height of 0,61 
meters for children and small people and 0,915 meters for adults and tall people. 
It must be ensured, that the handrails are grip-friendly and easy to hold on to. 
They are installed without interruptions over landings and terminate 
horizontally. The handrails should be equipped with metal studs to indicate the 
floor number and adjacent corridor junctions. 
 
 
Figure 24 - Diagram for pattern "Tangible route" 
 
6 EGRESS WITHOUT STAIRS 
… “Do not use the lift in the event of fire” – this is a very common sentence everybody 
has likely read at least once in their life. According to current regulations, elevators are 
only allowed to be used by firefighters to get to the source of the fire and/or rescue 
people waiting in safe waiting areas to be carried down. But what if there is a way to 
==PAGE:89==
Results 
 
78 
make use of elevators for residents with mobility impairments in cases of emergency? 
This pattern will introduce this solution, which already has been implemented in non-
residential buildings.  
 
Mobility-impaired people cannot rescue themselves from their apartments in a 
fire emergency. They are dependent on further residents or the firefighters to 
carry them down.  
 
Currently, the building regulations in Denmark state that in the case of a fire 
emergency, only firefighters are allowed to use the firefighting elevator. All other 
elevators are prohibited from being used. It is essential that firefighting elevators are 
not utilized as an escape route, but rather by firefighters to reach the fire's source and 
rescue people who cannot descend the stairs. Further, those elevators must have a 
safe area in front of every landing door which is used for firefighting operations, as 
protection for the lift, and as a waiting space for people who need to be evacuated 
(Dansk Standard, 2020a). However, those firefighting elevators are only mandatory in 
buildings higher than 22 meters above ground (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023). 
Hence, in buildings lower than 22 meters above ground, a normal lift is built which can 
neither be used by the firefighters nor by residents in a case of fire.  
 
Adams & Galea (n.d.) analyzed in their paper An Experimental Evaluation of 
Movement Devices Used to Assist People with Reduced Mobility in High-Rise Building 
Evacuations different assistive technologies to rescue mobility-impaired people fast 
and safely from high-rise buildings. Yet, all their solutions needed support from one to 
four people to carry down the impaired person. Some papers, for example by Proulx 
(2004) Evacuation by elevators: who goes first? or Papaioannou (2006b) Escape of 
people with disabilities from fire, introduce the feasibility or necessity of elevators used 
as an evacuation method.   
 
In 2011, the Comité Européen de Normalisation (CEN) approved the technical 
specification DS/CEN/TS 81-76: Evacuation of disabled persons using lifts. This 
specification introduced elevators usable for egress by people with physical or mental 
impairment who therefore are not able to use the stairs. The technical specification 
assumes that people without disabilities will use the stairs as the primary escape route. 
==PAGE:90==
Results 
 
79 
(Dansk Standard, 2011). DS/CEN/TS 81-76 specifies the requirements, control 
systems, and further necessary elements to allow smooth operation. An implemented 
solution can be seen at the house of the DOPD, where the elevators are placed behind 
a fire-proofed wall including fire-proofed doors, closing in case of fire. The area of the 
elevators can then be accessed via an access balcony on the outside of the building. 
areas and the atrium have additional exit doors which can be opened automatically by 
pushing a button and lead the occupant to the outside and back inside. In the context 
of residential buildings, access balconies are corridors on the outside of the building, 
leading from the apartment to the staircase and elevator (further referred to as access 
balcony) 
 
Therefore:  
Introduce elevators as evacuation devices, enabling people with mobility or 
mental impairment, causing mobility impairments to rescue themselves in a 
case of fire. The elevators should be established according to the given 
standards and strictly reserved for residents and visitors not able to walk 
downstairs. All elevators should be in fire-protected areas and reachable 
through access balconies.  
 
 
Figure 25 - Diagram for pattern "Egress without stairs" 
 
 
==PAGE:91==
Results 
 
80 
5.8 Evaluation Criteria for the Pattern Language 
To assess the quality of the implementability of the rules given by the pattern language, 
evaluation criteria were developed. This was achieved by applying and comparing 
certification systems, including DGNB, LEED, BREEAM, and isUD. The approach 
facilitated the identification of the optimum requirement for each criterion. The Danish 
building regulation BR18 was established as the minimum requirement. The 
requirements of DGNB and BR18 are aligned. It is important to note that LEED and 
BREEAM are primarily focused on energy, which limits their application. Moreover, 
certain BREEAM requirements were below the minimum requirements stated in BR18. 
The solutions proposed by the isUD tool appeared to be more suited for publicly 
accessible buildings. However, due to it being a universal design guideline, it is 
included. Many solutions can be adapted for residential buildings. 
 
Table 7 summarizes the criteria and requirements applicable to each pattern, 
considering the minimum and optimum approaches, and enhancements where no 
additional benefit is identified. The criteria, similar to the pattern language (5.7) and 
solution spaces (5.6), are one possible implementation. The list of criteria and solutions 
can be extended. The comprehensive table can be found in Appendix F, also including 
the specific solution space incorporated in the pattern, the values below the minimum 
requirement, and the values between the minimum and the optimum requirement. The 
optimum requirement is also presented in more detail and values identified in 
regulations and certification systems are referenced.

5.9 Pattern Language Tool 
The pattern language tool was established to assess the performance of a project by 
applying the evaluation criteria of the pattern language (Table 7 (section 5.8)). 
The tool was developed on the theoretical level. The following description and display 
of the tool are intended to illustrate what a real tool could potentially look like and 
contain. The objective of the tool is to facilitate the communication, implementation, 
and usage of universal design solutions between stakeholders.  
 
The tool consists of the following: 
§ An upload section in which a floor plan can be uploaded via drag and drop. It 
is possible to zoom in and navigate on the plan. It should contribute to clarity by 
having only one window open. 
§ The solution description of the pattern. The user can read the written 
description and look at the diagram.   
§  A checklist indicating the fulfillment of the criteria designated to the pattern. 
The kind and amount of values to be entered depends on the pattern. 
Depending on the criteria, multi- and/or single-selection is feasible.  
§ A scale indicates the performance of the project according to the specific 
pattern. The scales are divided into four categories: outside the building 
regulation (blue), within the building regulation but not universal (no color), the 
optimum range for universal design (green), and where no additional benefit 
was identified (grey). The user can simultaneously view the current performance 
(green triangle) of the project and the potential for optimization. 
§ A short evaluation of the performance and a link (pink flame) to open the 
solution spaces in a new window to get inspiration for optimization potentials.  
§ An indication of which requirements in the certification systems are met. The

mentioned multiple times on the scale, it indicates minimum, medium, and 
maximum requirements. They are, as the scale, organized from the left 
(minimum) to the right (maximum).

5.10 Case Study: Application of Pattern Language Tool for 
Residential Buildings 
As introduced in section 4.5, a case study was provided by an industry stakeholder. 
This project is currently under construction in the greater Copenhagen area. The 
building complex consists of three buildings. As illustrated in Figure 28, the complex is 
distributed into three buildings and 13 individual building entrances. The buildings are 
three to five stories and have a maximum top-story floor height of 12 meters. The 
building partially includes a basement. The building is classified in building category 4 
and fire risk class 3. It is designed and constructed according to the Danish building 
regulations BR18. The project information regarding the architect, the owner, and the 
project name are kept anonymous. 
 
 
Figure 28 - Case study: Floor plan of the second floor illustrating the building complex 
==PAGE:98==
Results 
 
87 
The previously introduced pattern language and its evaluation criteria were applied for 
the analysis of the provided and projected building. Using separate cut-outs of the floor 
plans, evaluations are made for each pattern, including a scale for the performance. 
Lastly, already implemented solutions, gaps, and limitations are identified.  
 
The scale indicates the current performance according to the provided plans. A short 
form of the requirement and reference to compliance with BR18 and certification 
systems are illustrated. The evaluation criteria are described in section 5.8 and a 
comprehensive overview can be found in Appendix F. 
 
Pattern 1 – Integrated Alerting System 
This pattern is mainly focusing on the apartment of a building. As introduced in section 
5.7, the proposed solution for this pattern is: 
 
Smoke alerting systems with different alerting signals should be integrated as 
standard in residential buildings. This will ensure the recognition of fire alarms 
no matter if people are affected by a disability or develop an impairment over 
time. Smoke detectors indicating a sound should be further connected to 
devices providing vibration, flashlight, and voice introduction. 
 
Figure 29 shows a floor plan of an apartment within the project. The current integration 
of an alerting system is highlighted with a red circle. The apartment has one smoke 
detector in the corridor that stretches from the living room and kitchen to the apartment 
door. As known from the requirements in BR18, it is an audible alarm device indicating 
a high-frequency tone as soon as smoke is detected in the apartment. The device is 
connected to the electricity supply.  
==PAGE:99==
Results 
 
88 
 
Figure 29 - Floor plan of the case study showing an example apartment and its electrical installations. 
 
The floor plan was analyzed according to the evaluation criteria. The performance for 
criterion 1 variety of alerting systems (Figure 30) as well as criterion 2 amount of alarm 
systems (Figure 31) is presented in the following.  
 
Due to the building being built according to BR18, it fulfills its requirements. The 
apartments are equipped with one type of smoke alarm (criterion 1) and one device in 
total (criterion 2). As the DGNB requirement aligns with BR18, it is also achieved.  
However, the optimum requirement for considering this pattern as a universal design 
has not been met. Universal design can be achieved by installing more than one device 
in the home. In Germany, the installation of multiple smoke detectors is already 
required, as smoke detectors must be installed in all bedrooms and children's rooms, 
as 
well 
as 
in 
hallways 
leading 
to 
the 
exit 
of 
the 
apartment 
(§48, 
MBO,(Bauministerkonferenz, 2022)). In addition to the installation of multiple smoke 
detectors, different alarm signals should be implemented so that people with different 
abilities can hear the alarm in case of a fire emergency.

systems” 
 
Figure 31 - Performance of the first pattern “integrated alerting system” and the second criterion “amount of 
alerting systems” 
 
Pattern 2 – Learned Behavior 
This pattern is independent of the floor plan’s layout. It is universally applicable. As 
introduced in section 5.7, the proposed solution for this pattern is: 
 
At key handover, evacuation practices should be introduced to the tenant. 
Conducted by the landlord, the tenant will gain knowledge about the alerting 
systems and egress route design. Further, it should be possible to refresh the 
knowledge annually. 
 
In general, no introduction to the egress route or fire alerting system is currently done 
at apartment handovers, nor is a description, guideline, or handbook attached to the 
contract or sent to the tenant.   
 
The performance for criterion 1 training (Figure 32) is presented in the following.  
 
Currently, the developer of the building which the case study is based on, is not 
implementing an educational introduction of the egress route design and alerting 
system. To reach the optimum requirement, identified for this pattern to be universally 
designed, the landlord can integrate a one-time introduction into their apartment 
handover procedure. For further improvement, the landlord can prepare a training

Pattern 3 – Enough Space 
This pattern is mainly focusing on the common hallway layout in a building. As 
introduced in 5.7, the proposed solution for this pattern is: 
 
Create safe waiting spaces on each floor. Spaces that are sufficiently spacious 
to accommodate individuals with mobility impairments until the use of the 
elevator is possible or a rescue team can carry them down. Corridors and doors 
should be designed to permit wheelchair users to pass without risk of injury or 
collision. 
 
Figure 33 shows a floor plan cut-out showing two differently designed hallways on the 
second floor of the provided project. The current width of the hallways is 1,31 to 1,60 
meters and stretches from the apartments directly to the stairwells or the elevator. The 
door width is a minimum of 0,91 meters. Additional space, i.e. in the form of “pockets”, 
is not considered.

==PAGE:102==

Figure 33 Floor Plan of the case study showing two examples of hallways in the building complex. 
 
The floor plan was analyzed according to the evaluation criteria. The performances for 
the criterion 1 hallway width (Figure 34), criterion 2 door width (Figure 35), and criterion 
3 safe spaces (Figure 36) are presented in the following.  
 
The building is constructed in accordance with BR18, and thus meets all requirements 
set by BR18. The hallway’s width of 1,31 to 1,60 meters is one to 30 centimeters above 
the minimum required value of BR18 and DGNB. Although the hallway width of 1,60 
meters does meet the criteria set based on universal design principles as drawn out in 
section 5.8, it is only implemented in one building staircase and is not coherent for the 
whole level. Therefore, the scale is set to 1,31 meters which represents the majority of 
the hallways and results in no compliance with universal design. Hallways should have 
a minimum width of 1,40 meters to allow the simultaneous passage of a walking person 
and a wheelchair user. This width permits the passing residents to keep a neutral body 
position and no turning is necessary. The highest achievable valuation would be 
achieved at a maximum width of 1,80 meters which would allow the uninterrupted 
passage of two wheelchair users simultaneously and in two directions. Regarding the 
door width, the case study fulfills the optimum requirement identified for this pattern to 
be a universal design. The width taken here (1,10 meters) is the apartment door 
leading to the common hallway. However, also the doors within the apartment are wide 
==PAGE:103==
Results 
 
92 
enough (0,90 meters) to allow people with assistive technology, such as wheelchairs, 
to pass. Moreover, the floor plans do not contain safe spaces for people waiting for the 
elevator or rescue personnel. Universally designed spaces were defined as spaces or 
“pockets” with an area of a minimum of 1,40 x 1,42 meters up to 1,70 x 1,42 meters. 
This will accommodate one person in a wheelchair and one standing person, 
respectively two wheelchairs.

spaces within the residential building. Visibility can be supported through

Patter 5 – Tangible Route  
This pattern primarily concerns the wayfinding in a common egress route within a 
building, with a particular focus on tactile characteristics. As introduced in section 5.7, 
the proposed solution for this pattern is: 
 
In the entrance area inside the building, tactile patterns such as guidance and 
attention patterns should be included and placed at the necessary route to the 
stairwells and elevators. Further, two handrails at two different heights on both 
sides of the stairs should be installed to facilitate the moving of all residents no 
matter how tall they are. The handrails should be located at a height of 0,61 
meters for children and small people and 0,915 meters for adults and tall people. 
It must be ensured, that the handrails are grip-friendly and easy to hold on to. 
They are installed without interruptions over landings and terminate 
horizontally. The handrails should be equipped with metal studs to indicate the 
floor number and adjacent corridor junctions. 
 
Figure 42 shows an example section of the case study. According to the current 
building regulations, tactile elements on the floor need to be considered outside of the 
building. Inside the building, handrails must be installed on both sides of the stairs and 
at a height of approximately 0,80 meters and should be grip-friendly.  
 
 
Figure 42 – Section of the case study showing an example of two building’s stairwells and entrance areas in the 
building complex. 
==PAGE:107==
Results 
 
96 
The section was analyzed according to the evaluation criteria. The performances for 
criterion 1 tactile pavement (Figure 43), criterion 2 handrails (Figure 44), and criterion 
3 braille (Figure 45) are presented in the following.  
The building meets the minimum BR18 and DGNB requirements for criterion 1 tactile 
pavement. Nonetheless, the optimum requirement identified for this pattern to be 
universal design is not reached. Tactile elements, for instance, guidance and attention 
patterns could be implemented in the wider entrances on the ground floor. The project 
does not meet the minimum requirement of the current BR18 in criterion 2 handrails. 
The provided floor plan indicates that the stairwells are only equipped with one handrail 
on one side of the stairs. Based on the floor plans and dialogue with the project owner 
no relevant information regarding fire safety and the egress route design in Braille is 
displayed in the building. It is not a requirement in the Danish building regulations or 
standard practice.

Pattern 6 – Egress without stairs 
This pattern mainly focuses on the common egress route in a building. In particular, it 
provides an alternative route for mobility-impaired residents. As introduced in section 
5.7, the proposed solution for this pattern is: 
Introduce elevators as evacuation devices, enabling people with mobility or 
mental impairment, causing mobility impairments to rescue themselves in a 
case of fire. The elevators should be established according to the given 
standards and strictly reserved for residents and visitors not able to walk 
downstairs. All elevators should be in fire-protected areas and reachable 
through access balconies.  
 
Figure 46 shows a floor plan with the emergency exits highlighted by green arrows. 
The main egress routes are the stairwells reachable from all apartments. For people 
with mobility impairments, balconies are established as rescue areas. Elevators are 
prohibited to use in a fire emergency. 
 
 
Figure 46 - Floor plan of the case study showing two example buildings and their egress routes.  
 
==PAGE:109==
Results 
 
98 
The floor plan was analyzed according to the evaluation criteria. The performances for 
criterion 1 elevator (Figure 47), criterion 2 gradient (Figure 48), and criterion 3 access 
balcony (Figure 49) are presented in the following.  
 
The building is constructed in accordance with BR18, and thus meets all requirements 
set by BR18. Each staircase within the project has an adjacent elevator to provide 
leveled access for all residents to each floor. The elevator established in the building 
is a type 2 elevator with inner dimensions of 1,10 x 1,40 meters. It has one door, 
meaning no walk-through is possible. Due to this installation, the project also reaches 
the minimum score for DGNB and BREEAM. To achieve the optimum requirement of 
this pattern, the elevator should be established as an escape route for people with 
mobility impairments. Further improvements can be made by increasing the elevator 
car dimensions (type 3: 1,10 x 2,10 meters, type 4: 1,60 x 1,40 meters, or type 5: 2,00 
x 1,40 meters). Plans regarding the door, details visible in the plans indicate that neither 
the exit out of the building or apartment nor the exits to the balconies or terraces are 
without thresholds. The lowest threshold was from the apartment to the hallway with 
approximately 1 centimeter. The exit of the building to the outside has a threshold of 2 
centimeters, and the exit from the apartment to the balconies and terraces is 2,5 
centimeters. Considering that the balcony is supposed to be the rescue area for 
wheelchair users, no threshold should exist. Figure 49 shows no score for the provided 
project due to the non-existence of access balconies. The values for the widths of them 
align with those from the hallway in pattern 3, criteria 1 (Figure 34). In summary, the 
projected building fulfills the minimum requirement set by BR18 and the DGNB with 
regard to pattern 6.

5.11 Validation of Pattern Language and Solution Spaces 
To validate the applicability of the pattern language to the case study and the 
developed solution spaces, a second interview series was conducted with the same 
industry experts (experts) from the first interview series. The interviews aimed to catch 
their opinions and suggestions.  
 
Moreover, an online survey as described in section 4.6 was conducted to evaluate the 
solution spaces. The survey was distributed to architects, project developers, 
consultants, and other industry stakeholders. 
 
Expert Interviews 
The developed pattern language was used to evaluate a currently developed building 
according to universal design principles (section 5.10). For validation of the results, the 
outcome was presented to the same experts from the first interview series, whereby 
the interview partner from AKF changed from Christian Gjessing Bruun to Anna Mohr 
who is a project developer. The prepared PowerPoint presentation shortly introduced 
the derived taxonomy, solution spaces, and pattern language. Following the 
introduction, the application of the pattern language and its evaluation criteria to the 
case study were shown and discussed. Each pattern was individually presented 
including the status quo of the project (based on provided floor plans) and the 
D
No extra
benefit
Case study

Universal

1,80 m

No extra

Outside building
