==PAGE:111==
Results 
 
100 
suggestions derived from the pattern language. The two interviews with Victoria 
Chenais (Henning Larsen) and Emma Nielsen (Carlberg) were conducted via teams. 
The interview with V. Chenais took place on May 14th, 2024, and with E. Nielsen on 
May 16th, 2024. The third interview with Anna Mohr (AKF) was conducted via a 
questionnaire that followed the same structure and questions as the interviews. The 
following paragraphs present the findings. The presentation, transcripts, and 
responses to the questionnaire can be found in Appendix G.  
 
General Feedback  
The overall feedback of the three experts was positive. The pattern language and tool 
are seen as a very integrated solution that can create dialogue, knowledge, and 
awareness, as well as help to challenge the building code. The simple and visual 
structure is easy to understand and supports the simple application to projects in the 
design and program phases.   
 
Additionally, several valuable suggestions and limitations were identified, which should 
be taken into account in future steps. Implementing the solutions, the right balance 
should be found. First, installing too many different devices within an apartment can 
“make your disability very visible” (E. Nielsen) and make people feel like they live in a 
care home. It should be prepared for the installation and implemented on request 
rather than implementing everything up front. However, in the main areas of the 
building, “it makes sense that everything is preinstalled” (E. Nielsen), installations 
should be provided from the beginning as it is unknown which person will move through 
the corridors. Second, “the tricky part will be to merge and have both interests met, 
between following the regulation and going beyond the regulation in a way to make it 
pleasant and livable for the people with impairments and both something aesthetically 
pleasant. Because this is what architects look at” (V. Chenais). She further explained, 
that especially with contrasting elements in terms of colors or texture, it could look 
unpleasant. Yet, it was still seen as necessary because contrast is “the visible” to 
people with visual impairments.  
 
Responsibility for implementing the pattern language 
The majority of the patterns were identified as the responsibility of the architects due 
to them creating the first draft and already considering some of the introduced 
==PAGE:112==
Results 
 
101 
solutions. These were related to the interior design, such as signage, lighting, tactility, 
and spaces. Additional responsibilities are given to fire engineers and electricians to 
ensure that the system is set up for different and interconnected alerting systems. (Fire) 
Engineers further should be included in implementing the egress without stairs, the 
tangible and visible route. A. Mohr from AKF could see the responsibility for the second 
pattern with the project developer/owner, “as [they] hand over the keys. [They] have a 
procedure of what to inform when [they] deliver the keys, plus a handbook with 
information. Here could information about the egress route and alerting system be 
implemented, plus [the] extra service of a different system if [a] hearing impairment” 
exists. She further emphasized that “it is important to make clear that right now you do 
no more than what the requirement says. DGNB has some universal design points, but 
not regarding this”. In general, it was mentioned by E. Nielsen that some of the patterns 
could be beneficial for the development of building programs. They could be utilized to 
identify its sophistication. For instance, if they “need to do something that is beyond 
the building regulations because [they] want extra space or extra safety”. Therefore, 
an early implementation of the pattern into the process and across stakeholders would 
be beneficial. 
 
Tool Application 
The usability and use of the tool were seen as very supportive for people who are 
involved in a project but may not know the building regulations of the country or the 
project itself that well. The tool can indicate the importance of specific requirements 
and create awareness about the needs of people with disabilities. Furthermore, it 
allows for the argument and implementation of criteria to go beyond the building 
regulations.  
 
Additionally, the interviewees suggested some improvements to be implemented in the 
tool. The tool should include a “simple description” (E. Nielsen) of the pattern which is 
supported by “a little call back on one of these sketches every time” (V. Chenais) or 
“adding icons because when you skim something and have an icon, that always helps.” 
(E. Nielsen). Further, it was suggested to “highlight even more what is the optimal zone” 
(V. Chenais) to ensure the visibility of where the project should be. A last suggestion 
was that “it could have more or less the same setup as DGNB, [to use] a use point 
matrix” (A. Mohr). 
==PAGE:113==
Results 
 
102 
In conclusion, the tool should have a simple design and language to ensure usability 
for every project participant. Adding some icons or sketches of the six patterns will 
facilitate the understanding of the solutions. Further, a point matrix could be 
implemented.  
Two of three experts would be interested in implementing the tool into their projects. 
However, one expert hesitated regarding its extent. Still, if an incentive such as a 
certificate would be implemented, it could be of interest.  
 
Lastly, the experts were asked about the person responsible for the tool. It was 
mentioned that the client, contractor, and/or architect together with a client advisor 
could be in charge of it. If it is implemented in the DGNB system under the criteria of 
universal design, the DGNB auditor would be in charge.  
 
Validation Survey 
The validation survey was distributed to architects, project developers, and 
consultants. In total 11 people responded. The survey was conducted anonymously.  
 
Figure 50 shows the distribution of people according to their profession, years of 
experience, and country they are working in.  
       
    
   
 
Figure 50 - Survey Participant's Information: Profession, Experience, and the Country of their workplace. In total 
seven people from Denmark, Germany, and Iceland responded to the survey. 
 
The survey was completed by six architects, two consultants, and in each case one 
project developer, corporate real estate manager, and project manager. The 
participants' experience ranged from 0-2 years (1 response) to > 10 years (3 
responses). Moreover, the participants are employed in Denmark, Germany, and 
Iceland.  
 
==PAGE:114==
Results 
 
103 
The following four figures present the results of the survey. The participants were asked 
to respond to each question regarding the probability of implementing the solution 
spaces. The response could be: yes, with further improvements, and no. Costs were 
not considered. In addition, a field for comments was available to enter thoughts, 
suggestions, or concerns. It should be noted that not all comments are included in this 
chapter; only those deemed relevant are presented. Further comments are integrated 
into the discussion. The complete survey results can be found in Appendix G.  The 
figures will be distributed in the three groups egress route design, alerting system, and 
people’s behavior. The solution spaces are mentioned with their ID-no. previously 
introduced in Table 6 in section 5.6. 
 
Egress Route Design 
As shown in Figure 51 and Figure 52 the participants can imagine implementing the 
majority of the solution spaces. Three out of 22 solutions, E2, E9, and E15, were 
answered with no from the majority of participants.  
 
The installation of two handrails on both sides (E2) resulted in a fairly even distribution 
of participants. Five participants answered with yes, and six with no. Comments to this 
solution space were: “Not particularly for fire safety. I would install two handrails at 
different heights depending on the target group and users’ ability.” And “Handrail on 
both sides of the stairs, Double handrail (one above the other) not absolutely 
necessary (85cm also works very well for children) [translated from German]”.  
 
The result for the design of door frames with more contrast (E9) displayed a very 
distinct distribution of responses. Two participants would consider implementing this 
solution space while eight participants would not. No comments were left under this 
solution.  
 
Similar results are seen for the implementation of rectangular ceiling lamps (E15). Two 
participants answered yes and seven no. The solution space was not defined with 
sufficient precision, particularly with regard to the comments that concentrated on the 
question rather than the evaluation of the solution: “Why is rectangular better?”, and 
“might be depending on building design – lighting should be sufficiently bright 
[translated from German]”.

People’s Behavior 
In Figure 54, the results for the solution spaces concerning people’s behavior are 
presented. The majority of the survey participants would implement both solution 
spaces. In the case of the one-time introduction to the evacuation procedures and 
alerting systems at key handover (P1), all participants agreed on its implementation. 
Furthermore, it was proposed that a one-pager can be provided as an emergency 
manual, along with the keys. Regarding an annual opportunity to participate in 
evacuation training organized by the landlord (P2), the responses diverge to a greater 
extent.  Six participants would implement the solution. It is seen as “relevant for more 
than just rented properties. For example, it could be offered to private apartment 
owners through owner associations”. Four participants would not implement the annual 
training, and one participant suggested offering more than one date with an incentive 
for participation.

Tool 
Finally, the participants were asked to identify the person in charge of a tool indicating 
and checking the implementation of universal design solutions regarding fire safety. 
The tool could combine the previously introduced taxonomy and pattern language 
tools.  The participants did not agree on a specific profession to which the responsibility 
should be allocated. Some of the respondents indicated that architects should be held 
responsible. This could involve either the architect alone (three votes) or in 
collaboration with an engineer or quality control consultant (six votes). Additionally, two 
participants proposed the involvement of a consultant to ensure compliance.

==PAGE:118==
Discussion 
 
107 
6. Discussion 
This chapter examines critical topics including regulations and certification systems, 
existing solutions, taxonomy, pattern language, and validation. The regulatory 
frameworks and certification requirements are analyzed, and the efficacy of current 
solutions is assessed. Moreover, the comprehensiveness of the taxonomy and 
application of the pattern language is evaluated.  
 
Finding 1: Industry lacks awareness for people with disabilities 
The Disabled People’s Organizations Denmark (DOPD) has chosen a unique path to 
select the right stakeholders to make sure their house is designed and built universally. 
All stakeholders in the design and construction process had to conduct and pass a 
course organized by the DOPD and the Danish Building Research Institute, before 
entering the competition phase to design and calculate the costs of the building. The 
course focused on personally experiencing the limitations that people with disabilities 
face in the built environment. The course generated awareness of understanding the 
importance of accessibility as architectural premises and the user’s perspective. 
(Dansk Handicaporganisationer, 2019) 
 
The described selection process is far from the normality experienced in the industry, 
where missing awareness for people with disabilities is continuously being observed. 
In a panel discussion on the topic of Art Beyond Barriers, it was mentioned that a 
problem is not there if it is not seen. Also, in one of the interviews, V. Chenais supported 
that “we should all spend a day being blind or deaf or in a wheelchair or just with 
crutches”. Further, Chris Downey, who worked as an architect before and after losing 
his sight reflected in a keynote at the World Congress of Architects in 2023, that he 
has now a different perception of the built environment and that architecture should go 
beyond what can be seen (Downey, 2023).  
 
This illustrates the necessity for those involved in the design and construction of the 
building to enhance their awareness of people with disabilities who are unable to 
experience the built environment in the same manner as those without disabilities. 
Time must be allocated at the beginning of a project to gain an understanding of the 
limitations and opportunities universal design presents. Once completed, the 
==PAGE:119==
Discussion 
 
108 
knowledge can be implemented in future projects and shared. This will ensure equal 
consideration of all people.  
 
The expert interviews also revealed a lack of common knowledge and understanding 
of universal design within the building industry. Each of the three experts provided 
different definitions, with one limiting universal design to accessibility. In reality, 
accessibility is just one aspect of universal design. Accessibility and usability are the 
foundation of it (Interaction Design Foundation, 2016), with a focus on performance 
and addressing issues for people of all ability levels. (Steinfeld & Maisel, 2012).  
 
Finding 2: Current Regulations and Certification Systems for Building and Fire Safety 
lack Universal Design Criteria 
Buildings are constructed according to current standards set by the International 
Organization for Standardization (ISO) or European norm (EN), as well as national 
regulations, such as the Danish building code BR18. Standards are defined to ensure 
a common understanding of building practices across countries, however, they first 
become mandatory when included in the national building regulations.  
 
The gap analysis (section 5.1) revealed that the requirements regarding access and 
fire safety in the Danish building regulations BR18 and certification systems (DGNB, 
LEED, BREEAM) seem to be well intended and supposed to include the majority of 
people, however, the focus is mainly on 1) the accessibility of wheelchair users, 2) 
outdoor areas, or 3) non-residential buildings. The requirements for residential 
buildings include minimal fire safety regulations, with hardly any standards established 
for alerting systems or egress route design. For instance, a single smoke detector in 
an apartment and the provision of escape route stairs and rescue areas on balconies 
are seen as sufficient while findings revealed in section 5.8 that in both cases optimum 
requirements can go further. A comparable situation can be observed with regard to 
the certification systems. As an illustration, BREEAM introduced the criteria Inclusive 
and Accessible Design in their latest version, 6.0, in 2021. However, the detailed 
requirements exclusively target wheelchair users or people with walking impairments. 
The certification system LEED did not have criteria in their applicable version v4.1.  
However, they introduced a pilot credit Inclusive design which includes more universal 
design requirements, also concerning fire safety. These include, but are not limited to, 
==PAGE:120==
Discussion 
 
109 
emergency alarms with different alerting signals (audible and visual), safety zones, and 
clear wayfinding to ensure that emergency egress routes are inclusive (USGBC, n.d.). 
DGNB, the third certification system assessed in the gap analysis, included Universal 
Design criteria encompassing a variety of requirements aimed at optimizing buildings 
for all occupants. The minimum standards also align with the Danish building 
regulations BR18. Consequently, while this criterion is a decisive factor, meaning 
certification is withheld if not met, compliance can be achieved simply by adhering to 
these regulations, which have been identified as insufficient.  
 
Certification systems play a crucial role in improving buildings and raising awareness 
about various aspects of building design, although they are often voluntary. Companies 
have the flexibility to choose the certification system they consider most suitable. 
Moreover, not all criteria must be met to achieve certification, allowing buildings to 
compensate for deficiencies in some areas by excelling in others. To enhance the 
effectiveness of these systems, a combination of stricter regulations and innovative 
approaches is necessary. Additionally, building programs should include mandatory 
requirements to ensure buildings are more usable and accessible. This would 
guarantee equal opportunities for residents with disabilities, those without disabilities, 
and those who may develop disabilities over time. 
 
 Fire safety considers people’s lives and a more universal design can ensure equal 
chances for people to be alerted and be able to rescue themselves without help, yet, 
“architectural qualities of a building or fitting the program will always prevail on the 
quality of an egress route” (V. Chenais, 22.04.2024). It appears that addressing this 
topic holds less priority in today’s industry. Designing buildings for minorities, without 
a certain assurance that these people will actually reside, is often considered 
impractical. However, this mindset within the industry results in inadequate buildings 
for people with disabilities, as they are more likely to choose not to move in due to the 
poor design of these structures. 
 
Nevertheless, change is challenging to implement, and if it is not compelled by 
legislation, it is unlikely to occur. The implementation of change is perceived as a costly 
and time-consuming process, and due to it being voluntary, it is not considered a 
priority. Architects and constructors must allocate time from the initiation to the 
==PAGE:121==
Discussion 
 
110 
finalization of a project to engage with the topic of universal design and exchange with 
pertinent experts. However, a reevaluation of current building regulations is necessary 
to challenge and enhance existing standards. Establishing minimum standards is 
crucial to ensure that all people, regardless of disability status, have equal chances to 
evacuate in fire emergencies. 
 
Finding 3: Best practice shows Feasibility  
As described in Finding 1, the DOPD improved the process of selecting the right 
stakeholder for the design of their building which can be adapted into the design 
process of residential buildings. Further, the design outcome in the form of several 
universal design solutions presented in section 5.2 proved to be adaptable for 
residential buildings. The clear structures with illuminance and color contrasts, tactility 
on the floor and walls, as well as different alerting systems are incorporated in the 
solution spaces (section 5.6) and pattern language (section 5.7). For instance, the 
tactile pavement in the atrium of the DOPD house aligns with the introduced solution 
spaces of tactile guidance and attention patterns on the floor in wider entrance areas. 
The observed luminance and color contrast in the design of walls and door frames are 
incorporated into the solution spaces for the level contrast. The successful 
implementation of elevators as egress routes for people with mobility impairment in the 
DOPD house could substantiate the proposed solution space of introducing the same 
in residential buildings. Lastly, the different alerting systems provided by DOPD, such 
as sound and light used as signaling, and played instructions to leave the building were 
incorporated into the solution spaces.  
 
Furthermore, the Dansk Handicaporganisationer (2019) proved in fire drills that 300 
employees, including 60 wheelchair users, can be evacuated in six minutes. It is 
assumed that implementing further alarm devices and integrating elevators as egress 
routes for wheelchair users and mobility-impaired people in residential buildings, could 
guarantee similar fast evacuation in case of fire.  Especially the need for further alerting 
systems became evident in evaluating the questionnaires of people affected by 
disabilities (see section 5.2). The hearing-impaired people claimed to not have deaf-
friendly alarm signals in the building and that they had to install their own devices to 
be able to recognize an alarm. The landlord could be obligated legally to provide equal 
alerting systems, pre-installed or easy to retrofit on request.  
==PAGE:122==
Discussion 
 
111 
Finding 4: Framework in the form of the Taxonomy allows for comprehensive 
Classification 
The goal of the taxonomy was to characterize buildings according to attributes and 
levels that support universal design and to share a common definition.  
 
The attributes identified for characterization are comprehensive and encompass the 
diverse needs of people with varying abilities. By integrating tactile, visual, and audible 
elements into the design, the building can be utilized by all residents in a more 
multisensory manner. Furthermore, the behavior of the residents is also taken into 
account by making preparatory training in the taxonomy mandatory. In addition to their 
comprehensiveness, the attributes are flexible and can be adapted to all particularities 
worldwide. The taxonomy does not impose any national regulations. The attributes and 
levels are designed to be general, ensuring their applicability across the world.  
 
The literature review, gap analysis, empirical research, and expert interviews are 
incorporated into the development of the taxonomy for fire safety in residential 
buildings. Limitations and opportunities identified in each method were integrated into 
the attributes and level. If applied, it can share a common definition of characteristics 
concerning the egress route, alerting system, and people’s behavior. The 
comprehensive implementation can value a building as universal.  
 
To simplify the application of the taxonomy, a tool based on the EU Taxonomy 
Compass was developed. Yet, this tool was only developed theoretically, and the 
efficacy could not be demonstrated. To convert the outline into a web tool, additional 
resources must be allocated. Nonetheless, the use of the EU Taxonomy Compass as 
a template proved to be a beneficial approach.  
 
The taxonomy tool is divided into the three groups egress route design, alerting 
system, and people’s behavior, as introduced in section 5.5, to generate a clear 
overview and focus. The structure of the tool is designed to facilitate rapid identification 
of existing requirements. To execute the taxonomy, solution spaces according to the 
three groups were identified and defined. These solution spaces were shaped by 
findings from the literature review, gap analysis, empirical research, and expert 
interviews. Those diverse methods provided a comprehensive understanding of 
==PAGE:123==
Discussion 
 
112 
various perspectives, ranging from industry insights to the experiences of those directly 
affected. The objective was to develop solution spaces that are beneficial and practical 
for a diverse population, rather than for a limited group of people. Given that all solution 
spaces can be verified by standards, certification systems, and regulations, or were 
requested by people with disabilities, this objective was met. Moreover, to 
accommodate individuality and personalization, each taxonomy requirement typically 
offers multiple solution spaces. These solution spaces should be considered versatile 
design options that can be customized to various locations and applications. 
 
To verify the use of the solution spaces and taxonomy in the context of universal 
design, the solution spaces were compared to the seven principles developed by 
Mace, Connel, et al. (1997). The solution spaces were individually collated to the 
guidelines of the principles to identify their compliance with one or more of them 
(section 5.6). Although there are some critics of the application of the principles, such 
as the fit with the needs in the field, the fit with context, the difficulty for benchmarking, 
and their translation into other languages (Steinfeld & Maisel, 2012), the use in this 
context was suitable. Compliance with the eight goals developed by Steinfeld & Maisel 
(2012) (body fit, comfort, awareness, understanding, wellness, social integration, 
personalization, and cultural appropriateness), with regard to the criticism, was taken 
into account.  
 
Finding 5: Pattern Language introduces Design Rules to be followed by the Industry 
The goal of the pattern language was to develop design rules that can be followed to 
create more universally designed buildings regarding fire safety. The pattern language 
is a versatile tool that follows one standardized language and can be adjusted to meet 
specific needs and suit different locations. Its general description allows for broad 
applicability, ensuring flexibility in its implementation. 
 
The taxonomy and solution spaces were utilized as the foundation for the development 
of the six patterns, which were created by combining different attributes and levels from 
the taxonomy and integrating the solution spaces.  Some attributes are associated with 
multiple patterns, indicating the comprehensive applicability of the taxonomy and, 
consequently, the pattern language. In this regard, the use of the original pattern 
language by Christopher Alexander as a template proved to be highly beneficial. The 
==PAGE:124==
Discussion 
 
113 
structure permitted a precise presentation of the problem, with a direct link to a solution. 
In addition, the sketches serve to illustrate potential implementations of solution 
spaces.  
 
To evaluate the patterns, the certification systems DGNB, LEED, BREEAM, and isUD 
were employed. With the available values, a scale was developed to indicate the extent 
to which an implementation is good and universally applicable. For each pattern, 
criteria were defined with minimum and optimum requirements, whereby the optimum 
equals universal design. Besides, values beyond the optimum (no benefit, see section 
5.8) were introduced to demonstrate further improvements which, however, have 
limited benefit for fire safety in residential buildings. The certification systems provided 
a comprehensive overview of existing requirements which were incorporated in the 
evaluation criteria. Yet, certification systems were also lacking requirements regarding 
universal design which became evident as some evaluation criteria could not be 
supported by them.  
 
The utilization of BR18 as a baseline was suitable to the context of having a Danish 
case study. However, for the evaluation criteria of the pattern language, a requirement 
set by BR18 could not always be identified (see section 5.8). The pattern language 
was developed to not exclusively be applied to Denmark. The adjustment to different 
locations is set as an objective. Regarding this, the application in other countries 
should be straightforward and only require minor adjustments. LEED and BREEAM 
are universally applicable across the globe; rather, they can be adopted without 
adjustment. The DGNB is, to some extent, contingent upon the country in which it is 
employed. To accommodate these modifications in the pattern language, additional 
effort may be required. The application of the isUD is not country-specific; however, 
the values provided are principally based on US data. Therefore, the pattern language 
can be applied outside of Denmark. If national requirements are adjusted, it can share 
knowledge and contribute to more universally designed residential buildings regarding 
fire safety. 
 
The upper end of the evaluation criteria, indicated by “no benefit”, was included to 
illustrate that further enhancements may be possible, but that they do not lead to 
greater value for the building. Thus, they are negligible concerning universal design in 
==PAGE:125==
Discussion 
 
114 
residential buildings. Furthermore, certain solutions underscore shortcomings in BR18, 
where only the universal design scale can be applied. There is a need to reassess and 
question building regulations to enhance the adoption of more universally designed 
buildings, particularly concerning fire safety. Currently, the industry complies with the 
regulations without surpassing them. The pattern language serves as a starting point 
for integrating more universal solutions and potentially adjusting building regulations. 
  
For the application of the pattern language, a tool was developed, and a potential 
layout was proposed in section 5.9. To confirm the feasibility, further resources must 
be allocated.  
 
Finding 6: The Case Study revealed that the Design of the Building did not follow a 
Universal Design Approach  
The pattern language tool was applied to the case study provided to generate an 
overall overview of its performance according to the individual evaluation criteria of the 
pattern. As presented in 5.10, the building plans were evaluated according to the 
evaluation criteria (section 5.9). It became evident that the plans are following the 
BR18 requirement, as they set the minimum standards in Denmark. However, these 
are insufficient for universal design regarding access and fire safety in residential 
buildings (see finding 2).  
 
The pattern language tool facilitated the identification of gaps with relative ease and 
speed. The tool simultaneously presents the performance of the project, the criteria, 
and the optimum requirement that needs to be achieved to define the implementation 
of the pattern as a universal design. This allows for the development of solutions while 
analyzing the current state. In total, the case study met two optimum requirements in 
the third pattern enough space for the third criteria door width, and in the fourth pattern 
visible route for the fourth criteria signage (section 5.10). However, the potential to 
implement further criteria was recognized. For instance, further alerting systems, color 
and illuminance contrast on the floor and wall, and tactile guidance elements can be 
retrofitted in the now-existing building. Moreover, the preparatory training can be 
included at any time. This would increase the equal chance for people with or without 
a disability to use and access the apartments.  
 
==PAGE:126==
Discussion 
 
115 
In general, the defined scales for the pattern language tool were suitable for the 
application and could demonstrate the already implemented universal design 
approaches and where the building is lacking in terms of more equal fire safety 
planning. 
 
Finding 7: Feedback from the Industry shows the Feasibility of Solution Spaces and 
Pattern Language Tool 
To validate the feasibility and effectiveness of the proposed solution spaces and 
pattern language, feedback was gathered from industry stakeholders. This was 
achieved through an online survey assessing the potential implementation of the 
solutions, as well as by presenting the pattern language applied to the case study to 
industry experts (see section 5.11). 
 
The survey results represent a broad perspective of industry stakeholders with varying 
years of experience and profession. However, due to the limited number of responses, 
the significance is limited.  
 
In general, the feedback regarding the proposed solutions was positive, with a majority 
of participants indicating that they would implement most of the solution spaces. It is 
presumed that some solution spaces would have been evaluated more positively with 
the provision of additional information in the survey. The brief description of them may 
have resulted in the loss of key messages. A further explanation might have been 
beneficial to underline the importance of each solution space for fire safety in 
residential buildings. This can be derived by some solutions being seen as unrelated 
to fire safety, for instance, the implementation of two handrails. Further, it was 
suggested to “install two handrails at different heights depending on the target group 
and users’ ability”, indicating that it is not deemed a necessary feature in all residential 
buildings. Yet, elderly residents and visually impaired people rely on handrails when 
walking down the stairs (Maki et al., 1998; Zhang, n.d.). They give support and 
guidance, respectively. Especially in a fire emergency, the handrails facilitate 
wayfinding by guiding the resident from the apartment to the ground floor and outside. 
Moreover, they can ensure that less force has to be exerted because of a more neutral 
body position. The same was observed with the solution space braille. It is deemed 
relevant “if the project has high requirements regarding handicapped accessibility, if 
==PAGE:127==
Discussion 
 
116 
not [it] should not be prioritized for fire safety”. While it may not be relevant for a fire 
emergency, it is, however, important to ensure that all residents are equally informed 
about fire safety regulations. 
 
One participant introduced the solution, already implemented in Germany, of placing 
apartments on the ground floor for mobility-impaired people. However, those 
apartments are not seen as a universal design solution. Although it allows fast egress, 
it effectively excludes people who are unable to navigate stairs from moving into 
apartments on a higher floor. Furthermore, it's crucial to acknowledge that not all 
people prefer to live on the ground floor. Therefore, providing alternatives to ground-
floor living is essential. Additionally, considering that disabilities can emerge over time, 
people should not be required to relocate simply because they cannot independently 
exit their apartment during a fire emergency anymore. 
 
In general, the positive feedback to the solution spaces could indicate the awareness 
of people with different abilities and disabilities and may result in the rethinking of some 
building standards regarding fire safety in residential buildings.  
 
The feedback from industry experts on the pattern language tool during the interviews 
was generally positive. The tool's utility for projects involving people unfamiliar with 
country-specific regulations or the specific building was particularly emphasized. 
According to E. Nielsen (16.05.2024), the tool can be shown to stakeholders “for [them] 
to gain knowledge and something [they] can use when looking at what are the needs, 
we need to be aware of and how can we make sure that we have this”. V. Chenais 
(14.05.2024) sees the pattern language tool “as a very integrated solution that is not 
just ticking the boxes of following the requirements, but more like how can we make 
this become an added value”. Further, she emphasized that if it is communicated well 
to the client and other stakeholders, it can be “kind of a pilot project but then it can 
become the flagship project”. However, “a way to make it work is to make it mandatory 
and make sure that it is really well updated with new requirements and the new norms” 
(V. Chenais, 14.05.2024). 
 
Conducting this evaluation with different industry stakeholders and experts was 
beneficial in gaining insightful feedback from different perspectives. Reflecting on the 
==PAGE:128==
Discussion 
 
117 
positive feedback and suggestions, it can be conceived that the implementation of the 
pattern language tool is a viable proposition for the industry as a whole. Nevertheless, 
it may be necessary to provide an incentive, such as a certificate as proposed by A. 
Mohr. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:129==
Conclusion 
 
118 
7. Conclusion 
In summary, shortcomings in the implementation of universal design concerning fire 
safety in residential buildings were identified. The shortcomings are due to the deeply 
rooted and limited openness of the building industry for change, and the failure to 
engage with cutting-edge research or the users themselves. Moreover, a knowledge 
gap and missing common understanding were recognized. 
 
Both hypotheses, presented in Chapter 1, were validated. The first hypothesis which 
states that “the current architectural design methodology lacks comprehensive 
universal design considerations, which impede both egress from the building and the 
ability to recognize the alarm in fire emergencies” was verified by the findings. Applying 
the pattern language tool to the case study indicates gaps in the implementation of 
universal design in current practices. Only two of the evaluated criteria fulfilled the 
requirement to be accounted as universal design. Simultaneously, it illustrated the 
potential for future improvements in the other 14 evaluated criteria. For their 
implementation, industry stakeholders can seek inspiration in the connected solution 
spaces (see section 5.6). As the solution spaces are primarily based on existing 
standards, best practices, or certification systems, their implementation is 
uncomplicated and most of them can be retrofitted.  
 
Both, the performance of the case study and feedback from the experts in the first 
interviews showcased that the industry does not exceed the requirements of regulatory 
and certifying bodies. In addition to a needed adaption of the building regulations, the 
industry stakeholders have to become more aware of people with different disabilities. 
It is frequently regarded as irrelevant, as these people do not move-in anyway. The 
denial of the necessity for accessible buildings results in inaccessible buildings 
(Grangaard, 2018) and misleading knowledge sharing.  
 
Incorporating pattern language and solution spaces in both new developments and 
existing buildings can facilitate a swift transition of residential structures toward more 
universal fire safety design. The positive feedback received for the pattern language 
and solution spaces is seen as a first step toward the implementation of more universal 
design considerations in the design methodology.  
==PAGE:130==
Conclusion 
 
119 
The second hypothesis which states that “the current building regulations, standards, 
and certification systems lack a holistic approach to the integration of universal design 
considerations regarding fire safety” was also confirmed by the findings.  
 
BR18 emphasizes that buildings must be sufficiently safe for residents in a fire 
emergency and that the ability of a resident must be considered in the design of escape 
routes (Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023), yet the minimum requirements incorporated 
do not include all residents with different disabilities. It is crucial to acknowledge that 
to effect change, the building regulations must be the first to become more precise 
regarding universal design in fire safety. The developed taxonomy and pattern 
language (sections 5.4 and 5.7) can provide a baseline for the industry to challenge 
and adjust requirements.  
 
The gap analysis and development of the evaluation criteria for the pattern language 
have particularly pointed out where requirements are deficient or missing, especially 
in residential buildings. The universal criteria, that were introduced in BR18 and the 
certification systems, did not take into account fire safety considerations in residential 
buildings. Moreover, they were oriented toward the external areas, non-residential 
buildings, and wheelchair users. The latter is exemplified by the barrier-free design 
introduced in Japan in the 1970s, which aimed to create an accessible built 
environment eliminated from obstacles for wheelchair users because it was the most 
visible disability. (Kose, 1998) It is reasonable to assume that the requirements 
presented by the industry would have been evolving over the past 50 years. However, 
the current practice, based on the requirements set in building regulations, standards, 
and certification systems, has proven to diverge from the initial assumption.  
 
European and national standards, best practices, and already existing solutions can 
facilitate the implementation of new regulations. For instance, introducing elevators as 
an escape route was presented in 2011 with the standard DS/CEN/TS 87-76:2011 
Evacuation of disabled persons using lifts. Further, private companies, such as 
Hörgeschädigten Technik B&K GmbH in Germany (see 5.7, pattern 1) developed 
alerting devices for people with hearing impairments. 
 
 
==PAGE:131==
Limitations 
 
120 
8. Limitations 
This chapter outlines the limitations encountered during the research, highlighting the 
constraints and challenges that may impact the findings and their generalizability. 
 
The development of the taxonomy, solution spaces, and pattern language, as well as 
the tools, followed a theoretical approach. While the taxonomy followed the EU 
Taxonomy Compass, the pattern language tool was developed from the ground up. 
The efficacy and feasibility of both could not be verified. The solution spaces were 
derived from standards, certification systems, or real-world implementations. However, 
the extent to which these benefit the residents remains uncertain.  
 
The validation revealed the acceptance of the pattern language and solution spaces. 
Nonetheless, the significance of the feedback from the three experts and 11 survey 
participants is restricted. Further evaluations are required to determine their 
implementability.  
 
In the context of the gap analysis, the criteria of the isUD certification were selected as 
target performance. The certification's focus on public buildings rather than residential 
buildings resulted in limited comparability of certain criteria.  
 
The development and validation processes did not consider the associated costs. A 
cost-benefit analysis would be necessary to determine the value and prioritize patterns 
and solution spaces. This could serve to enhance the decision-making basis for the 
industry, given its primary focus is on costs.  
 
==PAGE:132==
Future Implications 
 
121 
9. Future Implications 
In order to determine the efficacy and feasibility of the taxonomy and pattern language, 
as well as their tools, further validations must be conducted. To validate the viability of 
the solution spaces, further industry stakeholders must be consulted. Further 
interviews or surveys with people with disabilities are necessary to ascertain the extent 
to which the solution spaces can benefit them. 
 
A cost-benefit analysis can be incorporated to support the significance of the patterns 
and solution space. By comparing the gained benefit of a solution to the costs of its 
implementation, the worth of a solution will be determined. This incorporation will allow 
for the validation and verification, especially of the solution spaces.  
 
The theoretically developed tools should be transferred into actual software, including 
the cost-benefits, to ensure the practicability for the industry. This will facilitate the 
identification of optimal combinations of patterns and solutions concerning costs for 
the stakeholders and benefits for the residents.  
 
The pattern language tool can be integrated into new developments as a pilot project. 
The incorporation of patterns and solutions in a stepwise manner allows for the 
development of combinations and, subsequently, field-proven standards. 
 
These empirically resulting and ﬁeld-proven standards can ﬁnally be utilized to 
challenge and adjust the current building regulations and certification system 
concerning fire safety in favor of people with different disabilities and abilities. 
 
Lastly, the findings are not limited to the use-case of “fire safety”. Their development 
toward general principles and standards can enhance the development of more 
universally designed residential buildings.  
 
(FlameStop, n.d.) (Valeria Ziavras, 2021) (Dansk Standard, 2010) (Dansk Standard, 
2013) (Dansk Standard, 2019b) (Gesetzliche Unfallversicherung, 2016) (Dansk 
Standard, 2019c) (Dansk Standard, 2020b) (Dansk Standard, 2019a) (Schmidt & 
Buser, n.d.)

Category of use and risk classification: §84
On determination of the necessary fire protection required in order to 
comply with the requirements in Chapter 5, a building must be 
divided into one or more building section with comparable fire risks.
(2) All building sections must be referred to a category of use, see 
Appendix 1, Table 1, and a risk class,see Appendix 1, Table 2
-
Category of use and risk classification: §85
The category of use must be determined based on whether a 
building section includes sleeping facilities, whether persons in the 
building section have been informed of escape routes, whether 
people are able to bring themselves into safety and based on the 
maximum number of persons which can be accommodated in each 
room. Determination of the category of use for a building section 
must be carried out in accordance with Appendix 1, Table 1.
-
Category of use and risk classification: §86
The risk class of a building section must be determined based on the 
use of the building, the complexity of the evacuation procedures for 
people with due consideration of the layout of the building and 
based on the fire load of the relevant building section, see Appendix 
1, Table 2.
(2) When a building is designed with several building sections with 
shared escape routes, the number of persons and the determination 
of the risk class must be based on the total number of persons in the 
building
sections with shared escape routes.
(3) If a building has several building sections which are classified 
into different risk classes, and the building sections share escape 
routes, the highest risk classification of one of the building sections 
must apply to all the building sections allocated to the escape 
routes.
Fire safety installations: §88
Fire safety installations and hand extinguishing equipment installed 
in and around buildings must contribute to the fire safety of the 
building. The choice of fire safety installations must take into account 
the need to:
1) Detection of the fire at an early stage in the fire process.
2) Quick alarm call to the emergency services.
3) Alerting of persons in the building in a way which enables quick 
commencement of evacuation.
alerting in different kinds of signals
audio, visual effect, vibration
no further definition here
4) Evacuation of persons in the building must be possible via safe 
escape routes.
no further definition here
5) The development of the fire is under control.
-
6) Smoke and heat is directed away from the building.
-
7) Persons in and around the building are able to assess the escape 
routes and find their way in connection with an evacuation 
procedure.
wayfinding supported by different elements 
tactile, contrast, light
8) The conditions are safe for rescue and firefighting personnel.
(2) Fire safety installations must be planned and installed in such a 
way that they can be inspected and maintained throughout their 
entire useful life.
-
Fire safety in case of fire: §90
Buildings' fire-technical installations and hand-extinguishing 
equipment must be designed and installed according to the Building 
Regulations' guidance for chapter 5 - Fire or in another way which 
similarly documents that the fire-technical installations function as 
intended.
PCS. 2. Automatic fire alarm systems and automatic sprinkler

Bilag 12: 
A special design of the ABDL system is that the activation part is 
designed as a pre-tensioned spring which, when activated, pushes 
the door into a closed position, but otherwise has no contact with the 
door. The door will thereby be able to be used as a normal door 
without a self-closing mechanism, but will still be closed when fire is 
detected near the door. This special function is called ABDL system 
with clearance pump.

The location of the building on the plot and its design must ensure 
that, in case of fire, the emergency services can safely carry out 
search and rescue as well as evacuation of persons and animals. It 
must also be ensured that the necessary extinguishing work can be 
carried out and material spreading of fire prevented between fire 
safety units.

Options for emergency service action: §127
Fire technical installations for the use of the emergency services 
must be clearly marked.
see ISO 7010 for characterisitics of each sign
Signs to mark fire-related aspects must be rectangular or square 
in shape. This is done with a white pictogram or text on a red 
background. The red color must cover at least 50% of the sign's 
surface.
On the left side of the sign is placed a square with a pictogram, a 
simple, universal image or symbol that has a clear meaning. 
Signage intended for the attention of the rescue services must be 
marked with a pictogram such as flames and a helmet, while 
signage for the attention of users only marked with flames.
A main text and any supplementary text is placed on the right-hand 
side. Words must not be spaced or underlined. Capital letters can be 
used for initial letters and for short words, while long words are 
easier to read with regular letters. The font should be sans serif 
(without feet).

Elevators § 244
In buildings with 3 floors and above, at least one lift must be installed 
that can serve each floor, including the used attic and basement. In 
buildings with several entrances, the requirement applies to each 
entrance. A building with a ground floor, 1st floor and 2nd floor is 
considered a building with 3 floors. Basements are not included in 
this context when calculating the number of floors.
isUD 3.3: All stairs and escalators along 
primary circulation routes have an adjacent 
ramp, elevator, or inclined moving walkway
two-story houses need elevators too
Elevators §246
In new construction, cf. § 244, where a lift is installed, at least one lift 
must be designed and constructed as a type 2 lift

1 Concept for universal design
1.1 Development of a strategy for equal access and use in the early 
design phase 
A project-specific accessibility strategy is drawn up in the early 
design phase and is available at the latest with the authority's 
project. The strategy for equal access and use contains an overall 
description of applicable user needs, legal requirements and 
instructions and how these are conceptually accommodated in the 
project. Furthermore, the strategy relates to all relevant points, cf. 
Appendix 2. basic requirements.
isUD1.1 & 1.2: Project development team 
selects a universal design development 
coordinator responsible for managing the 
design process and preparing the necessary 
materials to support certification.
Project development team includes diverse 
stakeholders (e.g., employees of different job 
titles, gender, various age groups, people 
with disabilities, potential visitors, 
representatives from client organization, 
representatives from design team, 
etc.).‘Project development team consults with 
universal design specialists.
Project development team conducts 
research with future facility users to identify 
and prioritize issues (e.g., confidential 
survey, design workshop, focus group, open 
meeting, etc.).
Project development team conducts 
research on best practices in universal 
design, with particular attention to examples 
that demonstrate integration with other 
human centered design goals (e.g., 
sustainability, active living, environmental 
health, etc.).
Project development team develops 
marketing strategy to educate facility users

Protection §58
Corridors, stairs and ramps in common access roads as well as 
balconies, French balcony doors, balcony entrances, airlocks, roof 
terraces, external stairs and other raised living areas must be 
secured with guards and provided with handrails, taking into 
account the building's use. All types of guardrails or railings must be 
designed, taking into account the building's use, so that their height, 
design, free openings in the guardrail, etc. prevents people from 
falling over or through them.
The provision is considered fulfilled when:
--> Description of users and how needs are taken into account in 
relation to traffic and stay in corridors, stairs, ramps, balconies etc. 
must be available.

C: at least 1,0m
B: at least 1,1m
A: at least 1,2m

above landings is at least 0.90 m.
C: at least 0,8m for stairs and ramp/ at least 0,9m above landings
B: -
A: -

m, all-gangways and airlocks is at least 1.20 m.
C: protection height at least 1,2m
B: at least 1,2m and appears visible (not transparent)
A: Guard designed so that it is not possible to get close when 
sloping or planting in front
4) The height of guardrails must be measured above the front edges 
of steps and ramps and from the top edge of the floor/deck.
5) Handrails must be easy to grip and hold on to.

Protection §59
Protection must be carried out so that the mutual distance between 
all types of balusters, both vertical and horizontal, does not give rise 
to personal injury. In this connection, special consideration must be 
given to ensure that children cannot climb the railing or get trapped 
between balusters
C: Distance between any vertical balusters maximum 89 mm.
B: Protection is completely or partially made of, for example, plate or 
glass.
A: Guards are made of perforated plate or frosted glass throughout 
the entire height
Handrails §61
Corridors, stairs and ramps in shared access ways, where there is 
no protection, must be provided with handrails on both sides, taking 
into account the design and use of the building. The handrails must 
be easy to grasp and hold on to. Handrails must be passed 
unbroken past the landing and end horizontally.
C: andrails suitable for gripping on both sides of stairs and ramps in 
shared access routes and in corridors in shared access routes, 
taking into account the use of the building. The handrails run 
continuously past the landing and end horizontally.
B: All stairs and ramps have graspable handrails on both sides. 
They are continuous on both sides and end approximately 30 cm 
horizontally.
A: All stairs and ramps have graspable handrails on both sides in 
two heights, taking into account the use of the building. They are 
unbroken around both sides and end about 30 cm horizontally.

Information §62 for publicly accessible buildings
User-operated facilities §159
User-operated facilities, including waste disposal systems, IT stands, 
payment and self-service machines and similar facilities with 
publicly available service functions, must be set up so that users can 
operate them with their own help . The requirement is considered 
fulfilled when the user-operated system is designed and installed in 
such a way that:
--> Description of which user-operated facilities exist and an 
explanation of how users can operate them.
isUD 7.11: All equipment and emergency 
controls are visible and located within reach.
isUD 2.4: Reach targets are not under or 
behind built elements or furniture, except 
where an equivalent target is located above 
the built element or furniture (e.g. if an 
electrical outlet is located under the desk, 
one is also located above). 
Where knee or toe clearance is not provided, 
all reach targets have a clear floor space 
positioned for side approach. 
Where only a forward approach to a reach 
target is available, the reach target overlaps 
the clear floor space by at least 100 mm (i.e., 
knee and/or toe clearance brings reach 
target closer to the user).
Selected reach targets have overlapping 
clear floor spaces allowing right and left 
shoulder alignment for side and/or forward 
reach. 
Selected reach targets have a clear floor 
space for side approach positioned 510 mm 
or less from the reach target

differences in this area are equalised.

least 305 mm from a corner.

sitting.

floor or ground.

Elevators §244
In buildings with 3 floors and above, at least one elevator must be 
installed that can serve each floor, including utilized attic and 
basement. In buildings with several staircases, the requirement 
applies to each staircase. A building with a ground floor, 1st floor 
and 2nd floor is considered a building with 3 floors. Basements are 
not included in this context when calculating the number of floors.
C: Lift in buildings with 3 floors and above.
B: In buildings with 2 access floors, there is, for example, a lifting 
platform to the 2nd floor.
A: In buildings with only 2 access floors there is a lift, incl. possible 
basement and roof floor.

The layout of the buildings §196
Buildings must be designed and constructed so that, taking into 
account their use, satisfactory conditions are achieved with regard to 
safety, health, functionality and use for the users, as well as taking 
into account cleaning and maintenance .
--> Depending on who the users are, it is described how the 
relationship regarding safety, soundness, functionality and use are 
achieved.

Goals
The aim of this criterion is to ensure the buildings' functionality and 
flexibility and to promote the building's spatial and architectural 
qualities.
Advantages
/ Planning
N/A
N/A

Appendix A
Gap Analysis
DGNB
Functionality and flexibility also affect users' satisfaction with the 
building. Extra spacious and functional facilities, the architectural 
quality of the areas of use and the interaction between the spaces 
can increase comfort (and health) for all users of a building 
(tenants/owners and/or for external users).
Residential Building
1) Variety of applications
1.1. The building type's potential for differentiated use
Evaluation is based on the variation of dwelling sizes (number of 
rooms) and possibly the type of dwelling.
- At least 2 housing types in smaller residential buildings with less 
than 10 housing units
- At least 3 housing types in medium-sized residential buildings with 
between 11-20 housing units
- At least 4 housing types in large residential buildings with more 
than 21 housing units.
housing types usable for several kinds of 
users 
different sizes to allow different amount of 
people living in their
inclusion, diversity of residents
1.2. Additional range of uses
- Common meeting places and communication zones inside the 
building, e.g. seating areas, common rooms, multifunctional rooms, 
workshops and exercise rooms. Shared washing/drying rooms or 
shower facilities are not included.
- The building's shared facilities cater to at least 3 age groups. 
- There are rooms that can be rented for non-residential purposes 
such as guest apartments, function rooms or offices/studios.
- There are communal laundry facilities with sufficient capacity for 
the building. 
- There is the possibility of outdoor/covered clotheslines for drying 
clothes - shared or connected to the individual home.
inclusion
all ways to the shared facilities should be 
wide enough to ensure all people to reach the
spaces
design of the ways should be designed with 
tactile and contrast elements to ensure 
wayfinding for everybody

usable for everybody, depending on the 
design of the rooms
additional effort for physical impaired 
people 
2) Quality of the application areas
2.1 Flexibility of toilets and bathroom 
- The toilet and bathroom are arranged in such a way that there is 
space for a washing/drying machine (usually at least 230x240 cm).
- Toilets and bathrooms are laid out so that they can be easily 
adapted for disabled users.
-
2.2 Quality in internal entrance and access areas
The entrance areas of the homes have enough space to receive 
guests and create wardrobe space. There is also direct or indirect 
daylight in the entrance area:
Stairwells/common access areas have:
- Art 
- Daylight 
- View 
- Nature e.g. plants, water element 
- Seating options e.g. "sitting staircase", bench/furniture or niche 
- Opportunity to add a personal touch with space for decoration or 
artifacts such as pictures, plants, etc. 
- Storage space/wardrobe e.g. for shoes etc. (especially for homes 
and institutions) 
- Gamification (building-integrated play) 
- Designed so that regular users are directed to stairs and that 
elevators are "reserved" for users with needs.
For homes without stairwells/common access areas, the entrance 
area is evaluated for
- Daylight 
- View 
- Storage space/wardrobe e.g. for shoes etc.

Connection between housing and outdoor open spaces
The direct and visual connection between the home and the 
outdoors is considered open spaces. Open spaces are defined in 
this case as gardens, balconies or terraces, but a differentiation is 
made between green/planted and fortified open spaces including 
balconies.

Inclusive design prioritizes the experience and participation of 
building users by considering the full range of ability, age, gender, 
language, cultural understanding, and other characteristics of

13 An access strategy is developed in line with Checklist A3 on

Inclusive and accessible design (residential only)
15 Where there are national best practice standards or local

checklist requirements (see Checklist A4 on page 430), the

16 Where the country of assessment does not have a compliant

criteria.
This checklist should not be used on its own. Please refer to the 
Lifetime Homes website to view the details of
each of the 16 individual criteria. This checklist briefly summarises 
all 16 criteria.
(1) Parking (width or widening capability)
(1)a ‘On plot’ (non-communal) parking: Where a dwelling has
car parking within its individual plot boundary, at least one
parking space length should be capable of enlargement to
achieve a minimum width of 3300mm.
(1)b Communal or shared parking: Where parking is provided
by communal or shared bays, spaces with a width of
3300mm, in accordance with the specification detailed on
the Lifetime Homes - parking website, should be provided.
Lifetime Home: Where there is car parking adjacent to the
home, it should be capable of enlargement
to attain 11 feet (3300mm) width.
(2) Approach to dwelling from parking (distance, gradients and 
widths)
The distance from the car parking space of Criterion 1 to the 
dwelling entrance (or relevant block entrance or lift core), should be 
kept to a minimum and be level or gently sloping. The distance from 
visitors' parking to relevant entrances should be as short as 
practicable and be level or gently sloping.
isUD 5.2: The site does not have any 
stairways or ramps between any site access 
points and primary destinations, nor between 
any parking lots and primary destinations. 
Lifetime Home: The distance from the car parking space to the 
home should be kept to a minimum and should be level or gently 
sloping. It is preferable to have a level approach. However, where 
the topography prevents this, a maximum gradient of 1:12 is 
permissible on an individual slope of less than 16½ feet (5 meters) 
or 1:15 if it is between 16½ and 33 feet (5 and 10m), and 1:20 where 
it is more than 33 feet (10m) (providing there are top, bottom and 
intermediate landings of not less than 4 feet (1.2m) excluding the 
swing of doors and gates). Paths should be a minimum of 3 feet 
(900mm) width
isUD 3.8: All ramps with a run less than or 
equal to 6 feet (1830 mm) have a slope of 
1:12 or less, and all ramps with a run greater 
than 6 feet (1830 mm) have a minimum slope
of 1:15. 
(3) Approach to all entrances
The approach to all entrances should preferably be level or 
gently sloping, and in accordance with the specification detailed on 
the Lifetime Homes - approach to dwelling website.
isUD 3.4: All primary entrances and exits are 
at grade with no raised threshold. 
All entrances have protection against water 
infiltration (e.g., covered, sloped at 1:48 
max., drainage, etc.).

BREEAM
All entrances should:
a) Be illuminated
b) Have level access over the threshold (max.1:12)
c) Have effective clear opening widths and nibs as specified below.
In addition, main entrances should also:
d) Have adequate weather protection
e) Have a level external landing. (1,2x1,2m)
Lifetime home: All entrances should be illuminated and have 
accessible level access over the threshold level and the main 
entrance should be covered
(5) Communal stairs and lifts
(5)a Communal Stairs: Principal access stairs should provide easy 
access in accordance with the specification detailed on the Lifetime 
Homes - communal stairs website, regardless of whether or not a lift 
is provided.
(5)b Communal Lifts: Where a dwelling is reached by a lift, it should 
be fully accessible in accordance with the specification detailed on 
the Lifetime Homes website
Lifetime home: Communal stairs should provide easy access, and 
where homes are reached by a lift it should be fully wheelchair 
accessible. The minimum dimensions and control 
positioning/specification should be as described in the Part M 
standard, above, except that the internal dimensions of the lift should 
be 3½ x 4½ feet (1100 x 1400mm)
(6) Internal doorways and hallways
Movement in hallways and through doorways should be as 
convenient to the widest range of people as possible, including 
those using mobility aids or wheelchairs, and those moving furniture 
or other objects. As a general principle, narrower hallways and 
landings will need wider doorways in their side walls. The width of 
doorways and hallways should conform to the specification detailed 
on the Lifetime Homes - internal doorways and hallways website.
Lifetime home: The width of the internal doorways and hallways 
should conform to the Part M standard shown to the left, except that 
when the approach is not head on and the corridor / passageway 
width is only 3 feet (900mm), the doorway clear opening width 
should be 3 feet (900mm) rather than 31½ inches (800mm). The 
front door should have a minimum 31½ inches (800mm) clear 
opening width. There should be 12 inches (300mm) to the side of 
the leading edge of the doors on the entrance level (this would 
usually only apply to the front door, as other doors open into the 
room).
(7) Circulation space
There should be space for turning a wheelchair in dining areas and 
living rooms and basic circulation space for wheelchair users 
elsewhere.
Lifetime home: There should be space for turning a wheelchair in 
dining areas and sitting rooms, and adequate circulation space for 
wheelchair users elsewhere.
(8) Entrance level living space
A living room or living space should be provided on the entrance 
level of every dwelling.
(9) Potential for entrance level bedspace
In dwellings with two or more storeys, with no permanent bedroom 
on the entrance level, there should be space on the entrance level 
that could be used as a convenient temporary bed-space
Lifetime home: In houses of two or more stories, there should be 
space on the entrance level that could be used as a convenient bed 
space.
(10) Entrance level WC and shower drainage
Where an accessible bathroom, in accordance with criterion 14 of 
this list, is not provided on the entrance level of a dwelling, the 
entrance level should have an accessible WC compartment, with 
potential for a shower to be installed, as detailed in the specification 
on the Lifetime Homes - entrance level shower and drainage 
website.

BREEAM
Lifetime home: There should be a) a wheelchair accessible 
entrance level WC, with b) drainage provision enabling a shower to 
be fitted in the future. In dwellings of three bedrooms or more, a 
wheelchair user should be able to close the door from within the 
closet and achieve side transfer from a wheelchair to at least one 
side of the WC. 
There must be at least 3½ feet (1100mm) clear space from the front 
of the WC bowl. The door of the WC will often need to open outward 
to allow this accessibility. In small two bedroom dwellings where the 
design has failed to achieve this fully accessible WC, the Part M 
standard WC will meet this standard.
(11) WC and bathroom walls
Walls in all bathrooms and WC compartments should be capable of 
firm fixing and support for adaptations such as grab rails
Lifetime home: Walls in bathrooms and WCs should be capable of 
taking adaptations such as handrails. Reinforcements should be 
located between 11 4/5 inches and 5 feet (300 and 1500mm) from 
the floor.
12) Stairs and potential through-the-floor lift in dwelling
The design within a dwelling of two or more storeys should 
incorporate both: a) Potential for stair lift installation; and b) A 
suitable identified space for a through-the–floor lift from the entrance 
level to a storey containing a main bedroom and a bathroom 
satisfying Criterion (14) Bathrooms on the next page.
Lifetime home: The design should incorporate: a) provision for a 
future stair lift (diagrams 12a and 12b). b) an identified space for a 
potential through the floor lift from the entrance level to first floor, for 
example to a bedroom next to a bathroom (diagram 12c). There 
should be a minimum of 3 feet (900mm) clear distance between the 
stair wall (on which the lift would normally be located) and the edge 
of the opposite handrail/ balustrade. Unobstructed ‘landings’ are 
needed at the top and bottom of stairs.
(13) Potential for fitting of hoists and bedroom or bathroom
Structure above a main bedroom and bathroom ceilings should be 
capable of supporting ceiling hoists and the design should provide a 
reasonable route between this bedroom and the bathroom.
Lifetime home: The design should provide for a reasonable route 
for a potential hoist from a main bedroom to the bathroom.
(14) Bathrooms
An accessible bathroom, providing ease of access in accordance 
with the specification detailed on the Lifetime Homes - bathrooms 
website should be provided in every dwelling on the same storey as 
a main bedroom.
Lifetime home: The bathroom should be designed to incorporate 
ease of access to the bath, WC and wash basin.
(15) Glazing and window handle heights
Windows in the principal living space (typically the living room), 
should allow people to see out when seated. In addition, at least one 
opening light in each habitable room should be approachable and 
usable by a wide range of people, including those with restricted 
movement and reach.
Lifetime home: Living room window glazing should begin at 31½ 
inches (800mm) or lower and windows should be easy to 
open/operate.
(16) Location of service controls
Service controls should be within a height band of 450mm to 
1200mm from the floor and at least 300mm away from any internal 
room corner.
Lifetime home: Switches, sockets, ventilation and service controls 
should be at a height usable by all (i.e. between 1½ and 4 feet (450 
and 1200mm) from the floor).

Interview – Dansk Blindesamfund – 18.04.2024 
Interview participants: 
Christian Bundgaard (CB) – Politisk konsulent, DBS, blind  
Inke Lindberg (IL) – Student, DTU  
 
IL 
I would just walk through my questions with you. I think they are easy to answer, 
hopefully. So, as I said, it's about residential buildings. I would like to know how you 
live like in a house or an apartment?  
 
CB 
In an Apartment. 
 
IL 
And do you live alone? 
 
CB 
No. I live with my wife.  
 
IL 
Okay. 
And is there an elevator in the building?  
 
CB 
No. 
 
IL 
Do you have difficulties in wayfinding in your apartment?  
 
CB 
Not at all.  
 
IL 
Probably because you are used to it. Or in the building at all, like in the hallway?

Appendix B 
Empirical Research 
Questionnaire 
CB 
No, not at all.   
 
IL 
Okay. But was there in the beginning? 
 
CB 
In the beginning, finding my way is always more difficult. You know, or if someone has 
moved something that shouldn't be standing there or, you know, just for some reason 
has left something or moved something around which I'm not aware of. You know, then 
it becomes dangerous but as long as things are as I'm used to them, then it's quite 
easy to navigate.
