C 
Appendix C – Transcript Expert Interviews 
 
 
 
  
 
==PAGE:181==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
Interview 1 – AKF – 15.03.2024 
Interview participants: 
Christian Gjessing Bruun (CGB) – ESG & Bæredygtighedschef, AKF  
Inke Lindberg (IL) – Student, DTU  
 
IL 
Do you know what universal design is? 
 
CGB 
Yes, but I am not an expert. I guess the architects should be the experts. 
 
IL 
Have you heard about the eight goals and seven principles of universal design? 
 
CGB 
No. Not all.  
 
IL 
What would you understand under universal design?  
 
CGB 
I would say the main focus of the Danish concept is accessibility for all users. So 
especially these larger stairways and level-free entrances and all that stuff. At least 
that's the thing that comes up the most and talking about it. 
 
IL 
Yes. Okay. Do you have an idea why the industry could be slow in implementing 
universal design because it's more than just accessibility? 
 
CGB 
Everything costs money. And every time you ask your architect or your engineer to do 
something else, they would have to have more hours to do it. And then the solution to 
build would probably be more expensive as well. So, in the building industry, we tend 
to be a bit slow in, in making changes. In general. Universal design is just one of them. 
==PAGE:182==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
IL 
Yes. 
 
CGB 
But of course, when it's regulated and in the building codes, then we have to do it. And 
then everybody does it. And then it's like the standard product that you get from your 
advisors and your construction team in the end.  
 
IL 
Okay. Have you considered disabilities in the design process? 
 
CGB 
Yes. Both in terms of building codes, but also in terms of DGNB certification, when 
there are a lot of extra demands within that scheme. Apart from that, depends on what 
you're looking at. Like one of the projects we have in Brøndby, we have this common 
culture facility and some smaller common buildings with shared functions and common 
facilities. I don't know if that's within the Universal Design scope, but we would say it's 
something that's making the apartments more accessible because it's built smaller and 
cheaper in absolute terms for the inhabitants, but they still have a lot of the functions 
that they would need within the shared facilities. Which is easier accessible because 
it's in the main buildings. With floor entrance. But yeah, otherwise the Danish building 
code has a lot of universal design within it. So, I'd say we do. We do what's demanded 
what everybody else does. And that's some projects we do a bit more but not a lot 
more. 
 
IL 
Okay. But you're not considering a specific disability like blind people or for hearing 
impairments. So mainly the accessibility part if you have physical impairments. 
 
CGB 
Yes, at the moment, it's like it's a physical so it's making sure that elderly people or 
handicapped people, physically handicapped people can enter buildings and all the 
functions. We don't have specific focuses on certain disabilities. 
 
==PAGE:183==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
IL 
Okay, but do you question sometimes those regulations or requirements in favor of 
people with other disabilities? Have you ever done it like this could be maybe better if 
we do it differently? 
 
CGB 
Because I'm not that much into the project. So that would be the project developers 
making the questions with their advisors. So, the engineers and the architects are 
about finding the right solution for the given case so of course everything is questioned. 
But again, it's like it's difficult to take, let's just say one disability if it's blind people and 
make sure that all apartments and the entire development is living up to all the needs 
of the blind people, especially when you don't even know if there is going to be any 
blind people in the development. So, it's a give and take. It has to make sense 
 
IL 
So, when I understand it right, the only universal design solutions you have 
implemented are the ones for physical impairments with lower entrances, elevators, 
for sure, ramps. 
 
CGB 
I'm not into the details. But we also do include some of the criteria in the DGNB. I can't 
remember all of them. It's a long list. But if you want to look into it, you can see some 
of the criteria. 
 
IL 
Yes, I have the DGNB criteria. catalog.  
 
CGB 
So yes, but then you also see some of the scores that we have made on the social part 
that, the disability part. I'm not that into the details of it. So, it could be more than that. 
But for sure, as a base, we do the building code stuff. But then, much of the DGNB 
criteria is just those demands. A bit extra. But I think they have additional criteria as 
Well, I can't remember. I am not that into the details of that. 
 
==PAGE:184==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
IL 
So, are you - I'm not sure if you're now the right person to ask but - are you planning 
to implement more universal solutions in the future? Or is it also more the developers 
who could answer that question? 
 
CGB 
No, we are always trying to. It's called building a product, making the best product, the 
more universal it is, the bigger the group of customers are. So, it's like our company 
has always had this large social focus in terms of our owners being the unions. And 
we are always focused on making it very including and safe. I don't know if that's a 
grey area of the universal design. 
 
IL 
So social integration is a part of the goals. 
 
CGB 
It's like we see a lot of developments all around where they have very little focus on 
how you feel when you're in the area or the building. And we have a large focus on that 
as a start. Yeah. And then, of course, everything else should make sense as well. So, 
making the pathways with the correct amount of lighting and having eyes on all the 
outside areas, at all times so that there are windows and no hidden corners, where you 
feel unsafe.  All of those things are a very big focus point in our developments. That's 
a bit more low practical, but you would have to design your building envelopes in the 
matter of having eyes on every edge of the lot. 
 
IL 
Do you think it would also benefit the people without disabilities implementing more 
universal solutions? 
 
CGB 
Depends on which universal solutions you think of. It's a give-and-take. It's like, if you 
just take this stairway case where it's like, you get the points from making the stairways 
even wider, or at least that's in the DGNB criteria and then you also make the netto 
area of the apartment smaller because you have more common areas. So, you get a 
==PAGE:185==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
smaller apartment, you have to pay the same, but you have, you only have like a wider 
staircase. And that's maybe not necessarily the best for the common people, if you 
could say there is common people because maybe they would have wanted that extra 
square meter within their apartment instead of the stairway and it's never black and 
white. It's like you would have to balance it. And it is like that, but everything that you 
do, and it's also in the end, it's a monetary question as well.  
 
IL 
That's actually the next question. 
 
CGB 
In the end, someone is going to pay for the apartment or the house that we're building. 
And if you pay a lot more to implement all these universal design strategies and could 
be very low practical solutions. You could make the apartment too expensive and, in 
the end, it wouldn't be suitable for the one with the needs because they can't afford it. 
So, there is some sort of give and take in that debate as well. 
 
IL 
So, you recognized a kind of cost increase when you implemented those accessibility 
solutions? 
 
CGB 
Depending on what it is, something is cost neutral. But again, back to the start. When 
you ask your advisors to do something different, they will have to use more time on it 
than just a standard product that they have on the shelf. And they can just oh we made 
that already in this project. You can have it here. Then they would have to think and do 
something else. And that's always costing upfront. In the council, advisor costs, but 
again, also when you come to the build phase in the construction phase, because then 
it's another product than the standard product that's on the shelf, then you'd have to 
go and find it somewhere else. That's not what everybody else buys. And that's making 
it more expensive normally, but of course, some things are less costly than others. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:186==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
IL 
But you still see a cost-benefit of implementing those solutions? So like, you pay more 
but you can offer it maybe to more people. 
 
CGB 
That's a difficult question because we don't have data on it. It's something that's quite 
new within the business. And it's quite new all over. So, I don't really know if we have 
any correct data on it, showing that this is something that's a win-win in the end. 
Because then you also with, if we, just an example, is these level free entrances as the 
one you see down there. If you get a lot of rainwater, you could have issues with water 
within the building. That's costly on the operational budget. So, some of these things 
that are regulated, you have to do that there's no way of not doing it. But it can be more 
costly, on the operational budget as well. While something could go the other way. So, 
it's difficult just to put it up like is this a win or not. 
 
IL 
Okay. Do you have an idea where those solution makes sense and we're not? 
 
CGB 
In general, they all make sense. The issue is to just implement it correctly. The example 
with the level-free entrance, you could have some light ramps going up to the level-
free entrance instead of having it completely level-free in terms of not having water 
within that entrance door as often as you'd have if it's completely. 
 
IL 
But is it right now that it has to be like no angle? I'm just not sure right now. 
 
CGB 
There is like a minimum demand like it should be very little, almost water level like very 
sewer angle. But if you just do a little slope, or as much slope as you can, then you 
would prevent some of those issues. But often when you design a building the advisors 
design it within just the given building regulation minimum demands, so it's in Danish 
we say […], just like it's just on the edge of what you should do. Instead of doing what 
would be the right solution and the more long-term sustainable solution. That's also 
==PAGE:187==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
one of the maybe weaknesses in the building code. It is like it's, it's very specific about 
how much angle you should have as a minimum or whatever it is. Instead of saying 
like, that there could be like a guidance on. This is the minimum, but you should 
probably do like this instead. 
 
IL 
I talked about this with Kristoffer once, like there is a minimum requirement, a maximum 
requirement, and maybe there's an optimum somewhere in the middle or so.  
 
CGB 
And if you have these minimum requirements, often you'll get the minimum solution. 
You wouldn't get the in-between solution. That would make the project overall, like in 
the total economic perspective, sustainable. 
So that's, that's a big issue. For sure. 
 
IL

use it in another way than the engineers are thinking it, We see that with very, 
especially with technical stuff, it's like it's never used as you would expect it. So that 
==PAGE:188==

CGB 
Yes, I think that's one of the criteria in the DGNB as well. It's like, at least that entrances

CGB 
The honest answer to that would be not at all because there are so many limitations

it's often just given that you would have to place your kitchen where it is. Because there 
is that one solution. I don't think that's very high on the priority list in terms of making 
the apartment plans.

Transcript Interview Series 1 
 
them and maybe gives them a cup of coffee and they can smell buns being cooked or 
something. But in terms of floor plans in apartment buildings, we did a project where 
we looked at housing solutions for young people who came from homelessness and 
who are, for various reasons extra sensitive to the space they are in. And this is often 
very, very small homes, like the smallest was 16 square meters, which is super small. 
And what we looked at there was how a lot of the functions end up overlapping and 
that makes it really difficult to live there. Because if your bed is on top of the places, 
like it's only you'll be able to have your bed at the place where you're also supposed to 
be sitting. And your kitchen is basically in that space as well. It becomes too packed to 
get people to have a life and everything but the benefit of having the kitchen closer to 
because now people work with open floor plans. So, the kitchen in these very very 
small flats. The kitchen becomes a division between the very intimate space which is 
the bedroom and the entrance. Because a lot of these people don't want you to open 
the door and then you open it right into their most intimate private space which is the 
bed and that's where having the kitchen closer towards the door is a useful thing but I 
do see how some of the fire regulations it's a fire hazard in a sense. But what maybe 
you should look into in terms of the open floor plans and kitchens as well as one thing 
is the risk of fire. But at the moment there's a lot of very interesting research coming 
out I think Realdania funding it. But about the health risks from when we cook so 
because all the chemicals being, coming from cooking and all the particles from 
cooking, go straight out into homes and a lot of I mean a lot of flats don't have a vent. 
So there's, I mean and if you have an open plan solution, you have all the particles and 
chemicals from cooking, staying in the room where you're sleeping and then you're 
sleeping in all these things and there's a big kind of health risk in terms of cancer in 
terms of other things as well. So it's quite intensive.

Interview 1 – Henning Larsen – 22.04.2024 
Interview participants: 
Victoria Chenais (VC) – Architect, Henning Larsen  
Inke Lindberg (IL) – Student, DTU  
 
IL 
Do you know what Universal Design is? 
 
VC 
Yes.  
 
IL 
And have you heard about the eight goals and seven principles?  
 
VC 
No. 
 
IL 
I can give you a little insight. The eight goals are body fit, comfort, awareness, 
understanding, 
wellness, 
social 
integration, 
personalization, 
and 
cultural 
appropriateness. Those are like some goals they always want to implement in their 
solutions. The University of Buffalo is a very front runner in this kind of thing and the 
seven principles they developed were equitable use, flexibility and use, simple and 
intuitive use, perceptible Information, tolerance for error, low physical effort, and 
appropriate size and space for approach and use. So, it's all these things you should 
kind of try to fit in the solutions.  
Okay. Then, what do you understand under universal design?  
 
VC 
I think it's a way to integrate a wider array of needs and make sure that everyone 
regardless of their physical or mental capacities, feels welcomed, seen, safe, and can 
have as equal access to spaces, as people without physical impairments. 
 
 
==PAGE:211==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
 
IL 
Why do you think the industry is slow in implementing universal design? What do you 
think are obstacles or limitations? 
 
VC 
I would say that as long as there's not a law that forces you to do things people will not 
do things because we do not like change. And we do not like when something is slightly 
different. Change might also mean more reflection. More work, hence more budget. 
And sometimes, designing for the lesser crowd can be tricky when you're building a 
project for everyone. And that's not necessarily your wish for developers, for instance, 
because a standardized solution is always easier. 
 
IL 
Are you considering disabilities in your design process? If yes, which one? 
 
VC 
Yes. 
Both, because we have to, I would say mainly because we have to by law. For instance, 
if I just base it on the last two projects that I worked on, then we're in France and we're 
quite, in France, the norms are, the building code is a bit more strict than in Denmark 
in terms of building for accessibility. And by that I mean physical and non-physical 
disabilities and accessibility.  
There was a second part to your question. 
 
IL 
Which kinds of disabilities?  
 
VC 
Physical disabilities, people in wheelchairs, for instance, people with visual 
impairments, but slightly, like there is, likely, it's mostly focused on the physical 
disability. 
 
IL 
Do you question regulations and requirements in favor of people with disabilities? 
==PAGE:212==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
 
VC 
Yes, but I would say both. Yes, because they should have the same equal chances. 
And I keep saying I think we should all spend a day being blind or deaf or in a 
wheelchair or just with crutches. And then I think it would make all of us a bit more with 
empathy towards people who have it less easy. It can be also in regards of sounds or 
lights that are too bright and then can create like stress. I don't think this is very much 
accounted for. But at the same time, some rules can be, I mean, the rules themselves 
are valid and are very well-intentioned but then the translation in the built environment 
can not necessarily bring qualities and by that, for example, I mean, when building 
housing or, like a group of housing then when we know that, okay, the bathroom needs 
to be fully accessible. All bathrooms need to be fully accessible, then we end up with 
having huge bathrooms in smaller places. So sometimes it can be a little bit less 
proportionate. And the space can lose qualities. So the way I see it could be better to 
say there's a proportion and we're going towards that again, as a proportion of housing 
units in the case of this type of project that is made for people with disabilities where 
the physical disabilities are more visible in terms of plan, and then the rest that could 
be at least flexible or transformable to accommodate different needs. 
 
IL 
Have you implemented universal design solutions already in projects here, or older 
projects? 
 
VC 
Yes, but not necessarily with a like a clear intention, that was called universal design 
or that would fit within the norms and the goals that you listed before. But we're sure it 
is in our minds and in our wishes to make people feel visible, seen, welcomed, and 
have equal access to all places. But it's not always the case that when we want to push 
for something, then the client wants to pay for it.

IL 
I'm focusing on the fire egress route design and alerting system, so the next questions 
are a bit more to that. How do you develop a fire strategy? 
 
VC 
We usually work with fire consultants or engineers who are specialized in this so as 
architects when you start working in a bit more […] phases, you have some general 
ideas, even if the code changes from one country to another where you build that you 
have general ideas of the rules and what you can and cannot do. But then, of course, 
we work with people who have a very specific project-oriented knowledge and it's 
usually very dry, in the sense that there's a requirement where you apply to it. There's 
not going to be emotional thought to it, or making it smooth. I would say that the 
architectural qualities of a building or fitting the program will always prevail on the 
quality of an egress route. It's more like we have to tick the box and make sure it 
complies. 
 
IL 
Which fire safety installations are you considering in a residential building?  
 
VC 
I haven't worked in residential buildings, recently, so I'm not sure I can provide a quality 
answer to that.

to reach everyone so not just the smoke detector with a sound? 
 
VC 
No. At least not in phases a project was on maybe it would be something to be 
implemented later on. But the way it was thought was more in terms of spatial 
requirements, like in the space, that the plan complies. That we have enough egress, 
the green luminous signs and all that. Of course, there will be some sound alarms. But 
other than that, the traditional way, the classic way.

Okay. Are you implementing visual effects such as contrast to improve wayfinding for 
people with visual disabilities?

Yes, or some tactility elements on the floor for instance. 
 
IL

Yes. 
 
IL

VC 
Tactile and contrast, it's because it's, again, like dictated in the building code. There 
are some codes. For instance, the American code refers to it. The Saudi Building Code, 
which is based on the American code refers to it and makes it mandatory. The French 
code also talks about it, especially in outdoor spaces. But for the housing code, I'm not 
so sure. And in Denmark, it's much laxer.  
 
IL 
Okay. How do you decide the shape of your floor plan and why?  
 
==PAGE:217==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
 
VC 
First in terms of site constraints, like building boundaries, then fitting the program, then 
making sure that there's a nice flow and a nice access to daylight. These would be the 
first requirements and quantities that we look for. And then yes, later on, we'll be like, 
“Oh, this route to an egress is too long or this corridor is too narrow”, but it really comes 
as a second layer of priority. 
 
IL 
Given that in kitchens, most fires occur. Could you imagine shaping the floor with not 
having the kitchen in the direct escape route? Or has it already been planned too far? 
If you're looking at the egress route? 
 
VC 
Well, but usually you have kitchen and then a relationship to the entrance and then 
towards the egress route. So, I don't, since we're talking about cells, basically like 
group of cells and an apartment is a cell. Then we look more at the apartments as cells 
in relationship to the group, rather than what's going on inside the apartment. That will 
be the logic. And then I highly doubt that the floor plan would be looked at in order to 
replace the kitchen. That relationship to egress routes and closeness to it would be the 
first element. It would be more about how to group them. So, ventilation and water 
systems are collected all together for instance. 
 
IL 
Okay. How do you develop the egress route then in and from the apartment? 
 
VC 
Maximum distances and as a second layer avoiding weird-shaped corners, circulation 
spaces so you don't bump into something and that also usually, if you have an - actually 
I have designed some housing, it was part of a cultural thing. So, if you what I try to do 
all the time is that the bedroom that is accessible for a wheelchair was placed and also 
if you talk about toilet stalls in a public building, for instance, that you place these 
accessible, special accessibility rooms closer to the circulation space and as a 
consequence closer to the egress and exit. 
 
==PAGE:218==
Appendix C 
Expert Interview 
Transcript Interview Series 1 
 
IL 
And these exits would be the stairwells? You don't consider the balconies, just because 
I heard it before. 
 
VC 
No, because you're stuck in there. So, it's better to be able to reach a common place 
where someone can help you and carry you for instance, rather than wait alone or with 
someone else on your balcony while the building is on. Fire. I would find that extremely 
dreadful. 
 
IL 
And how do you provide safe exits for people with disability disabilities? So is there a 
solution for the people in a wheelchair? 
 
VC 
In housing not necessarily. Because they cannot take the elevator, so they have to wait 
to be picked up.  
 
IL 
And that would be in the circulation spaces in the stairwell so there is like an area 
where people could wait? 
 
VC 
Exactly. There are like, the landing areas are wider and they are fire free these 
stairwells, so then they can wait there, on their level or be taken by someone and 
carried for instance. 
 
IL 
Okay. That was my last question.

Haptic
1.1
Tactility
Haptic refers to everything 
concerning the sense of touch 
(Hatzfeld & Kern Editors, 2014). It 
helps people to be aware of things 
without seeing them and guide 
themselves through the built 
environment. Haptic elements, 
which can be touched with hands, 
feet, or assistive technology will 
facilitate wayfinding and 
orientation in a residential 
building. Haptic devices can 
support an alerting system to 
indicate that an action is required. 
The installation of tactile elements in 
the common circulation areas that 
extend from the outside entrance to the 
top floor of the building. 1. Tactile 
elements must be integrated on the 
floor guiding the way through areas 
with wider circulation spaces. 2. Tactile 
elements must be installed on the 
walls in narrow circulation spaces, 
indicating the location and possible 
corridor junctions.
Grip: Tactile elements mounted on the 
wall that are supposed to support a 
person walking, need to have a grip-
friendly and anti-slippery surface. 
Contrast: Tactile elements located on 
the floor and wall must be in a different 
color and/or darker, respectively 
lighter than the adjacent flooring or 
wall paint.
In universal design, solutions are 
meant to facilitate the use for 
everyone. However, this level will 
especially support blind and 
visually impaired people, as well as 
people with poor motor 
planning/control & body 
awareness.

Enough space & Egress 
without stairs
Space
5.1
Area
Space is a boundless, three-
dimensional extent in which 
objects and events occur and 
have relative positions and 
directions (Vastaranta et al., 
2020). The built environment is a 
human-made space in which 
people live, work, and recreate 
(Kaklauskas & Gudauskas, 2016). 
Here, space is meant to be an 
extent where people can freely 
move or wait without interfering 
with other residents or visitors of 
In residential buildings, a dedicated 
area must be implemented as a safe 
waiting space for people not being 
able to use the stairs. This area must 
be provided on each floor in front or 
close to the elevator. Especially, in 
case of emergency, it must be ensured, 
that the space is not interfering with 
passing residents or visitors of the 
building and that it is close to the 
elevator or rescue area.
Width: The area must have the 
minimum required width. Lighting: The 
area must be equipped with lamps to 
ensure people see and to be seen by 
others. Signage: The area must be 
marked as a dedicated safe waiting 
space. 
In universal design, solutions are 
meant to facilitate the use for 
everyone. However, this level will 
especially support people having a 
physical, precisely walking 
impairment and are dependent on 
assistive technology, such as 
wheelchairs. 
Enough space
Space
5.3
Spatiality
Space is a boundless, three-
dimensional extent in which 
objects and events occur and 
have relative positions and 
directions (Vastaranta et al., 
2020). The built environment is a 
human-made space in which 
people live, work, and recreate 
(Kaklauskas & Gudauskas, 2016). 
Here, space is meant to be an 
extent where people can freely 
move or wait without interfering 
with other residents or visitors of 
The floor plans have been designed in 
such a way that each apartment has 
direct access to a safe corridor, which 
leads to the staircases and elevators. 
This corridor is located either on the 
outside of the building or inside the 
building along the outside wall, with 
exit options.
Width: The space must have the 
minimum required width. Area: At the 
end of the corridor, in front of the 
elevators is a dedicated area as a safe 
waiting space. 
In universal design, solutions are 
meant to facilitate the use for 
everyone. However, this level will 
especially support blind and 
visually impaired people, as well as 
people having a physical, preciesly 
walking impairment. 
Egress without stairs

In case of an emergency, 
residents must be ready to cope 
with potentially stressful situations. 
Previous preparation is essential 
to enable residents to handle such 
situations in an appropriate and 
timely manner, while also 
facilitating an orderly and secure 
evacuation for all other residents 
and their visitors. Preparation in 
this context, is the practice and 
awareness of situations requiring 
fast and correct actions.

to prepare for a case of emergency, to

egress route design, evacuation

residents aware of different alerting 
systems and installations.

Current Requirement in BR18
Will not work differently because: 7 Principle Compliance
E
1 Haptic
Tactility
Tactile guidance and attention 
patterns on the floor in wider 
entrance areas. 
Establishment of tactile guidance and attention patterns 
on the floor in common areas where wayfinding can be 
challenging. These are primarily the larger entrance 
areas where the elevators and stairs are not in the direct 
access area. The patterns are designed to assist the 
residents in navigating from the stairs or elevators to the 
exit of the building. 
Tactile patterns are designed with a height of 4 
milimeters above ground. Attention patterns with 
truncated domes or cones have a minimum width of 560 
milimeters. The upper diameter of the domes or cones is 
12 milimeters, with a distance of 42-61 milimeters 
between them. Guidance patterns with flat-toped 
elongated bars have a width of minimum 250 milimeters. 
The bars have an upper height of 17 milimeters and a 
distance of 57-78 milimeters between them. These 
values correspond to DS/ISO 23599:2019. (Dansk 
Standard, 2019)
BR18 § 49(6) defines the 
establishment of clear tactile 
partition of pedestrian access 
paths to the building to other 
traffic categories. 
BR18 §51(5) states that areas 
outside outer doors must have 
tactile markings or markings in 
another colour than the 
surrounding surface. (Social- 
og Boligstyrelsen, 2023)
Entrance areas can be designed to 
be wider than the other corridors 
leading to the apartments. Without 
tactile elements on the floor, 
guidance can be difficult, especially 
for visually impaired or blind 
people. However, in an emergency, 
when the entrance area may 
already be filled with smoke, this 
also helps all other residents to find 
their way around. The raised 
domes or cones as well as the 
poles allow people to feel where 
they need to go with the soles of 
their feet. 
Perceptible information
E
2 Haptic
Tactility
Two handrails at different heights 
installed on both sides of the 
stairwells and corridors. 
Installation of two handrails in the stairwells at a height of 
0,61 meters and 0,915 meters above the floor. 
Handrails continue through the corridors to ensure 
guidance to the apartments or exits. (IDEA Center, n.d.-
b).
BR18 §61(2) defines an 
approximate height of handrails 
in 0,80 meters above ground. 
§58(2) define s a maximum 
height of fencing over landings 
in stairwells with minimum 0,90 
meters. (Social- og 
Boligstyrelsen, 2023)
If the handrails are only mounted at 
one height, people of a different 
height than average may have 
difficulty holding on to the 
handrails. The handrails not only 
serve as a guide but also have a 
support function. 
Flexibility in use, Low 
physical effort
E
3 Haptic
Tactility
Indication of floor numbers or 
corridor junctions through metal 
studs on the handrails. 
Attachment of metal studs to the handrails on the 
landings of each floor, indicating the floor number. 
Based on DS/ISO 19028:2019(E), the stud is a round dot 
with a diameter of 9 millimeters and a height of 5 
millimeters. (Dansk Standard, 2019) The dot indicates 
the current location.
This means: 
Basement floor: one stud curved inwards
First floor: a stud without curvature
Second floor: one stud curved outwards 
Second floor: two studs curved outwards
(consecutive for further floors)
In corridors, there is also a stud at the location of corridor 
junctions, which indicates a corridor leading in a different 
direction.
-
Handrails without tactile elements 
do not indicate the floor number or 
whether corridors lead away from a 
certain location in the main hallway. 
Studs can facilitate orientation on 
the lower floors, especially in the 
case of fire emergencies. The risk 
of missing the ground floor or 
confusing an earlier floor with the 
ground floor can be reduced. The 
studs also help people who are not 
visually impaired to find their way 
down when smoke may have 
already spread in the stairwells. 
Perceptible information

4 Haptic
Braille
Permanently displayed information 
written in Braille.
Writing all information permanently displayed in the 
building in Braille. This includes information about 
escape routes, the use of the elevator and general 
concerns of the landlord. 
BR18 §62: In building open to 
the public which offers citizen 
services, important information 
about the layout and use of the 
building must be easy to read 
and understand. (3) With due 
consideration of the use of the 
building, this information must 
be provided in Braille, sound 
and pictograms, writing and 
graphics in relief and guiding 
lines. (Social- og 
Boligstyrelsen, 2023)
If information about the use and 
location of escape routes is 
displayed in the building, but not in 
languages that everyone 
understands, the information may 
be lost and residents may not be 
prepared in an emergency. 
Equitable use, Perceptible

5 Haptic
Grip
Handrails with anti-slip material that 
clearly distinguish tactile elements 
(e.g., metal studs).
Handrails are designed in a grip-friendly material that 
reduces the risk of slipping and it must clearly distinguish 
from the metal studs implemented. 
BR18 §61 defines grip-friendly 
hand rails which are easy to 
grip and hold on to in the sides 
not equipped with fencing 
(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 
2023)
Material that poses a risk of slipping 
can lead to accidents in stairwells 
and thus on the direct escape route. 
Low physical effort
E
6 Levelness
Gradient
Elevators for the evacuation of 
people with mobility impairments.  
Residential buildings are equipped with at least one 
elevator that can be used by mobility- or mentally-
impaired residents and visitors who are not able to take 
the stairs as an escape route. The area of the lift is 
protected from fire and smoke (seperate fire 
compartement). In front of the elevator are safe waiting 
spaces (see no. 18). 
Residential buildings higher than 22 meters have one 
firefighting lift and one lift for evacuation of people who 
are not able to use the stairs. 
BR18 §244 Lift is required in 
new buildings with 3 or more 
storeys. BR18 §133 In buildings 
higher than 22 meters, one fire 
fighting lift must be installed. 
DS/EN 81-72 the fire fighting lift 
is not used as an escape route
DS/EN 81-76 introduces the 
evacutation of disabled persons 
using lifts. (Dansk Standard, 
2011; Dansk Standard 2020; 
Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 2023)
Without an elevator, mobility 
impaired people will not be able to 
rescue themselves in a case of fire 
emergency. They could be trapped 
in the corridors and simultaneously 
be an obstacle for leving residents 
or visitors of the building. Especially 
in a highrise building, the 
evacuation can take time until the 
firefighters access that specific 
floor. Further it can be assumed, 
that more than one firefighter is 
needed to carry down the waiting 
person. 
Equitable use, Low physical 
effort
E
7 Levelness
Gradient
No treshold on the pathways 
leading out of the apartment or 
building. 
All floors are at grade. There are no thresholds (=0.0 
centimeters) in the entrances, whether coming from the 
building or the apartment.  
BR18 §51(2) and §56(6) define 
the maximum height of a door 
step with 2,5 centimeters. 
(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 
2023)
Thresholds always pose a risk of 
tripping for people walking. In 
addition, people with mobility 
impairments who rely on a 
wheelchair or rollator could get 
stuck. 
Tolerance for error, Low 
physical effort
E
8 Optic
Contrast
Tactile guidance and attention 
patterns are supported by light-dark 
contrasts. 
The tactile pavement implemented in the common areas 
of the building must have a luminance contrast value of 
more than 30% than the sourrounding or adjacent 
surfaces. To calculate the contrast, the Michelson 
Constrast formula according to DS/ISO 23599:2019 is 
used. Where tactile walking surface indicators (TWSIs) 
are a discrete unit or used for indicating a hazard, the 
luminance contrast value should be equal or greater 
50%. 
The methods for measurign the parameters required to 
calculate luminance contrast can be taken from DS/ISO 
23599:2019 Annex A. (Dansk Standard, 2019)
-
Contrast elements can help to 
locate tactile guidance or attention 
patterns on the floor. Without 
differentiating the luminance, the 
floor would look like a large 
monochrome surface regardless of 
the tactile elements used. 
Perceptible information

9 Optic
Contrast
Design the frame of the apartment 
door or building exit in a darker 
contrast than the door leaf.
Design the door frame with a luminance contrast value 
greater than 30% of the door leaf and the surrounding 
wall. To calculate the contrast, the Michelson Constrast 
formula according to DS/ISO 23599:2019 is used. 
The methods for measurign the parameters required to 
calculate luminance contrast can be taken from DS/ISO 
23599:2019 Annex A. (Dansk Standard, 2019)
-
Contrast elements can help to 
locate the exit faster and more 
easily. Without differentiating the 
luminance, the wall would look like 
a large monochrome surface.

10 Optic
Contrast
Tactile guidance and attention 
patterns are supported by color 
contrasts. 
Color differences between TWSIs and the surrounding or 
adjacent surfaces are used to increase the detectability. 
One color option to use is safety yellow, which is 
supposed to have the best color conspicuity, defined in 
ISO 3864-1.  Following DS/ISO 23599:2019 green and 
red tones should be avoided. (Dansk Standard, 2019)
BR18 §51(5) states that areas 
outside outer doors must have 
tactile markings or markings in 
another colour than the 
surrounding surface. (Social- 
og Boligstyrelsen, 2023)
Contrast elements can help to

patterns on the floor. Without 
differentiating the color, the floor 
would look like a large 
monochrome surface regardless of 
the tactile elements used.

11 Optic
Contrast
The visibility of the stair edges is 
facilitated by colored markings. 
On the edge of each stair is a colored stripe indicating 
the end of a stair. One color option to use is safety 
yellow, which is supposed to have the best color 
conspicuity, defined in ISO 3864-1:2011. (Dansk 
Standard, 2019) 
BR18 §51(5) states that areas 
outside outer doors must have 
tactile markings or markings in 
another colour than the 
surrounding surface. (Social- 
og Boligstyrelsen, 2023)
Without a marker at the end of a 
staircase, the entire stairwell can 
look like a monochrome surface, 
which poses a risk of tripping and 
falling.

Tolerance for error
E
12 Optic
Lighting
Installation of anti-panic lighting 
throughout the building to lead 
residents to the escape routes. 
Anti-panic lighting is installed in all residential buildings, 
no matter of their height. Following the requirements 
from DS/EN 1838:2013 the horizontal illuminance is not 
less than 0,5 lx at the floor level of the empty core area, 
the diversity Ud ratio of the minimum to the maximum 
illuminance is not less than 1:40, disability glare is kept 
low (Imax = 500cd if h<2,5 meters and 900cd if 2,5<h<3 
meters), the color rendering indes Ra is 40, the minimum 
duration allowed for fire escape purposes is 1 hour and 
the anti-panic lighting reaches 50% of the required 
illuminance within 5 seconds and 100% within 60 
seconds. (Dansk Standard, 2013)
According to BR18 §96(1) anti-
panic lighting must be installed 
in escape route staircases in 
buildings with a top story floor 
height higher than 22 meters.
EN 1838:2013(E) defines anti-
panic lighting as Open area 
lighting (Social- og 
Boligstyrelsen, 2023; Dansk 
Standard, 2013)
Without lighting elements in the 
escape route, it is very difficult to 
navigate. Residents and their 
visitors can trip if they cannot see 
the stairs or bump into each other. 
Furthermore, waiting areas are 
difficult to see for people with 
mobility impairments, which poses 
the risk of them becoming obstacles 
for passing residents. 
Equitable use
E
13 Optic
Lighting
Installation of escape route lighting 
throughout the stairwells to lead 
residents to the outside of the 
building. 
Installation of escape route lighting throughout the 
stairwells and corridors in the building. For escape 
routes up to 2 meters in width the horizontal illuminance 
along the center line of the route is not less than 1 lux 
(DS/EN 1838:2013). The central band consisting of not 
less than half of the width of the route is illuminated to a 
minimum of 50% of that value. According to DS/EN 
1838:2013, the diversity Ud ratio of the minimum to the 
maximum illuminance is not less than 1:40, disability 
glare is kept low by limiting the luminous intensity. 
The luminous intensity of the luminaires in horizontal 
escape routes  do not exceed Imax = 500cd if h<2,5 
meters and 900cd if 2,5<h<3 meters within the zone 60° 
to 90°. For all other escape routes the maximum is not to 
be exceeded for all angles. (Dansk Standard, 2013)
-
Without lighting elements in the 
escape route, it is very difficult to 
navigate. Residents and their 
visitors can trip if they cannot see 
the stairs or bump into each other. 
Furthermore, waiting areas are 
difficult to see for people with 
mobility impairments, which poses 
the risk of them becoming obstacles 
for passing residents. 
Equitable use

14 Optic
Lighting
Photoluminescent guide strips on 
the floor to help residents navigate 
when there is no light. 
Use of photoluminescent guide strips to mark escape 
routes on the floor. The strips are 0.05 meters wide and 
run as a line on the floor of the corridor at the edge of the 
wall. The areas in front of the apartment doors remain 
free. The areas facing the corridor are marked with dots 
to indicate the exit. 
-
In the event of a failure of the 
lighting systems, whether normal 
lighting, escape route lighting, or 
anti-panic lighting, the corridors 
would be dark. Guide strips on the 
floor, which do not require a power 
source, can help people find their 
way. If smoke has already 
developed in the corridors, it tends 
to build up. Possible crawling 
occupants can find their way out 
using the guide strips on the floor. 
Perceptible information

lamps according to the corridor 
layout. 
Installation of rectangular ceiling lights with the long side 
parallel to the wall. This creates a visual line that helps 
with orientation and wayfinding. 
-
Although the hallways are not very 
wide, it is easier to find your way 
around than without them. 
Simple and intuitive use
E
16 Optic
Lighting
Signs are illuminated or made of 
photoluminescent material to 
ensure visibility when there is no 
light. 
All signs in the escape routes are either illuminated and 
connected to an emergency power supply or made of 
photoluminescent material, ensuring visibility even when 
the lights are off. 
-
In case of a failure of the electricity 
supply, guidance through the 
building is not provided. Therefore, 
signs need to be visible at all 
conditions at all time. 
Perceptible information, 
Tolerance for error
-
Without the installation of safety 
signs, various obstacles can arise. 
Firstly, it can be difficult for the fire 
department to find the equipment 
they need to extinguish the fire. 
Secondly, occupants may take 
longer to leave the building 
because, for example, they do not 
know how to open the doors or 
where the nearest escape route is. 
And thirdly, mobility-impaired 
people waiting in the wrong areas 
can become obstacles for residents 
and their visitors leaving the 
building via the stairwells. 
Perceptible information

Signs
Signs are placed for orientation and 
with a clear meaning according to 
the current standard. 
Signs are placed according to DS/EN ISO 7010:2020. 
For the residential building and design of the egress 
route, the following signs according to category E 
(Evacuation route, location of safety equipment or safety 
facility, safety action (safe condition signs) in 
signalgreen and signalwhite need to be installed: 
E001/E002 - Emergency exit; E016 - Emergency window 
with escpae ladder (if applicable); E017 - Rescue 
window; E022/E023 - Door opens by pushing; E024 - 
Evacuation temporary refuge (safe waiting areas); 
E026/E030 - Emergency exit for people unable to walk 
or with walking impairment; E057/E058 - Doors open by 
pulling; E059 - Escape ladder; E060 - Evacuation chair 
(if applicable). For the residential building and design of 
the egress route, the following signs according to 
category F (Fire equipment signs)  in signalred and 
signalwhite need to be installed: F001 - Fire 
extinguisher; F002 - Fire horse reel; F003 - Fire ladder; 
F004 - Collection of fire fighting equipment; F005 - Fire 
alarm call point; F007 - Fire protection door; F016 - Fire 
blanket. The RAL-colors are defined in ISO 3864-4. 
Signalgreen = RAL6032; signalred = RAL3001; 
signalwhite = RAL9003 (DGUV). The height and width is 
according to the regognition range minimum 25 to 100 
milimeters (Gesetzliche Unfallversicherun Deutschland, 
2016; Dansk Standard 2020)

18 Size
Font size Large, bold letters and numbers are 
used for the information and signs 
displayed.
Letters and numbers on permanently displayed 
information and signs are sized according to the 
recognition range. Information displayed should be 
defined concerning the distance of the person to the 
sign. It is defined 30 mm font size per 1 meter distance. 
(Schmidt & Buser, n.d) 
-
Information that is written too small 
will not attract the attention of 
residents. It must be ensured that 
the information can be recognized 
by everyone. Especially if it 
concerns safety instructions and/or 
evacuation measures. 
Perceptible information
E
19 Size
Width
Common hallways have a minimum 
width of 1,50 meters to allow for 
simultaneous passage of both 
individuals who are walking and 
wheelchair users. 
Common hallways must have a minimum width of 1,50 
meters. This ensures free passage for a wheelchair user 
(0,80 meters) and a person walking (0,531 meters).
BR18 §56(2) defines a 
minimum hallway width of 1,30 
meters.
(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 
2023)
According to DS/CEN ISO/TR 7250-
2:2024 the average 95 percentil 
elbow-to-elbow breadth of an adult 
is 0,531 meters. By adding these 
values to the recommended 
maximum occupied width of a 
wheelchair of 0,80 meters (ISO/TR 
13570-2_2014(E)), the resulting 
width is 1,331 meters.  The 
currently required width of hallways 
at 1,30 meters carries the risk of a 
collision, particulary in emergency 
situations, where people tend to 
run. Therefore, hallways must be 
wide enough to allow for free and 
fast movement. (Dansk Standard, 
2024, Dansk Standard, 2014)
Equitable use, Low physical 
effort, Size and space for 
approach and use

20 Size
Width
Doors have a minimum width of 
0,87 meters to allow for passage of 
wheelchair users. 
All doors in the building, no matter if in common areas or 
to and in the apartment, have a minimum width of 0,87 
meters (isUD solution 3.6). (IDEA Center, n.d.-b). 
BR18 §52, §56(5), and §208 
define a minimum of 0,77 
meters (Social- og 
Boligstyrelsen, 2023)
According to ISO/TR 13570-
2_2014(E), the recommended 
maximum occupied width of a 
wheelchair is 0,80 meters. A door 
only 0,77 meters wide will not allow 
wheelchair user to pass. (Dansk 
Standard, 2014)
Equitable use, Low physical 
effort, Size and space for 
approach and use

Area
Waiting spaces with a sufficiently 
large area in front of elevators to 
accommodate a wheelchair user 
and mobility-impaired person with 
e.g. crutches. 
The waiting areas near the elevators must be at least 
1,40 meters wide and 1,42 meters deep. This ensures 
that there is enough space for a wheelchair user (0,80 
meters) and a person (0,531 meters) who is dependent 
on crocheting, for example.

-
Size and space for 
approach and use

22 Space
Spatiality Connection of all apartments to an 
access balcony leading to the 
evacuation stairwells and elevators. 
The access balcony leading to the evacuation stairwells 
and elevators has the same characteristics as the 
hallway. With a minimum width of 1.5 meters, it ensures 
that residents who are walking and residents who are 
using a wheelchair can pass simultaneously. The 
adjacent area with the stairwells and elevators is a new 
fire section protected from smoke and flames.
-
Currently, balconies serve as safe 
places for residents who are unable 
to walk down the stairs. They are 
waiting until the rescue team 
arrives, which will carry them down. 
However, this method cannot be 
guaranteed if the highest rescue 
point is over 23 meters (in Austria 
and Germany) or 28 meters (in 
France). Following DS/EN 14043, 
these values define the nominal 
reach of a turntable ladder used for 
rescuing people. (Dansk Standard, 
2014)

A
1 Haptic
Vibration
Vibration alarm devices provided by the 
landlord. 
Provision of vibration devices to be installed on the bed 
for the hearing impaired immediately upon key handover 
or at the tenant's request. The flash frequency is between 
1,5 and 2 Hz. (FlameStop, n.d.; Dansk Standard, 2010)
BR18 §93(4) A residential unit 
in a building section in use 
category 4 must be established 
with a smoke alarm system 
(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 
2023)
Hearing-impaired people cannot 
respond to the current alarm 
systems. Without additional 
installations, quick rescue for all 
residents and their visitors cannot 
be guaranteed. 
Equitable use, Flexibility in 
use
A
2 Optic
Lighting
Optical alarm devices installed on the ceiling 
generate a red or white flashing light. 
Installation of visual alarm devices (VAD) on the ceiling. 
Following DS/EN 54-23, the device produces an 
effective light intensity of at least 1 cd in each part of the 
room, but no more than 500 cd at any point in the room. 
The flash rate of the visual signal is between 0.5 and 2.0 
Hz. The flashlight is either white or red.  (Dansk 
Standard, 2010)
BR18 §93(4) A residential unit 
in a building section in use 
category 4 must be established 
with a smoke alarm system 
(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 
2023)
Hearing-impaired people cannot 
respond to the current alarm 
systems. Without additional 
installations, quick rescue for all 
residents and their visitors cannot 
be guaranteed. 
Equitable use, Flexibility in 
use
A
3 Sound
Instructions
Voice sounders give clear instructions on 
evacuation procedures. 
Installation of voice sounders with clear instructions on 
evacuation procedures. Following DS/EN 54-3 the 
sequences are sepaerated into steps: a) attention 
drawing signal (2-10 seconds); b) silcence (0,25-2 
seconds); c) voice message (up to 30 seconds); and d) 
silence (0,25-2 seconds). It must be ensured that the 
time between the start of two cycles is no longer than 30 
seconds. (Dansk Standard, 2019)
BR18 §93(4) A residential unit 
in a building section in use 
category 4 must be established 
with a smoke alarm system 
(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 
2023)
Without instructions, people may 
feel overwhelmed and have 
difficulty making logical decisions. 
Equitable use, Flexibility in 
use
A
4 Sound
Tone
Low-frequency smoke detectors in bedrooms 
and living rooms. 
Installation of low frequency sounders in bed and living 
rooms. A signal with a low frequency of 520 Hz can 
awaken and alert people with hearing loss and alcoholic-
imapired adults (NFPA 72). An example product is the 
Eluxa low frequency sounders  by EATON. (Ziavras, 
2021)
BR18 §93(4) A residential unit 
in a building section in use 
category 4 must be established 
with a smoke alarm system 
(Social- og Boligstyrelsen, 
2023)
As people age, they lose their 
upper frequencies first. Therefore, 
alarm systems with a high 
frequency may not be recognized 
by all residents and their visitors. 
Equitable use, Flexibility in 
use

are at least 75 dB(A). (Traffik- Bygge- og Boligstyrelsen,

Current practice. Without the alarm 
sound, the majority of residents and

a case of emergency fast.

VALIDATION
PRESENTATION
A TAXONOMY & PATTERN 
LANGUAGE FOR FIRE SAFETY  
IN RESIDENTIAL BUILDINGS BASED 
ON UNIVERSAL DESIGN
Scope
§
Universal design in residential building
§
Multifamily houses
§
Focus on fire safety
Problem
§
People with disabilities are not considered in the design process of fire safety in residential regulations
§
Often considered in a later process – The architect designs a first draft and confirms with the fire engineer
§
Only minimum requirements are covered, and minimum requirements are mostly too low
§
If not forced by law, no changes
Purpose of this meeting
§
Evaluation of developed tool, patterns, and solutions adapted to a case study
§
Further improvements, obstacles, limitations
§
Your position
Follow up
==PAGE:238==
Appendix G - Validation Presentation 
6/11/24
2
Results
Results | Taxonomy Tool
==PAGE:239==
Appendix G - Validation Presentation 
6/11/24
3
Results | Solution Spaces
Examples
Results | Pattern Language
In residential buildings, only smoke alarms that emit an 
audible warning signal are mandatory and therefore 
integrated.
Residents are not prepared for the case of an emergency. If 
residents are moreover affected by disabilities those 
situations can result in sensory overwhelming, hiding, or 
panicking. 
The defined mandatory hallway and door width are 
insufficient to support the simultaneous passage of a person 
walking and a person in a wheelchair. Residents waiting for 
an elevator to carry them down must wait in the hallway, 
which creates an obstacle for other individuals.
Visual support in residential buildings is barely required in 
current regulations. No matter if egress route and anti-panic 
lighting, or contrast elements varying in luminance or color. 
The only touch-related element in a residential building is 
the handrail following along the stairs. 
Mobility-impaired people cannot rescue themselves from 
their apartments in a fire emergency. They are dependent on 
further residents or the firefighters to carry them down.

Appendix G - Validation Presentation

5
Case Study | 1 Integrated Alarm System
Smoke detectors with different alarm systems 
should be integrated as standard in residential 
buildings. This will ensure the recognition of 
fire alarms no matter if people are affected by 
a disability or develop an impairment over 
time. Smoke detectors and connected devices 
should provide vibration, flashlight, sound, and 
voice introductions.
Case Study | 2 Learned Behavior
Minimum once at key handover, evacuation 
practices should be introduced to the tenant. 
Conducted by the landlord, the tenant will gain 
knowledge about the alarm systems and 
egress route design. Further, it should be 
possible to refresh the knowledge annually.
§
No introduction to egress route or fire 
alerting systems at key handover
§
No further information in contract
§
No consideration of people with disabilities
§
Introduction to egress routes and alerting 
system at key handover
§
Signage of the tenant that it is done
§
Offering of vibration alarm devices in case 
of hearing-impairment
§
Introducing the option of later 
access to vibration alarm devices in 
case of development of hearing-
impairment
§
Yearly refresher course (e-learning) to 
introduce new systems or remind the old 
ones
§
E-learning test as confirmation
==PAGE:242==
Appendix G - Validation Presentation 
6/11/24
6
Case Study | 3 Enough Space
Create safe waiting spaces on each floor. 
Spaces that are sufficiently spacious to 
accommodate 
individuals 
with 
mobility 
impairments until the use of the elevator is 
possible or a rescue team can carry them 
down. Corridors and doors should be designed 
to permit wheelchair users to pass without risk 
of injury or collision.
§
Consider more space in hallways already 
in the early design process
§
Implement waiting areas that are a 
minimum of 1,40x1,42 meters to offer 
space for one wheelchair user and a 
person with crotchets 
§
Design the hallways with a width of 
minimum 
1,50 
meters 
to 
allow 
simultaneous passing of walking people 
and wheelchair user
§
The door width of 1,01 meters at the 
entrance 
and 
0,91 
meters 
in 
the 
apartment is wide enough for a wheelchair 
user to pass
§
A door with a minimum width of 
0,87 
meters 
should 
be 
implemented 
1,31 m
Case Study | 4 Visible Route
Installing several lighting or contrast elements 
must be crucial in designing spaces within the 
residential building. Visibility can be supported 
through specific light installations, such as 
photoluminescent guiding stripes on the floor, 
ceiling-mounted guiding lamps, egress route, 
and anti-panic lighting. Size and contrast 
variations must be considered in facilitating 
the recognition of installations and information. 
4
==PAGE:243==
Appendix G - Validation Presentation 
6/11/24
7
Case Study | 5 Tangible Route
In the entrance area, tactile patterns such as 
guidance and attention patterns should be 
included and placed at the necessary route to 
the stairwells and elevators. Further, two 
handrails at two different heights on both sides 
of the stairs should be installed to facilitate the 
moving of all residents no matter how tall they 
are. The handrails should be located at a 
height of 0,61 meters for children and small 
people and 0,915 meters for adults and tall 
people. It must be ensured, that the handrails 
are grip-friendly and easy to hold on to. They 
are 
installed 
without 
interruptions 
over 
landings and terminate horizontally. The 
handrails should be equipped with metal studs 
to indicate the floor number and adjacent 
corridor junctions.
© isUD
Case Study | 6 Egress without Stairs
Introduce elevators as evacuation devices, 
enabling people with mobility or mental 
impairment, causing mobility impairments to 
rescue themselves in a case of fire. The 
elevators should be established according to 
the given standards and strictly reserved for 
residents and visitors not able to walk 
downstairs. All elevators should be in fire-
protected areas and reachable through access 
balconies. 
§
Egress without stairs is possible through 
balconies and windows
§
Not all areas can be served with an aerial 
ladder 
§
Bigger people cannot be carried down 
through a hand ladder 
§
Implement fire protected areas accessible 
through an extra entrance with elevator 
carrying down mobility impaired people
§
Each apartment must be connected to 
corridors leading to the protected elevator

Interview 2 – Carlberg – 16.05.2024 
Interview participants: 
Emma Nielsen – Project leader, Carlberg  
Inke Lindberg – Student, DTU  
 
Inke Lindberg   0:03 
Can you see my screen? 
 
Emma Nielsen   0:14 
Yes, I can. 
 
Inke Lindberg   0:16 
As you know, I want to show you what I've found out so far and a little follow-up. 
My scope was universal design in residential buildings. I focus on multifamily houses 
and fire safety and some of the main problems were that people with disabilities are 
not really considered in the design process of fire safety. It's often considered too 
late in the process, so the architect makes the first draft and then just confirms it and 
only minimum requirements are covered and they are mostly too low. 
And if it's not forced by law, they won't implement a lot of changes and the purpose 
of this meeting is now to evaluate my created tool patterns and solutions. 
And hear you're further improvements. Maybe limitations and what you in general 
think of it. 
So I have kind of three results. I created a taxonomy, solution spaces, and a pattern 
language. I just introduce you to all of those now to understand it and if there's 
something unclear, just interrupt me. 
 
Emma Nielsen   1:32 
Yeah, sounds good. 
 
Inke Lindberg   1:34 
And so to the taxonomy. 
I organized it similar to the EU taxonomy compass. I don't know if you know it, and 
so it's a web tool where you can click on it and then you get these drop-down menus 
which explains like the overall requirements without getting very much into detail. 
And this is the idea behind this, so I divided it into the egress route design, alerting 
system, and people's behavior, and then behind all these attributes there are 
different levels of detail. Which includes tactility, contrast, or area for example. 
And out of those I made some solution spaces. I send out a survey to this. That's 
why the flames are still grey on the side. I am waiting for feedback from the industry 
==PAGE:248==
Appendix G 
Validation 
Transcript Interview Series 2 
 
if they think they would consider implementing it. 
For example, some take tactile guidance patterns or the entrance areas, if they're a 
bit wider, maybe there are photoluminescent stripes on the floors in the hallways to 
improve the guidance in case of light failure. 
And then out of those and the taxonomy, I created a pattern language. 
It is the six things on the right part here, and I defined some problems. 
They're maybe not formulated perfectly yet, but for example that it's just one smoke 
detector in the whole apartment that's required, and that is a sound detector or that 
the visual support in residential buildings is barely required. 
And that, for example, the only touch-related elements are handrails on one side of 
the stairs. And for these six patterns, I created 6 sketches which are supposed to 
make it a bit more visual for people if they would see it. 
And then it's like, there is something I can maybe already see, what could be 
implemented. And giving the first indications of some sizes or heights. These 
patterns I applied it now to a case I got from one project developer and this is what I 
want to present to you now and hear your feedback about those in the end. 
 
Emma Nielsen   4:24 
Cool. 
I'm happy to go through this. 
I can feel that I'm also a bit unsure about whether I have the right knowledge to be 
fully able to give you feedback on this, but I'll do my best and comment and give you 
feedback I guess as best as I can. Because I really think it's nice that it's so specific 
and so visually as well that you can go in and you can use it as a tool.
